Are Certain Principles Self-Evident? Ghazali's Challenge
Ghazali refutes the claim that impossibility of creation ex nihilo and annihilation are self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali’s critique of treating the impossibility of creation ex nihilo and annihilation as self-evident, and see how empirical discoveries can overturn supposedly self-evident truths.

Core Idea
Self-evidence can be an illusion — even basic beliefs can be overturned by new evidence.

Ghazali begins by identifying a common assumption among philosophers: that the impossibility of creating something from nothing and of turning something into nothing are self-evident rational truths. He immediately questions this, arguing that such claims shut down inquiry. He then provides a striking historical example: people once believed fire annihilates what it burns, but scientific experiments proved otherwise. This example becomes a weapon to undermine the claim of self-evidence for theological principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the strong rhetorical style: Ghazali directly addresses the reader and uses a series of sharp questions.
Pay attention to the shift from statement to questioning.
2
Identify the two claims under attack
The philosophers claim that two things are self-evident: that something cannot come from nothing, and that something cannot become nothing. Ghazali calls these 'belittling' and 'diversion' from examination.
These are the principles of creation ex nihilo and annihilation.
3
Analyze the burning candle example
Ghazali uses the historical belief that fire annihilates things. The invention of a device showed that a burning candle's weight remains the same, challenging the idea that fire annihilates. This undermines the claim that annihilation is impossible.
The example shows that even empirical observations can overturn what seems self-evident.
4
Consider the implications for divine power
Ghazali asks whether we have the right to subject God's power to experiment. If fire doesn't annihilate, perhaps other means do. Our lack of knowledge doesn't prove impossibility.
This leads to the conclusion that we cannot claim self-evidence for limits on divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعتقد كثير من الباحثين أن : عدم إمكان خلق شيء من لا شيء وعدم إمكان تصيير شيء إلى لا شيء . أمور أولية ، تفصح عن أوليتيهما ضرورة العقل و بداهته وأصارحك أيها القارئ أن دعوى البداهة في هاتين المسألتين ، استهانة بشأنهما ، وصرف للعقل عن فحصهما وتمعنهما ، إنها دعوة إلى الاستنامة والاطمئنان ، وما أظن أن العقل اليقظ يشعر بالرضى والاطمئنان حيال دعوى خطيرة كدعوى بداهة هاتين المسألتين فأين كانت البداهة يوم كان يظن أن ما تحرقه النار ، تهلكه وتفنيه ؟ هل تولدت في النفوس وتقررت في الأذهان منذ اختراع الجهاز الذي أثبت أن المتحلل من الشمعة المحترقة ، يساوي وزنها قبل الاحتراق ؟ والذى أثبت ، تبعاً لذلك ، أن الاحتراق ليس إفناء ، وأن المحترق لا يفنى ! ! هل تولدت البداهة فى النفوس، وتقررت فى الأذهان ، منذ ذلك الوقت فقط ؟ وعن هذا الطريق فقط ؟ وهل يمكن أن تعتمد البداهة على تجربة ، أو على دليل ؟ ثم إذا كانت النار لا تفنى ما تحرقه ، فهل انحصرت وسائل الإفناء في الإحراق ؟ وهل إذا كان العلم البشرى ، لم يقف إلى الآن ، على وسائل أعظم من النار خطراً ، وأشد منها بأساً ، يكون معنى ذلك أن مثل هذه الوسائل لم توجد ، ولا يمكن أن توجد ؟ وهل عدم الوجدان يدل على عدم الوجود ؟ وهل أخضع قدرة الإله - من يعتقد أن هناك إلهاً ـ للتجربة ، فقاس حدودها ، وعرف ما يدخل في هذه الحدود ، وما يخرج عنها ، وحكم بناء على ذلك بأن تصيير الشيء عدماً صرفاً، خارج عن حدود المقدور ؟
And many researchers believe that: the impossibility of creating something from nothing and the impossibility of turning something into nothing are primary matters, revealing their primariness by the necessity of reason and its self-evidence. And I declare to you, reader, that claiming self-evidence regarding these two issues is a belittling of their status, a diversion of the mind from examining and pondering them; it is an invitation to indolence and complacency. And I do not think that an alert mind feels satisfaction and complacency faced with a serious claim like the claim of self-evidence of these two issues. So where was self-evidence on the day when it was thought that what fire burns, it destroys and annihilates? Was it generated in souls and established in minds since the invention of the apparatus that proved that the decomposed matter of the burning candle equals its weight before burning? And which proved, accordingly, that burning is not annihilation, and that the burnt thing does not perish! Was self-evidence generated in souls and established in minds only from that time? And only through this way? And can self-evidence depend on experience or a proof? Then if fire does not annihilate what it burns, are the means of annihilation limited to burning? And if human science has not yet come upon means more dangerous and more severe than fire, does that mean that such means do not exist and cannot exist? Does the lack of finding indicate the lack of existence? And has anyone who believes there is a God subjected the power of God to experiment, measuring its limits, knowing what enters into these limits and what exits them, and then ruled accordingly that turning a thing into pure nothing is beyond the limits of the possible?
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that two principles—the impossibility of creating something from nothing and the impossibility of turning something into nothing—are self-evident (badīhī). The author first states the common belief that these are primary matters disclosed by the necessity of reason and its self-evidence. Then he directly challenges this claim, calling it a belittlement and a diversion from examination, inviting complacency. He argues that an alert mind would not accept it without scrutiny. He uses the historical example of fire and burning: people once believed fire annihilates what it burns, but scientific experiments (like the burning candle) showed that the weight remains, so burning is not annihilation. This discovery changed the understanding, proving that what was once considered self-evident (annihilation by fire) was false. He then raises a series of rhetorical questions: Was the self-evidence of that belief generated only after the experiment? Can self-evidence depend on experiment or proof? If fire does not annihilate, are we sure there are no other means of annihilation? Does lack of finding evidence of such means imply their non-existence? Finally, he asks whether those who believe in God have subjected God's power to experiment to determine what is possible or impossible. This is a powerful critique of considering physical principles as self-evident and independent of empirical inquiry, especially when applied to divine power.

Key Terms
بداهة
خلق من لا شيء
إفناء
جهاز
تجربة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the impossibility of creating from nothing is a self-evident truth that requires no proof.
Correction: Ghazali shows that what seems self-evident can be overturned by new evidence, as with the example of fire and burning. Therefore, such principles should not be accepted without examination.

Checkpoint
What historical example does Ghazali use to challenge the claim that certain principles are self-evident?
A) The creation of the universe
B) The belief that fire annihilates what it burns
C) The limits of human reason
D) The power of God

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that we cannot rely on claims of self-evidence to limit divine power, especially when empirical discoveries have overturned similar 'self-evident' beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a theological claim—like 'God can create from nothing'—as obviously impossible without stopping to ask whether your 'obvious' is just a habit of thought? When was the last time you let a new piece of knowledge shatter what you thought was self-evident?
?
This passage reveals that our certainty can be built on ignorance. What belief about God's power do you hold most tightly, and would you be willing to examine it as Ghazali examined the fire example? What would it mean for your life if that belief turned out to be false?
?
Ghazali says claiming self-evidence is a 'diversion of the mind.' Where in your own life have you used 'it's just obvious' as an excuse to stop thinking? Are you willing to revisit that area today?

Ghazali's Two Objections to Galen's Conditional Syllogism
Ghazali presents two objections: the conditional syllogism needs an extra condition, and observation is only approximate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two logical criticisms of Galen's argument that the sun must wither if it corrupts, and why the conditional syllogism fails without an additional premise.

Core Idea
A conditional argument is only as strong as its hidden assumptions.

Ghazali engages with Galen's philosophical reasoning about the sun's corruption. He identifies two flaws: first, the logical structure of Galen's conditional syllogism requires an extra condition for the conclusion to follow; second, empirical observations cannot provide certainty in this context. This passage illustrates Ghazali's skill in combining logical analysis with epistemological critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the structure: two objections followed by a concluding critique.
2
Identify the two objections
Find Ghazali's first and second objection. Notice how each is introduced and the reasoning used.
The first is about the need for an extra condition; the second about the limitation of observations.
3
Analyze the conditional syllogism
Ghazali dissects the logical form: if antecedent then consequent. Understand why he says the consequent does not follow without an added premise.
Ask yourself: what hidden assumption does Galen rely on?
4
Consider the epistemological objection
Reflect on why observations are insufficient according to Ghazali. What does 'approximate' mean for certainty?
This objection targets empirical evidence, a key theme in Ghazali's critique of the philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الغزالي: [الاعتراض عليه من وجوه: الأول: أن شكل هذا الدليل أن يقال: إن كانت الشمس تفسد، فلابد أن يلحقها ذبول. لكن التالي محال. فالمقدم محال. وهذا قياس يسمى عندهم «الشرطي المتصل». وهذه النتيجة غير لازمة؛ لأن المقدم غير صحيح ما لم يضف إليه شرط آخر، وهو قوله: إن كانت تفسد، فلابد أن تذبل (۱) فهذا التالي لا يلزم هذا المقدم؛ إلا بزيادة شرط، وهو أن يقول: إن كانت تفسد فساداً ذبولياً، فلابد أن تذبل في طول المدة. أو يبين أنه لا فساد إلا بطريق الذبول، حتى يلزم التالي للمقدم. ولا نسلم أنه لا يفسد الشيء، إلا بالذبول؛ بل الذبول أحد وجوه الفساد. ولا يبعد أن يفسد الشيء بغتة، وهو على حال كماله. الثاني: أنه لم سلم له هذا، وأنه لا فساد إلا بالذبول، فمن أين عرف أنه لا يعتريها الذبول؟ أما التفاته إلى الأرصاد فمحال؛ لأنها لا تعرف مقاديرها إلا بالتقريب. نقد: أما قوله: [فلا بد أن تذبل] فهي زيادة لا لزوم لها، لأنها التالي الذي لم يكن موضع. ويتجه نقد الغزالي في هذا الاعتراض: إما إلى المقدم الذي يلزمه شرط لا بد منه، وإما إلى الملازمة التي بين التالي والمقدم، إذ هي مفقودة إن لم يوافق على إضافة الشرط.
Al-Ghazali said: [The objection against it is from several aspects: First: The form of this argument is that one says: If the sun were to corrupt, then it would necessarily be overtaken by withering. But the consequent is impossible. Therefore the antecedent is impossible. This is a syllogism called by them a 'conditional conjunctive' (sharṭī muttaṣil). And this conclusion is not necessary, because the antecedent is not correct unless another condition is added to it, namely his statement: If it corrupts, then it must wither (1). For this consequent does not follow from this antecedent except by the addition of a condition, namely that he says: If it corrupts with a withering corruption, then it must wither over a long period. Or he must show that there is no corruption except by way of withering, so that the consequent follows from the antecedent. And we do not concede that a thing corrupts only by withering; rather, withering is one of the types of corruption. And it is not unlikely that a thing corrupts suddenly, while it is in a state of perfection. Second: And even if it is granted to him that there is no corruption except by withering, how does he know that it is not affected by withering? As for his reliance on observations, it is impossible because they know their measurements only approximately. Critique: As for his statement: 'it must wither', this is an addition that is not necessary, because it is the consequent that was not the subject. And al-Ghazali's critique in this objection is directed either to the antecedent which requires a necessary condition, or to the correlation between the consequent and the antecedent, since it is absent if he does not agree to the addition of the condition.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's two objections to Galen's argument. First, he analyzes the logical form: a conditional conjunctive syllogism. He argues that the premise that corruption necessarily entails withering is not valid without an additional condition. The sun might perish suddenly without prior withering. Second, even if one accepts that corruption must involve withering, Galen cannot know that the sun has never withered because observational instruments only give approximate measurements. Finally, a concluding critique reiterates that the conditional relationship is flawed without the added condition.

Key Terms
الشرطي المتصل
المقدم
التالي
الملازمة
الذبول
التقريب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Galen's conditional syllogism proves that the sun cannot corrupt.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the conditional premise is invalid without an additional claim that all corruption involves withering.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical flaw Ghazali identifies in Galen's argument?
A) The syllogism is not a conditional conjunctive but a disjunctive
B) The antecedent 'if the sun corrupts' does not necessarily imply the consequent 'it must wither' without an additional condition
C) Galen failed to observe the sun's actual withering
D) The conclusion is valid but the premises are false

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's critique shows that logical arguments must be scrutinized for hidden premises and that empirical observations cannot provide absolute certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own beliefs about Allah or the unseen, do you assume your reasoning is flawless? Are you willing to expose your most cherished arguments to the same scrutiny Ghazali applied to Galen?
?
Ghazali shows that empirical observations are only approximate. In your life, do you treat your personal experiences as absolute truth? What would it cost you to humbly admit your knowledge is incomplete and subject to error?
?
Galen's argument seemed conclusive to him, yet Ghazali revealed hidden gaps. Is there any belief you hold so confidently that you have never questioned? Can you honestly re-examine it, looking for missing conditions, as Ghazali did?

Ghazali's Demand for Demonstration
Ghazali demands a demonstration for the claimed impossibility of a complete cause delaying its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the philosophers' own logical standards to challenge their claim about causality, demanding either a necessary rational intuition or a valid demonstrative proof.

Core Idea
Claims of impossibility require proof, not mere assertion.

Ghazali faces a classic objection: if a cause is complete, its effect must follow immediately. Instead of accepting this as self-evident, Ghazali turns the tables. He asks the philosophers: how do you know this impossibility? Is it by rational necessity or by demonstration? This simple question shifts the burden of proof and reveals the weakness in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: first the objection, then Ghazali's response.
The objection is stated concisely; Ghazali's response is layered.
2
Identify the two epistemological options
Ghazali presents two ways to know the impossibility: by necessity of reason (ḍarūrat al-ʿaql) or by demonstration (burhān) with a middle term. Pause and think: which one does he expect the philosophers to claim?
3
Analyze the consequence of claiming necessity
If the philosophers claim necessity, Ghazali points out that many rational people (those who accept an eternal will creating in time) disagree. This shows it is not truly necessary. If they claim demonstration, they must produce it.
This is a classic argument from disagreement.
4
Reflect on Ghazali's strategy
Consider how Ghazali is not rejecting rational argument but using the philosophers' own logical tools (necessity vs. demonstration) to demand rigor. This is key to his approach in the Tahafut.
He forces them to meet their own standards.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل هذا محال بين الإحالة؛ لأن الحادث موجب ومسبب، وكما يستحيل حادث بغير سبب وموجب، يستحيل وجود موجب قد تم بشرائط إيجابه وأركانه وأسبابه، حتى لم يبق شيء منتظر ألبتة، ثم يتأخر الموجب؛ بل وجود الموجب عند تحقق الموجيب بتمام شروطه، ضرورى، وتأخره محال وجود الحادث الموجب بلا موجب من استحالة حسب. والجواب أن يقال : استحالة إرادة قديمة متعلقة بأحداث شيء - أي شيء كان ـ تعرفونه بضرورة العقل أو نظره ؟ وعلى لغتكم في المنطق ، تعرفون الالتقاء بين هذين الحدين ، بحد أوسط، أو من غير حد أوسط ؟ فإن ادعيتم حدا أوسط - - وهو الطريق النظرى - فلا بد من إظهاره ، و إن ادعيتم معرفة ذلك ضرورة ، فكيف لم يشارككم فى معرفته مخالفوكم ، والفرقة المتعقدة لحدوث العالم بإرادة قديمة لا يحصرها بلد ولا يحصيها عدد ، ولا شك في أنهم لا يكابرون العقول عناداً مع المعرفة ، فلابد من إقامة برهان - على شرط المنطق – يدل على استحالة ذلك
For if it is said: This is an obvious impossibility, because a temporal event (ḥādith) has a cause (mūjib) and a means (musabbab), and just as it is impossible for a temporal event without a cause, so it is impossible for a cause whose conditions of causation, pillars, and causes have been completed such that nothing at all remains awaited, and then the cause is delayed. Rather, the existence of the cause when the caused has realized with all its conditions is necessary, and its delay is impossible. The existence of a temporal event without a cause is impossible according to...? The answer is to say: The impossibility of an eternal will being related to producing something — any thing — do you know it by necessity of reason or by theoretical proof? And in your language of logic, do you know the conjunction between these two terms by a middle term or without a middle term? If you claim a middle term — which is the theoretical path — then you must produce it. And if you claim knowledge of that by necessity, then why do your opponents not share with you in this knowledge, and the group that believes in the creation of the world by an eternal will is not confined to a country nor counted by number, and there is no doubt that they do not argue against the intellects obstinately while knowing. So it is necessary to produce a demonstration — according to the conditions of logic — that indicates the impossibility of that.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents a typical kalām objection and response. The objector argues that it is impossible for a complete cause to delay its effect, similar to how an event cannot occur without a cause. Ghazālī's response is a key epistemological move. He asks: is this impossibility known by reason's necessity or by theoretical demonstration? In Aristotelian logic, knowledge is either immediate (necessary) or mediated by a middle term (demonstration). Ghazālī demands that the philosophers present either a middle term (i.e., a demonstrative proof) or admit that it is not known by necessity, since many rational people disagree. He points out that countless people hold the view of creation by an eternal will, and they are not denying reason. Therefore, the burden is on the philosophers to prove the impossibility. This demonstrates Ghazālī's methodological approach: he does not reject rational argument but insists on rigorous logical proof.

Key Terms
ضرورة العقل (ḍarūrat al-ʿaql – necessity of reason)
حد أوسط (ḥadd awsaṭ – middle term)
برهان (burhān – demonstration)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is rejecting logic and rational proof.
Correction: Ghazali is not rejecting logic; he is demanding that the philosophers meet their own logical standards. He points out that their claim lacks the required demonstration and is not universally self-evident.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazālī, how should the philosophers prove that an eternal will cannot produce a temporal effect at a specific time?
A) By quoting scripture.
B) By logical demonstration with a middle term.
C) By appealing to consensus.
D) By claiming necessity of reason.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that a claim of impossibility must be supported either by universal rational necessity or by a valid logical demonstration, and the philosophers have provided neither.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assert something about God, do you have a rational basis or a logical proof, or do you simply assume it because you've heard it before? What claim in your own faith are you holding without evidence?
?
Ghazali demands that opponents produce a demonstration. Are you willing to submit your own beliefs to the same standard of proof? Which of your convictions would fail that test?
?
The philosophers believed their claim was self-evident, but many rational people disagreed. Consider a belief you hold that others reject—are you truly certain it is self-evident, or are you unwilling to examine it?

Hamilton's Critique of Certainty
Hamilton argues that certainty about correspondence is impossible due to our inability to test our faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Hamilton's argument that certainty about the correspondence of ideas to objects is impossible, and why he does not advocate skepticism.

Core Idea
We cannot step outside our own minds to verify if our ideas match reality.

This passage presents Sir William Hamilton's powerful argument that the quest for absolute certainty about truth as correspondence is self-defeating. Building on Ghazali's insights, Hamilton shows that any attempt to verify our cognitive faculties leads to an infinite regress. Yet, as you will see, this argument does not lead to skepticism but to a humbler acceptance of common sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Hamilton defines 'real truth' as correspondence before raising his doubt.
2
Identify the problem of certainty
Hamilton asks how we can know that our ideas correspond to objects. He points out that all knowledge comes through our faculties, which we cannot step outside of to test.
This is the core of the epistemic problem.
3
Follow the infinite regress argument
Hamilton argues that even if we could get new faculties to test the old ones, the new ones would themselves be subject to the same doubt, leading to an infinite regress.
No external standpoint is possible.
4
Understand Hamilton's conclusion
Hamilton concludes that the only response to such radical doubt is to say that if it were true, human nature would be fundamentally deceptive—and we should not assume that without strong evidence.
This shows he is not a skeptic; he accepts common sense as a practical foundation.

أما « السير وليام هاملتون ( فقد كان واضحاً جد الوضوح في أنه أوقف العقل نفس الموقف الحرج الذى أوقفه إياه الغزالى ، وأنه أذهب عنه الحرج بنفس الوسيلة التي لاحت للغزالي ، يقول : [ الصدق الحقيقى هو مطابقة أفكارنا للوجودات التي تكون موضوعاتها لكن هنا صعوبة تنشأ . كيف نعرف أنه يوجد - أنه يمكن أن يكون - مثل هذا الانطباق ؟ إن كل ما نستطيع أن نعرفه عن الموضوعات ، يأتينا عن طريق قوانا ومداركنا ، لكنا لا نستطيع أن نتأكد من أن قوانا ومداركنا يمكنها أن تتصور هذه الموضوعات كما هي في نفسها ؛ لأن تأكدنا من كفاية قوانا ومداركنا لتصور الموضوعات ، يتطلب أن نتخلى عن كياننا ـ أن نتخلى عن قوانا و مداركنا - وأن نحصل على معرفة بهذه الموضوعات عن طريق قوى ومدارك أخرى ، وأن نقارن المعرفة القديمة التى حصلنا عليها عن طريق القوى والمدارك الأولى ، بالمعرفة الجديدة التى حصلنا عليها عن طريق القوى والمدارك الثانية ، لكن - حتى لو صح أن ذلك الفرض ممكن – فإنه سيكون عاجزاً عن أن يقدم لنا اليقين المطلوب . إذ على فرض أنه من الممكن أن نخلع قوانا ومداركنا القديمة ، وأن نحصل على أخرى جديدة نختبر بها القديمة ، فإن صدق القوى والمدارك الجديدة ، لا يزال عرضة لنفس الشك الذى تعرضت له القوى والمدارك القديمة ؟ إذ ما هو الضمان الذى يجعل الحالة الجديدة أوثق من الحال القديمة ؟ إن القوى والمدارك الجديدة يمكن أن تؤكد صدق نفسها فقط، لكن القوى والمدارك القديمة قد أكدت أيضاً صدق نفسها . ومن المستحيل أن نتخيل إدراكاً من الإدراكات التي تحدث لكائن من الكائنات ذات الذكاء المحدود، يسمو عن أن يشك في أنه ذاتي - غير موضوعي ابتدعه الكائن المدرك نفسه . كل ما يمكن - إذن - أن يقال في الرد على مثل هذا الشك ، هو أنه لو صح مثل هذا الشك لكانت الطبيعة الإنسانية طبيعة مزيفة ، وهو أمر لا ينبغي اللجوء إليه دون مبررات على أعظم جانب من القوة » (۱)
As for Sir William Hamilton, he was very clearly placing reason in the same critical position in which Ghazali had placed it, and he removed the difficulty by the same means that occurred to Ghazali. He says: 'Real truth is the correspondence of our ideas to the existents that are their objects. But here a difficulty arises: How do we know that such a correspondence exists – that it can exist? Everything we can know about objects comes to us through our faculties and perceptions, but we cannot be certain that our faculties and perceptions can conceive these objects as they are in themselves; because our certainty of the adequacy of our faculties and perceptions to conceive objects would require that we abandon our entity – that we abandon our faculties and perceptions – and obtain knowledge of these objects through other faculties and perceptions, and compare the old knowledge we obtained through the first faculties and perceptions with the new knowledge we obtained through the second faculties and perceptions. But – even if that assumption were possible – it would be incapable of providing us with the required certainty. For assuming it were possible to shed our old faculties and perceptions and obtain new ones to test the old, the truthfulness of the new faculties and perceptions would still be subject to the same doubt that the old faculties and perceptions faced. For what guarantee makes the new state more reliable than the old? The new faculties and perceptions can only confirm their own truthfulness, but the old faculties and perceptions also confirmed their own truthfulness. And it is impossible to imagine a perception occurring to any being of limited intelligence that is above suspicion of being subjective – not objective – invented by the perceiving being itself. All that can be said, then, in response to such doubt is that if such doubt were true, human nature would be a deceptive nature, and that is something that should not be resorted to without extremely strong justifications.' (1)
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents Sir William Hamilton's argument that certainty about truth as correspondence is impossible. He defines truth as correspondence of ideas to objects, then asks how we can know such correspondence exists. Since all our knowledge comes through our faculties, we cannot check their adequacy without stepping outside them – which is impossible. Any attempt to test our faculties using other faculties leads to an infinite regress of doubt, since the new faculties also need validation. The only response to such skepticism is that if it were true, human nature would be fundamentally deceptive, but we should not assume that without strong evidence.

Key Terms
الصدق الحقيقى
المطابقة
القوى والمدارك
اليقين
الشك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hamilton's argument leads to complete skepticism.
Correction: Hamilton does not advocate skepticism; he argues that absolute certainty is impossible, but we can still rely on common sense and practical certainty.

Checkpoint
According to Hamilton, why can we never be certain that our ideas correspond to objects?
A) Because objects don't exist.
B) Because we cannot step outside our faculties to test them.
C) Because our faculties are always deceptive.
D) Because the correspondence theory is false.

Key Takeaway
Hamilton's argument shows that absolute certainty about truth as correspondence is impossible, but this does not force us into skepticism; rather, it highlights the limits of reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your senses and reason every moment. How do you know they are not systematically deceiving you? What evidence do you have that your mind can grasp reality as it is?
?
If you cannot prove your faculties are reliable, what does that mean for your most cherished beliefs? Will you continue to hold them without absolute certainty, or will you seek a different foundation?
?
Hamilton says the only answer to radical doubt is that human nature would be deceptive, which is unacceptable. Can you live with that? Or do you need a stronger guarantee—perhaps from revelation—to ground your certainty?

Why Spatial and Temporal Terms Are Not Alike
Ghazali distinguishes spatial from temporal terms and argues for the essential nature of temporal endpoints.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that spatial terms like 'above' and 'below' are relative, while temporal terms like 'before' and 'after' are essential to the world's existence.

Core Idea
Spatial terms depend on perspective; temporal endpoints belong to reality itself.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali tackles a critical distinction that often confuses theologians and philosophers. He demonstrates that while terms like 'above' and 'below' shift with an observer's position, the beginning and end of the world's existence are fixed and unchangeable. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of time and the world's temporality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali contrasts the relativity of spatial terms with the fixity of temporal terms. Pay attention to the analogy of the piece of wood.
The passage includes an analogy to illustrate relativity.
2
Identify the key distinction
Ghazali shows that 'above' and 'below' change with orientation, but the 'before' (prior non-existence) and 'after' (subsequent non-existence) are essential to the world. Find these examples in the text.
3
Understand the objection and response
Ghazali anticipates an objection using 'inside' and 'outside' and shows the parallel applies. Then he addresses the role of estimation (wahm). Note how he argues that the failure of estimation does not disprove rational proof.
The objection from spatial terms is turned back on the philosophers.
4
Reflect on the role of estimation
Consider why estimation cannot picture a finite body without surroundings or a beginning without a prior thing. Ghazali concludes that this is the cause of error, and reason must prevail.
Estimation is habituated to spatial and temporal extension.

تلى الدقيق «فوقاً » إلى حيث ينتهى ، والجانب الآخر تحتاً ، لم يظهر بهذا اختلاف ذاتى فى أجزاء العالم ، بل هي أسامى مختلفة ، قيامها بهيئة هذه الخشبة حتى لو عكس وضعها لانعكس الاسم، والعالم لم يتبدل ، فالفوق والتحت نسبة محضة إليك لا تختلف أجزاء العالم وسطوحه فيه ؛ وأما العدم المتقدم على العالم ، والنهاية الأولى لموجوده ، فذاتى له لا يتصور أن يتبدل فيصير آخراً ، ولا العدم المقدر عند فناء العالم ، أمكننا أن نقول : ليس للعالم « فوق » ولا « تحت » ، ولا يمكنكم أن تقولوا : ليس لوجود العالم ) قبل » ولا « بعد » ، وإذا ثبت « القبل » و « البعد » فلا معنى للزمان سوى ما يعبر عنه » بالقبل » و « البعد » . قلنا : لا فرق ، فإنه لا غرض فى تعيين لفظ ( الفوق » و « النحت » ، بل نعدل إلى لفظ « الداخل » و « الخارج » ، ونقول : للعالم داخل وخارج، فهل خارج العالم شيء من ملاء أو خلاء ؟ فسيقولون : ليس وراء العالم لا خلاء ولا ملاء . وإن عنيتم بالخارج سطحه الأعلى فله خارج ، وإن عنيتم غيره فلا خارج له ، فكذلك إذا قيل لنا : هل لوجود العالم » قبل » ؟ ، قلنا إن عنى به هل لوجود العالم بداية، أى طرف منه ابتدأ فله ) قبل » على هذا، كما للعالم خارج على تأويل أنه الطرف المكشوف والمنقطع السطحى ، وإن عنيتم « بقبل » شيئاً آخر فلا قبل للعالم ، كما أنه إذا عنى بخارج العالم شيء سوى السطح ، قيل لا خارج للعالم ، فإن قلتم لا يعقل مبدأ وجود لا « قبل » له ، فيقال ولا يعقل متناهى وجود من الجسم لا خارج له ، فإن قلت « خارجه » سطحه الذي هو منقطعه لا غير ، قلنا قبله بداية وجوده الذي هو طرفه لا غير بقى أن نقول : لله وجود ولا عالم معه ، وهذا القول أيضاً لا يوجب إثبات شيء آخر ، والذى يدل على أن هذا عمل الوهم ، أنه مخصوص بالزمان والمكان فإن الخصم وإن اعتقد قدم الجسم يذعن وهمه لتقدير حدوثه ، ونحن و إن اعتقدنا حدوثه ، ربما أذعن وهمنا لتقدير قدمه ، هذا في الجسم ، فإذا رجعنا إلى الزمان لم يقدر الخصم على تقدير حدوث زمان لا « قبل » له ـ وخلاف (۱) المعتقد يمكن وضعه في الوهم تقديراً وفرضاً – وهذا (۲) مما لا يمكن وضعه في الوهم ، كما في – المكان (۳) فإن من يعتقد تناهى الجسم ومن لا يعتقد ، كل واحد يعجز عن تقدير جسم ليس وراءه لا خلاء ولا ملاء ، بل لا يذعن وهمه لقبول ذلك ، ولكن قيل : صريح العقل إذا لم يمنع وجود جسم متناه بحكم الدليل ، لم يلتفت إلى الوهم قصر الوهم صريح العقل لا يمنع وجوداً مفتتحاً ليس قبله شيء ؛ وإن فكذلك عنه فلا يلتفت إليه ، لأن الوهم لما لم يألف جسماً متناهياً إلا وبجنبه جسم آخر ، أو هواء تخيله خلاء ، لم يتمكن من ذلك في الغائب ؛ فكذلك لم يألف الوهم حادثاً إلا بعد شيء آخر ، فكاع عن تقدير حادث ليس له «قبل» هو شيء موجود قد انقضى ، فهذا هو سبب الغلط ، والمقاومة حاصلة بهذه المعارضة . (1) أى الشأن في كل معتقد أن خلافه يمكن وضعه في الوهم تقديراً وفرضاً (۲) إشارة إلى خلاف معتقد الفيلسوف في الزمان . (۳) كما أن معتقد الفيلسوف في المكان لا يمكن وضعه فى الوهم ولم يمنع ذلك من اعتقاده جرياً وراء العقل ، فكذلك خلاف معتقده في الزمان وإن لم يمكن وضعه في الوهم لكن العقل ساق إليه فيجب الأخذ به.
He said: 'above' precisely up to where it ends, and the other side 'below' – this does not show an essential difference in the parts of the world; rather they are different names, their applicability depends on the orientation of this piece of wood, so that if its position were reversed, the name would be reversed, while the world does not change. So 'above' and 'below' are pure relations to you; the parts and surfaces of the world do not differ with respect to them. As for the non-existence prior to the world, and the first endpoint of its existence, these are essential to it, not conceivable that they change and become last; nor is the estimated non-existence at the annihilation of the world, which is a subsequent non-existence, conceivable to become prior. So the two endpoints of the world's existence – one being first and the other last – are two essential fixed endpoints, not conceivable to change by changing the relations to them at all, unlike 'above' and 'below'. So then it is possible for us to say: the world has no 'above' or 'below', but you cannot say: the world's existence has no 'before' or 'after'. And when 'before' and 'after' are established, there is no meaning for time except that which is expressed by 'before' and 'after'. We said: there is no difference, for there is no purpose in specifying the term 'above' and 'below'; rather we shift to the term 'inside' and 'outside', and say: the world has an inside and outside. So is there outside the world anything, either a plenum or a void? They will say: beyond the world there is neither void nor plenum. If you mean by 'outside' its upper surface, then it has an outside; if you mean something else, then it has no outside. Similarly, when it is said to us: does the world's existence have a 'before'? We say: if by it is meant whether the world's existence has a beginning, i.e., an endpoint from which it began, then it has a 'before' in this sense, just as the world has an outside on the interpretation that it is the exposed surface and the boundary; and if you mean by 'before' something else, then the world has no before, just as if by 'outside' of the world something other than the surface is meant, it is said the world has no outside. For if you say: it is not intelligible that a beginning of existence has no 'before', then it is said: nor is it intelligible that a finite existence of a body has no outside. For if you say: its outside is its surface, which is its boundary, nothing else; we say: its before is the beginning of its existence, which is its endpoint, nothing else. It remains that we say: God exists and no world with Him. This statement also does not necessitate proving something else. And what indicates that this is the work of estimation (wahm) is that it is specific to time and space. For the opponent, even if he believes in the pre-eternity of the body, his estimation submits to the estimation of its temporal origination; and we, even if we believe in its temporal origination, perhaps our estimation submits to the estimation of its pre-eternity. This concerning the body. When we turn to time, the opponent cannot estimate a time that has no 'before' – whereas a contrary to the belief can be placed in the estimation as an estimate and assumption – and this cannot be placed in the estimation. Similarly concerning space: for whoever believes in the finitude of the body and whoever does not believe, each one is incapable of estimating a body beyond which there is neither void nor plenum; indeed his estimation does not submit to accepting that. But it was said: clear reason, if it does not prevent the existence of a finite body by virtue of proof, then one does not pay attention to the estimation – the deficiency of estimation. Clear reason does not prevent an existence that begins with nothing before it. And if so, then one does not pay attention to it, because estimation, having never become familiar with a finite body except with another body next to it, or air that it imagines as void, cannot conceive that for the absent. Similarly, estimation has never become familiar with a temporal entity except after something else, so it recoils from estimating a temporal entity that has no 'before' which is an existent thing that has passed. This is the cause of the error. And resistance is achieved by this counterargument. (1) That is, the case in every belief is that its contrary can be placed in the estimation as an estimate and assumption. (2) Reference to the contrary of the philosopher's belief concerning time. (3) Just as the philosopher's belief concerning space cannot be placed in the estimation, and that did not prevent him from believing it, following reason, so also the contrary of his belief concerning time, even if it cannot be placed in the estimation, yet reason leads to it, so it must be accepted.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an argument distinguishing between spatial terms like 'above' and 'below' and temporal terms like 'before' and 'after'. Ghazali begins by showing that spatial terms are relative: they depend on perspective, as with a piece of wood whose orientation changes the names of its sides, while the world itself does not change. In contrast, the non-existence before the world and the first endpoint of its existence are essential to it; they cannot change. Similarly, the non-existence after the world is essential and cannot become prior. Thus the two endpoints of the world (beginning and end) are fixed and essential, unlike 'above' and 'below'. Therefore, we can say the world has no absolute 'above' or 'below', but you cannot say it has no 'before' or 'after'. Time is defined by 'before' and 'after'. 

Ghazali then anticipates an objection: what if we use other spatial terms like 'inside' and 'outside'? He responds that the same relativity applies. The philosophers claim there is no void or plenum outside the world, only the surface. Similarly, if we ask about 'before', it can only mean the starting point of existence, nothing more. So the parallel holds: just as the world has no spatial outside except its surface, it has no temporal before except its beginning. 

Next, Ghazali addresses the role of estimation (wahm). Both the philosopher who believes the world is eternal and the theologian who believes it is created can mentally estimate the contrary (e.g., the eternity of a created body) for bodies. But when it comes to time, the philosopher cannot estimate a time with no 'before'. This is like the impossibility of estimating a body with no void or plenum beyond it. However, this failure of estimation does not disprove the rational proof. Reason shows that a finite body exists, even if estimation cannot picture it without surroundings. Similarly, reason shows that a temporal entity with no prior thing exists (i.e., the world's beginning), even if estimation struggles. The error arises because estimation is habituated to finding a prior thing for every event. Ghazali concludes that the counterargument holds, and the mistake is due to the limit of estimation, not reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That spatial terms like 'above' and 'below' are equally as essential as temporal terms like 'before' and 'after'.
Correction: Ghazali shows that spatial terms are relative and change with perspective, while temporal endpoints are fixed and essential to the world's existence.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is the world said to have no 'above' or 'below'?
A) Because the world is spherical and thus has no top or bottom.
B) Because these terms depend on the orientation of the observer, not on the world itself.
C) Because the world is infinite and thus has no boundaries.
D) Because the philosophers claim the world has no spatial limits.

Key Takeaway
The beginning and end of the world are essential properties, not relative relations, so time is real and not merely relative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your estimation (wahm) is comfortable with 'always' and 'forever'—it recoils from a beginning with no 'before'. But reason tells you that your own existence began from nothing, and your days are numbered. When did you last pause to let that fact pierce through the comfortable habit of imagining endless tomorrows? What is one concrete change you must make today because your life has an essential 'first' and 'last'?
?
Ghazali forces you to see that what feels impossible to imagine (a first moment with no prior time) is nonetheless true by rational proof. What deeply ingrained assumption about your own life—perhaps about your health, relationships, or spiritual state—do you treat as 'impossible to change' simply because you can't imagine otherwise? Will you continue to let your estimation dictate what you believe is possible, or will you submit your mental habits to the truth God has revealed and reason has proven?
?
Spatial terms like 'above' and 'below' are relative; they depend on your perspective. But the beginning and end of your existence are fixed, essential, and real. Are you living as though your relationship with God—your obedience, your repentance, your love—is something relative, adjustable like 'above' and 'below' depending on your mood or circumstance? Or do you treat it as the fixed, essential reality that it truly is? What would it look like for you to live today with that conviction?

Imagination, Habit, and Rational Proof
Ghazali argues that imaginative impossibility does not entail real impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the imagination's failure to conceive a beginningless time does not constitute a valid objection against the world's temporal origination.

Core Idea
Imagination is habit-bound; reason is not.

Ghazali has just argued for the temporal origination of the world. Now he anticipates a philosopher's objection: we cannot imagine a time without a beginning. His response is subtle and powerful. He distinguishes between what the imagination can picture and what reason can accept. He shows that imaginative failure stems from habit, not logical contradiction. This passage is a classic example of Ghazali's method of refuting opponents by turning their own principles against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage aloud
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own words and structure guide your initial understanding.
Pay attention to the footnotes marked (1), (2), (3) in the Arabic – they are part of the original text's apparatus.
2
Identify the two key claims
First, Ghazali states that we cannot imagine a time with no 'before'. Second, he says that the opposite of a belief can normally be imagined, but not here. Identify where these claims appear.
3
Follow the analogy with space
Ghazali draws a parallel: just as we cannot imagine a body with no void or plenum beyond it, yet reason may affirm it, so too with time. Work through the analogy step by step.
The footnote (3) explicitly connects the space and time cases.
4
Understand the cause of the error
Ghazali explains that the imagination fails because it is habituated – it never sees an originated thing without something before it. This is the root cause. See how he states it.
This is the crux of his refutation: the philosopher mistakes a psychological limit for a logical one.

حدوثه ، ربما أذعن وهمنا لتقدير قدمه ، هذا في الجسم ، فإذا رجعنا إلى الزمان لم يقدر الخصم على تقدير حدوث زمان لا « قبل » له ـ وخلاف (۱) المعتقد يمكن وضعه في الوهم تقديراً وفرضاً – وهذا (۲) مما لا يمكن وضعه في الوهم ، كما في – المكان (۳) فإن من يعتقد تناهى الجسم ومن لا يعتقد ، كل واحد يعجز عن تقدير جسم ليس وراءه لا خلاء ولا ملاء ، بل لا يذعن وهمه لقبول ذلك ، ولكن قيل : صريح العقل إذا لم يمنع وجود جسم متناه بحكم الدليل ، لم يلتفت إلى الوهم قصر الوهم ، فكذلك إذا لم يمنع العقل وجوداً مفتتحاً ليس قبله شيء ؛ فلا يلتفت إليه ، لأن الوهم لما لم يألف جسماً متناهياً إلا وبجنبه جسم آخر ، أو هواء تخيله خلاء ، لم يتمكن من ذلك في الغائب ؛ فكذلك لم يألف الوهم حادثاً إلا بعد شيء آخر ، فكاع عن تقدير حادث ليس له «قبل» هو شيء موجود قد انقضى ، فهذا هو سبب الغلط ، والمقاومة حاصلة بهذه المعارضة . (۱) أى الشأن في كل معتقد أن خلافه يمكن وضعه في الوهم تقديراً وفرضاً (۲) إشارة إلى خلاف معتقد الفيلسوف في الزمان . (۳) كما أن معتقد الفيلسوف في المكان لا يمكن وضعه فى الوهم ولم يمنع ذلك من اعتقاده جرياً وراء العقل ، فكذلك خلاف معتقده في الزمان وإن لم يمكن وضعه في الوهم لكن العقل ساق إليه فيجب الأخذ به.
Its occurrence: perhaps our imagination would submit to conceiving its pre-eternity. This is regarding the body. But when we turn to time, the opponent cannot conceive the occurrence of a time that has no 'before' — and the opposite of a belief can be placed in the imagination as estimation and supposition — but this is something that cannot be placed in the imagination, as in the case of space. Indeed, whoever believes in the finitude of body and whoever does not believe, each one is incapable of conceiving a body beyond which there is no void and no plenum; rather his imagination does not submit to accepting that. But it is said: clear reason, if it does not prevent the existence of a finite body by virtue of proof, does not pay heed to the imagination due to the deficiency of the imagination. Similarly, if reason does not prevent an existence that begins and has nothing before it, then it is not to be heeded. Because the imagination is not accustomed to a finite body except that there is alongside it another body or air that it imagined as void, so it could not manage that in the absent. Likewise, the imagination is not accustomed to an originated thing except after another thing, so it recoiled from conceiving an originated thing that has no 'before' that is something [already] existent that has elapsed. This is the cause of the error, and resistance is achieved by this objection. (1) That is, the situation in every belief is that its opposite can be placed in the imagination by estimation and supposition. (2) An indication of the opposite of the philosopher's belief regarding time. (3) Just as the philosopher's belief regarding space cannot be placed in the imagination and that does not prevent him from believing it, following reason, so likewise the opposite of his belief regarding time, even if it cannot be placed in the imagination, yet reason leads to it, so it must be adopted.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage defines and defends a key epistemological principle in the debate on the eternity of the world. Ghazali begins by noting that our imagination can sometimes conceive of pre-eternity for bodies, but when it comes to time, the opponent cannot imagine a time without a 'before'. He then states a general rule: for any belief, its opposite can normally be placed in the imagination, but here this is not possible. He draws an analogy with space: both those who believe the universe is finite and those who believe it is infinite cannot imagine a body with nothing beyond it—no void, no plenum. Yet reason may still accept such a possibility if proof supports it. Similarly, even if imagination cannot conceive of an originated thing with no prior existing thing, reason may still affirm it if there is no rational contradiction. The error, he explains, arises from the imagination's habit: it never sees a finite body without something next to it, and never sees an originated event without something before it. So it recoils from conceiving otherwise. But this is a limitation of imagination, not reason. The objection (mu'āraḍa) thus successfully counters the opponent's reliance on imaginative impossibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Whatever I cannot imagine is impossible in reality.
Correction: Ghazali shows that imaginative inability often stems from habit, not logical impossibility. Reason can accept what imagination cannot picture.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does the imagination fail to conceive a time without a beginning?
A) Because time is inherently inconceivable
B) Because the imagination is habituated to only conceiving an originated thing after something else
C) Because reason forbids it
D) Because the opponent's argument is invalid

Key Takeaway
The imagination's limitations, rooted in habit, do not constrain rational possibility when clear proof is present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is one belief or command of Allah that you have been avoiding because it feels 'impossible' or 'unimaginable' for you to implement? What specific proof from revelation or reason can you use to override that feeling, starting today?
?
Ghazali warns that our imagination recoils from what it is not used to. Think of a religious truth you struggle to accept (e.g., the reality of the afterlife, fate, or the necessity of certain obligations). Are you willing to let your intellect, guided by revelation, overrule your imaginative discomfort? What step will you take this week to submit to that truth?
?
The passage says the cause of error is habit. Where in your life have you mistaken a psychological limitation for a logical impossibility? Commit to one action you will take to break that habit and trust the proofs Allah has provided.

First Proof: Imtina' Analogy
The opponent defines the three categories and presents the first proof against imkan being relational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's initial definition of the three categories as purely intellectual judgments and his first argument that imkan cannot be a real attribute because the same logic would force imtina to have a subject, which it cannot.

Core Idea
Possibility and impossibility are judgments of the mind, not real attributes in things.

The opponent begins by clarifying what the Asharites mean by imkan, imtina, and wujub: they are not real properties but mental assessments. He then presents a powerful objection: if imkan required an existing subject, then imtina would too, but an impossible thing has no existence to serve as a subject, revealing the inconsistency of treating these as real attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's definition
Read the opening lines where he defines imkan, imtina, wujub as intellectual judgments.
Notice the concise definitions: possible = what can be conceived, impossible = what cannot, necessary = what cannot be conceived as non-existent.
2
Understand the first proof
The opponent argues by analogy: if imkan is relational, imtina must be too, but imtina cannot have a subject because the impossible does not exist.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument challenges the view that imkan is a real attribute.
If impossibility doesn't require a subject, why would possibility?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض أن يقال : الإمكان الذي ذكرتموه يرجع إلى قضاء العقل ، فكل ما قدر العقل وجوده فلم يمتنع عليه تقديره ، سميناه ممكناً و إن امتنع سميناه مستحيلا ، وإن لم يقدر على تقدير عدمه ، سميناه واجباً ، فهذه قضايا عقلية لا تحتاج إلى موجود حتى تجعل وصفاً له بدليل ثلاثة أمور . أحدها: أن الإمكان لو استدعى شيئاً موجوداً يضاف إليه ويقال : إنه إمكانه ، لاستدعى الامتناع شيئاً موجوداً يقال إنه امتناعه ، وليس للمتنع في ذاته وجود ، ولا مادة يطرأ عليها المحال حتى يضاف الامتناع إلى المادة والثانى : أن السواد والبياض يقضى العقل فيهما قبل وجودهما بكونهما ممكنين، فإن كان هذا الإمكان مضافاً إلى الجسم الذي يطرآن عليه حتى يقال : معناه أن هذا الجسم يمكن أن يسود وأن يبيض ، فإذن ليس البياض في نفسه ممكناً ولا له نعت الإمكان ، وإنما الممكن الجسم والإمكان مضاف إليه ، فنقول : ما حكم نفس السواد في ذاته ، أهو ممكن ، أو واجب ، أو ممتنع ؟ ولابد من القول بأنه ممكن . فدل أن العقل فى القضية بالإمكان ، لا يفتقر إلى وضع ذات موجودة يضيف إليها الإمكان . والثالث : أن نفوس الآدميين عندهم جواهر قائمة بأنفسها ، ليست بجسم ولا مادة ، ولا منطبعة فى مادة ، وهى حادثة على ما اختاره ابن سينا والمحققون منهم ، ولها إمكان قبل حدوثها ، وليس لها ذات ولا مادة ، فإمكانها وصف إضافى ، ولا يرجع إلى قدرة القادر ، و إلى الفاعل ، فإلى ماذا يرجع ؟!
The objection: that the imkan (possibility) you mentioned refers to a judgment of the intellect. So everything whose existence the intellect can conceive and its conception is not prevented, we call it possible; if its conception is prevented, we call it impossible; and if it cannot conceive its non-existence, we call it necessary. These are intellectual judgments that do not require an existing thing in order to be a description of it, as evidenced by three things. First: that if imkan required an existing thing to which it is attributed and it is said 'it is its imkan', then imtina' (impossibility) would also require an existing thing of which it is said 'it is its imtina''. But the impossible in itself has no existence, nor any substrate on which the impossible occurs so that impossibility could be attributed to the substrate. Second: that the intellect judges blackness and whiteness before their existence as being possible. If this imkan is attributed to the body on which they occur so that it is said 'it means that this body can become black and white', then whiteness is not possible in itself nor has it the attribute of imkan; rather the possible is the body and imkan is attributed to it. We say: what is the ruling of blackness itself in its essence—is it possible, necessary, or impossible? It must be said that it is possible. This shows that the intellect in its judgment of imkan does not need to posit an existing entity to which it attributes imkan. Third: that human souls, according to them, are substances subsisting by themselves, not a body nor a substrate, nor impressed in a substrate, and they are originated according to the choice of Ibn Sina and the verifiers among them. They have imkan before their origination, and they have no essence nor substrate. So their imkan is a relational attribute, and it does not refer to the power of the Powerful, nor to the agent. So to what does it refer?!
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's objection against the view that imkan (possibility), imtina' (impossibility), and wujub (necessity) are purely intellectual judgments that do not depend on existing things. The opponent argues that if these were mere mental judgments, they would not be real attributes. He defines the terms and then gives three proofs that imkan must be a relational attribute (wasf idafi) requiring an existing subject.

First proof: If imkan required an existing subject to which it is attributed, then likewise imtina' would require an existing subject. But an impossible thing (like a partner with God) has no existence at all, so impossibility cannot be attributed to any existing subject. Hence imtina' is purely intellectual, and by analogy imkan is also intellectual.

Second proof: Before blackness or whiteness exist, the intellect judges them as possible. If this imkan is attributed to the body that will receive them, then the body is possible, not the blackness itself. But when we ask about the blackness in itself, it must be possible. So the intellect's judgment of imkan does not require an existing subject; it applies to the blackness itself, even before existence.

Third proof: Human souls, according to Ibn Sina and others, are immaterial substances that originate. They have imkan before their origin, yet they have no matter or substrate. So their imkan cannot be relational to a substrate. It must be a pure intellectual judgment. The opponent challenges: if imkan is not relational to the power of the agent, to what does it refer? This sets up the following discussion.

Key Terms
إمكان
امتناع
واجب
قضاء العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That possibility and necessity are real properties that exist in things independently of the mind.
Correction: The opponent argues that they are purely intellectual judgments, as shown by the fact that impossibility would also have to be a real property, but it cannot attach to a non-existent subject.

Checkpoint
According to the opponent's first proof, why can't imtina' be a relational attribute?
A) Because the impossible thing has no existence to serve as a subject.
B) Because impossibility is purely negative.
C) Because the intellect cannot conceive impossibility.
D) Because impossibility requires a specific substrate.

Key Takeaway
The opponent uses the analogy with impossibility to argue that possibility does not require a real subject; both are mental judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is necessary' or 'a partner to God is impossible', do you realize that these are judgments your own intellect makes? Have you ever personally examined the rational basis for these judgments, or do you merely repeat them? Take a moment to articulate why you yourself consider impossibility to be a necessary truth, not just a cultural belief.
?
This passage teaches that possibility and impossibility are not real attributes in things but judgments of the mind. Does this make your faith feel less certain or more certain? If these categories are mental, then your conviction about God's oneness is anchored in your own intellect—could you defend it if challenged? Be honest: have you ever truly owned the rational proof for tawhid, or have you relied on it only as a named conclusion?
?
If impossibility were a real attribute, it would have to exist in a subject—but the impossible has no existence. Do you ever treat your doubts as if they were real things 'out there' rather than judgments of your own mind? What would change in your spiritual life if you saw your beliefs and doubts as decisions of your intellect rather than fixed realities?

Second Proof: Possibility of Blackness Before Existence
The opponent presents the second proof that imkan is intellectual, using blackness before existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's second proof: that the intellect judges blackness as possible before it exists, without needing a body to attach that possibility to, showing that imkan is a mental judgment.

Core Idea
The intellect can judge a non-existent quality as possible without linking it to an existing subject.

The opponent continues his argument by examining the attribute of blackness. Before it exists, the intellect says it is possible. If we tried to attribute that possibility to a body, then blackness itself would not be possible, which is absurd. This forces us to accept that the intellect can impute possibility directly to the quality itself, without needing an existing substrate.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully.
2
Understand the hypothetical
The opponent considers what would happen if imkan were relational: blackness's possibility would be attributed to the body, not to blackness itself.
3
Ask the critical question
The opponent then asks: what is blackness in itself? It must be possible, showing that the intellect judges it as possible without a body.
4
Draw the conclusion
Therefore, imkan does not require an existing subject; it is a mental judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the intellect can judge a quality as possible without it ever existing, what does that imply about the absolute sovereignty of Allah over all possibilities? Are you treating the future as a set of possibilities under your control, or as entirely dependent on His will?
?
This lesson demonstrates that your own mind can conceive of possibility in things that are not yet created. How does that humble you to realize that you are not the source of that possibility, but only recognize it? Will you thank Allah tonight for giving you a mind that can perceive imkan?
?
When you hope for something that does not yet exist, you are making a judgment of possibility. Does your hope rest on the mere possibility, or on certainty that Allah can bring it about? How can you transform your 'possibility' into 'certainty' through trust in Allah?

Third Proof: Human Souls as Immaterial Substances
The opponent uses human souls as a case to show imkan cannot be relational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's third proof: that human souls are immaterial substances with possibility before existence, yet they have no matter or substrate, so their possibility cannot be relational, forcing the opponent to question what it relates to.

Core Idea
If souls are immaterial yet have possibility, their possibility cannot be attached to anything physical, exposing the flaw in treating imkan as relational.

The opponent finally turns to the case of human souls. According to Avicenna and the verifiers, souls are immaterial substances that are originated. They have possibility before coming into existence, but they have no matter or substrate to which that possibility could be attributed. This challenges the view that imkan is a real attribute, because the opponent asks: to what does the soul's possibility relate? It cannot be to a material substrate, and it cannot be to the agent's power alone, so the question remains unanswered.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation about human souls.
2
Understand the nature of souls
Souls are immaterial substances, not in a substrate, originated.
3
Consider their possibility
They have imkan before their existence, but no essence or matter to carry that possibility.
4
Analyze the forced question
The opponent asks: if imkan is relational, to what does it relate? Not to the power of the agent, so it must be a pure intellectual judgment.

الاعتراض أن يقال : الإمكان الذي ذكرتموه يرجع إلى قضاء العقل ، فكل ما قدر العقل وجوده فلم يمتنع عليه تقديره ، سميناه ممكناً و إن امتنع سميناه مستحيلا ، وإن لم يقدر على تقدير عدمه ، سميناه واجباً ، فهذه قضايا عقلية لا تحتاج إلى موجود حتى تجعل وصفاً له بدليل ثلاثة أمور . أحدها: أن الإمكان لو استدعى شيئاً موجوداً يضاف إليه ويقال : إنه إمكانه ، لاستدعى الامتناع شيئاً موجوداً يقال إنه امتناعه ، وليس للمتنع في ذاته وجود ، ولا مادة يطرأ عليها المحال حتى يضاف الامتناع إلى المادة والثانى : أن السواد والبياض يقضى العقل فيهما قبل وجودهما بكونهما ممكنين، فإن كان هذا الإمكان مضافاً إلى الجسم الذي يطرآن عليه حتى يقال : معناه أن هذا الجسم يمكن أن يسود وأن يبيض ، فإذن ليس البياض في نفسه ممكناً ولا له نعت الإمكان ، وإنما الممكن الجسم والإمكان مضاف إليه ، فنقول : ما حكم نفس السواد في ذاته ، أهو ممكن ، أو واجب ، أو ممتنع ؟ ولابد من القول بأنه ممكن . فدل أن العقل فى القضية بالإمكان ، لا يفتقر إلى وضع ذات موجودة يضيف إليها الإمكان . والثالث : أن نفوس الآدميين عندهم جواهر قائمة بأنفسها ، ليست بجسم ولا مادة ، ولا منطبعة فى مادة ، وهى حادثة على ما اختاره ابن سينا والمحققون منهم ، ولها إمكان قبل حدوثها ، وليس لها ذات ولا مادة ، فإمكانها وصف إضافى ، ولا يرجع إلى قدرة القادر ، و إلى الفاعل ، فإلى ماذا يرجع ؟!
The objection: that the imkan (possibility) you mentioned refers to a judgment of the intellect. So everything whose existence the intellect can conceive and its conception is not prevented, we call it possible; if its conception is prevented, we call it impossible; and if it cannot conceive its non-existence, we call it necessary. These are intellectual judgments that do not require an existing thing in order to be a description of it, as evidenced by three things. First: that if imkan required an existing thing to which it is attributed and it is said 'it is its imkan', then imtina' (impossibility) would also require an existing thing of which it is said 'it is its imtina''. But the impossible in itself has no existence, nor any substrate on which the impossible occurs so that impossibility could be attributed to the substrate. Second: that the intellect judges blackness and whiteness before their existence as being possible. If this imkan is attributed to the body on which they occur so that it is said 'it means that this body can become black and white', then whiteness is not possible in itself nor has it the attribute of imkan; rather the possible is the body and imkan is attributed to it. We say: what is the ruling of blackness itself in its essence—is it possible, necessary, or impossible? It must be said that it is possible. This shows that the intellect in its judgment of imkan does not need to posit an existing entity to which it attributes imkan. Third: that human souls, according to them, are substances subsisting by themselves, not a body nor a substrate, nor impressed in a substrate, and they are originated according to the choice of Ibn Sina and the verifiers among them. They have imkan before their origination, and they have no essence nor substrate. So their imkan is a relational attribute, and it does not refer to the power of the Powerful, nor to the agent. So to what does it refer?!
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's objection against the view that imkan (possibility), imtina' (impossibility), and wujub (necessity) are purely intellectual judgments that do not depend on existing things. The opponent argues that if these were mere mental judgments, they would not be real attributes. He defines the terms and then gives three proofs that imkan must be a relational attribute (wasf idafi) requiring an existing subject.

First proof: If imkan required an existing subject to which it is attributed, then likewise imtina' would require an existing subject. But an impossible thing (like a partner with God) has no existence at all, so impossibility cannot be attributed to any existing subject. Hence imtina' is purely intellectual, and by analogy imkan is also intellectual.

Second proof: Before blackness or whiteness exist, the intellect judges them as possible. If this imkan is attributed to the body that will receive them, then the body is possible, not the blackness itself. But when we ask about the blackness in itself, it must be possible. So the intellect's judgment of imkan does not require an existing subject; it applies to the blackness itself, even before existence.

Third proof: Human souls, according to Ibn Sina and others, are immaterial substances that originate. They have imkan before their origin, yet they have no matter or substrate. So their imkan cannot be relational to a substrate. It must be a pure intellectual judgment. The opponent challenges: if imkan is not relational to the power of the agent, to what does it refer? This sets up the following discussion.

According to the opponent's third proof, what is the nature of human souls?
A) They are material bodies.
B) They are immaterial substances.
C) They are attributes of the body.
D) They are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Immaterial souls have possibility before existence, but no substrate, proving that imkan cannot be a relational attribute; it must be a mental judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul is immaterial, with no physical substrate, yet it has possibility before existence. If your possibility is not tied to matter, then your very existence is a direct act of the Creator. Are you living each day as if your existence is a gift from Him, or do you take it for granted? What would change if you truly internalized that your soul's potential is not bound by physics?
?
The passage challenges the idea that possibility is a real attribute you possess. Instead, it is a mental judgment. How does this strip away any excuse that your potential is limited by your circumstances or nature? If your imkan is a mere judgment, then you have no inherent limitation—only what you conceive. Will you stop confining yourself with false beliefs about what you can become?
?
The rhetorical question ends: 'So to what does it refer?!' It leaves you without a place to anchor your soul's possibility except in the will of the Agent. If your entire potential is referred to God alone, then your life's direction is a direct response to His will. Are you actively aligning your choices with what He desires, or are you drifting aimlessly? What concrete step will you take today to surrender your potential to Him?
