The Opponent's Objection and the Relational Nature of Impossibility
Ghazali presents the opponent's objection and his first refutation regarding impossibility and necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that imkan must be mind-independent because knowledge requires a known, and Ghazali's counter that impossibility and necessity are relational attributes.

Core Idea
Impossibility and necessity are relational, not absolute, attributes.

The philosophers argued that if imkan were merely a mental judgment, it would be dependent on the mind. But imkan is known, and the known exists independently of knowledge. Ghazali responds by showing that impossibility, too, is a relational attribute requiring a substrate. He further notes that necessity is clearly attributed to the Necessary Existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's objection
Read the first part where the opponent argues that reducing imkan to a mental judgment is impossible because knowledge requires a known object that exists independently.
Notice the claim that imkan would remain even if all minds ceased.
2
Identify Ghazali's counter
Ghazali immediately addresses the 'three matters' (imtina', wujub) showing they are also relational, so the objection does not hold.
3
Reflect on the relational nature
Consider the example: impossibility of blackness in a white locus is attributed to the subject, not absolute.
Similarly, necessity is attributed to the Necessary Existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: رد الإمكان إلى قضاء العقل محال ، إذ لا معنى لقضاء العقل إلا العلم بالإمكان ، فالإمكان معلوم ، وهو غير العلم ، بل العلم يحيط به ويتبعه ويتعلق به على ما هو عليه ، والعلم لو قدر عدمه ، لم ينعدم المعلوم ، والمعلوم إذا قدر انتفاؤه ، انتفى العلم ، فالعلم والمعلوم أمران اثنان ، أحدهما تابع والآخر متبوع ، ولو قدرنا إعراض العقلاء عن تقدير الإمكان ، وغفلتهم نقول : لا يرتفع الإمكان ، بل الممكنات في أنفسها ممكنات في أنفسها ، ولكن العقول غفلت عنها ، ولو عدمت العقول والعقلاء لبقى الإمكان لا محالة وأما الأمور الثلاثة فلا حجة فيها ، فإن الامتناع أيضاً وصف إضافى ، يستدعى موجوداً ، يضاف إليه ، ومعنى الممتنع الجمع بين الضدين ، فإذا كان المحل أبيض ، كان ممتنعاً عليه أن يسود مع وجود البياض ، فلابد من موضوع يشار إليه ، موصوف بصفة ، فعند ذلك يقال : ضده ممتنع عليه ، فيكون الامتناع وصفاً إضافياً قائماً بموضوع مضافاً إليه . وأما الوجوب فلا يخفى أنه مضاف إلى الموجود الواجب وأما الثانى ، وهو كون السواد فى نفسه ممكناً ، فغلط ، فإنه إن خذ مجرداً دون مجل يحله ، كان ممتنعاً لا ممكناً ، وإنما يصير ممكناً إذا قدر هيأة في الجسم مهيأ لتبدل هيأة ، والتبدل ممكن على الجسم ، و إلا فليس للسواد نفس مفردة ، حتى توصف بإمكان . وأما الثالث ، وهو النفس فهي قديمة عند فريق ، ولكن ممكن لها التعلق بالأبدان ، فلم يلزم على هذا ما قلتم . ومن سلم حدوثه ، فقد اعتقد فريق منهم منطبع في المادة تابع للمزاج على ما دل عليه كلام جالينوس فى بعض المواضع ، فتكون فى مادة ، وإمكانها مضاف إلى مادتها ، وعلى مذهب من سلم أنها حادثة وليست منطبعة ، فمعناه أن المادة ممكن لها أن يدبرها نفس ناطقة ، فيكون الإمكان السابق على الحدوث مضافاً إلى المادة، فإنها وإن لم تنطبع فيها فلها علاقة معها ، إذ هى المدبرة المستعملة لها، فيكون الإمكان راجعاً إليها بهذا الطريق، والجواب : أن رد الإمكان والوجوب والامتناع ، إلى قضايا عقلية، صحيح وما ذكروه من أن معنى قضاء العقل علمه ، والعلم يستدعى معلوماً ، فنقول له :
If it is said: reducing imkan to a judgment of the intellect is impossible, for the meaning of judgment of the intellect is nothing but knowledge of imkan. So imkan is known, and it is other than knowledge. Rather, knowledge encompasses it, follows it, and relates to it as it is. If knowledge were supposed non-existent, the known would not cease to exist. If the known is supposed to cease, knowledge ceases. So knowledge and the known are two things, one is follower and the other is followed. If we suppose that rational beings turn away from judging imkan and are heedless, we say: imkan is not removed. Rather, the possible things are in themselves possible in themselves, but the intellects are heedless of them. If intellects and rational beings ceased to exist, imkan would remain without doubt. As for the three matters, there is no proof in them. For imtina' is also a relational attribute that requires an existing thing to which it is attributed. The meaning of the impossible is the conjunction of two opposites. So if the locus is white, it is impossible for it to become black while whiteness exists. So there must be a subject indicated, described by a quality. Then it is said: its opposite is impossible for it. Thus impossibility becomes a relational attribute subsisting in a subject attributed to it. As for necessity, it is obvious that it is attributed to the necessary existent. As for the second—that blackness in itself is possible—it is a mistake. For if it is taken abstractly without a locus that contains it, it is impossible, not possible. It only becomes possible if it is considered as a state in a body prepared for the change of state, and change is possible for the body. Otherwise, blackness has no individual essence to be described with imkan. As for the third—the soul—it is eternal for some, but it is possible for it to be connected with bodies, so what you said does not follow. As for those who affirm its origination, some of them believe it is impressed in the substrate, following the temperament, as indicated by Galen's words in some places. Thus it is in a substrate, and its imkan is attributed to its substrate. According to the view of those who affirm its origination and that it is not impressed, its meaning is that the substrate is capable of being governed by a rational soul. Thus the imkan prior to origination is attributed to the substrate. For even if it is not impressed in it, it has a relation with it, as it is the governor and user of it. So imkan refers to it in this way. The answer: that reducing imkan, necessity, and impossibility to intellectual judgments is correct. And what they mentioned—that the meaning of the intellect's judgment is its knowledge, and knowledge requires a known—we say to him:
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage contains the opponent's counter-argument and Ghazali's detailed refutation. The opponent argues that if imkan were a mental judgment, then imkan would be a known thing distinct from the knowledge itself. Since knowledge depends on the known, and the known can exist without being known, imkan must exist mind-independently. Ghazali responds by addressing each of the three proofs.

First, he concedes that imtina' is also a relational attribute, but it requires an existing subject. For example, impossibility of opposites coexisting is attributed to a particular subject (e.g., whiteness prevents blackness in the same locus). So impossibility is relational, not purely mental.

Second, Ghazali rejects that blackness in itself is possible. Isolated blackness (without a body) is impossible; it is only possible as a state of a body. So its possibility is attributed to the body, not to itself.

Third, regarding the soul, Ghazali notes that some philosophers consider it eternal (so it doesn't have imkan before existence), and for those who consider it originated, some believe it is impressed in the substrate (like Galen). Hence its imkan is attributed to the substrate. Even for those who consider it non-impressed, its relation to the body as governor and user makes its imkan refer to the body.

Finally, Ghazali briefly states the correct answer: that imkan, imtina', and wujub are indeed intellectual judgments, but he is cut off before completing the full argument.

Key Terms
علم
معلوم
إمكان
امتناع
وجوب
وصف إضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That imkan, imtina', and wujub are absolute attributes independent of any subject.
Correction: Ghazali demonstrates that imtina' and wujub are relational attributes requiring a substrate, implying imkan is also relational.

Checkpoint
What does the opponent claim about the relationship between knowledge and the known?
A) The known follows knowledge.
B) The known is independent of knowledge.
C) Knowledge creates the known.
D) The known and knowledge are identical.

Key Takeaway
Impossibility and necessity are relational attributes requiring a substrate, undermining the objection that imkan must be mind-independent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of something as 'impossible' in your own life—like changing a habit or reconciling a relationship—do you treat that impossibility as absolute or relational? How does Ghazali's argument that impossibility requires a subject and a quality change how you might understand that 'impossibility'? Will you now pause to ask: 'What is the subject, and what is the opposing quality?'
?
The opponent claims that the known exists independently of knowledge, meaning reality is fixed regardless of your awareness. But Ghazali shows that impossibility is relational: it depends on the thing's current state. Is there an area where you have assumed your judgments about what is 'impossible' are objectively true, when in fact they may be contingent on your own perspective? How willing are you to re-examine that assumption today?

Ghazali's Refutation of Blackness and the Soul as Possible
Ghazali refutes the second and third proofs regarding blackness and the soul, then affirms the correct view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali rejects the claim that blackness in itself is possible, and how the possibility of the soul is always attributed to a substrate or relation.

Core Idea
Blackness without a body is impossible; the soul's possibility depends on a substrate.

The opponent had argued that imkan must be objective because blackness and the soul are possible in themselves. Ghazali shows that blackness isolated from a body is impossible—it only becomes possible as a state of a body. The soul's possibility, whether it is eternal or created, impressed or non-impressed, always refers to a substrate (the body or its relation). Ultimately, Ghazali affirms that the three categories are intellectual judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the second refutation
Focus on Ghazali's argument that blackness taken abstractly is impossible. It only becomes possible as a state of a body prepared for change.
2
Read the third refutation
Notice how Ghazali addresses the soul: for those who say it is eternal, its imkan is connected to bodies; for those who say it is created, its imkan is attributed to the substrate (body).
3
Understand the final answer
Ghazali concludes that reducing imkan, wujub, and imtina' to intellectual judgments is correct, and the opponent's objection is flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: رد الإمكان إلى قضاء العقل محال ، إذ لا معنى لقضاء العقل إلا العلم بالإمكان ، فالإمكان معلوم ، وهو غير العلم ، بل العلم يحيط به ويتبعه ويتعلق به على ما هو عليه ، والعلم لو قدر عدمه ، لم ينعدم المعلوم ، والمعلوم إذا قدر انتفاؤه ، انتفى العلم ، فالعلم والمعلوم أمران اثنان ، أحدهما تابع والآخر متبوع ، ولو قدرنا إعراض العقلاء عن تقدير الإمكان ، وغفلتهم نقول : لا يرتفع الإمكان ، بل الممكنات في أنفسها ممكنات في أنفسها ، ولكن العقول غفلت عنها ، ولو عدمت العقول والعقلاء لبقى الإمكان لا محالة وأما الأمور الثلاثة فلا حجة فيها ، فإن الامتناع أيضاً وصف إضافى ، يستدعى موجوداً ، يضاف إليه ، ومعنى الممتنع الجمع بين الضدين ، فإذا كان المحل أبيض ، كان ممتنعاً عليه أن يسود مع وجود البياض ، فلابد من موضوع يشار إليه ، موصوف بصفة ، فعند ذلك يقال : ضده ممتنع عليه ، فيكون الامتناع وصفاً إضافياً قائماً بموضوع مضافاً إليه . وأما الوجوب فلا يخفى أنه مضاف إلى الموجود الواجب وأما الثانى ، وهو كون السواد فى نفسه ممكناً ، فغلط ، فإنه إن خذ مجرداً دون مجل يحله ، كان ممتنعاً لا ممكناً ، وإنما يصير ممكناً إذا قدر هيأة في الجسم مهيأ لتبدل هيأة ، والتبدل ممكن على الجسم ، و إلا فليس للسواد نفس مفردة ، حتى توصف بإمكان . وأما الثالث ، وهو النفس فهي قديمة عند فريق ، ولكن ممكن لها التعلق بالأبدان ، فلم يلزم على هذا ما قلتم . ومن سلم حدوثه ، فقد اعتقد فريق منهم منطبع في المادة تابع للمزاج على ما دل عليه كلام جالينوس فى بعض المواضع ، فتكون فى مادة ، وإمكانها مضاف إلى مادتها ، وعلى مذهب من سلم أنها حادثة وليست منطبعة ، فمعناه أن المادة ممكن لها أن يدبرها نفس ناطقة ، فيكون الإمكان السابق على الحدوث مضافاً إلى المادة، فإنها وإن لم تنطبع فيها فلها علاقة معها ، إذ هى المدبرة المستعملة لها، فيكون الإمكان راجعاً إليها بهذا الطريق، والجواب : أن رد الإمكان والوجوب والامتناع ، إلى قضايا عقلية، صحيح وما ذكروه من أن معنى قضاء العقل علمه ، والعلم يستدعى معلوماً ، فنقول له :

?
Ghazali shows that even the possibility of your own soul depends on a substrate and a relation to a body. Do you live as if your existence and abilities are self-sufficient, or do you constantly acknowledge your dependence on Allah for every moment of being? How can you make that dependence more present in your daily actions?
?
The philosophers thought blackness in itself is possible. Ghazali corrects: it is only possible as a state of a body. What are some 'blacknesses' in your life—things you assume are good or possible in isolation—that you need to re-evaluate as only meaningful in relation to Allah's will and purpose?
?
The soul is described as the governor of the body. Are you currently allowing your soul to govern your body according to divine guidance, or are your bodily desires governing your soul? What specific change can you make today to restore the proper order?

The Three Rational Judgments as Real Relations
Ghazali argues that imkan, wujub, and imtina' are relational attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the three rational judgments (imkan, wujub, imtina') are not merely mental concepts but have real extra-mental existence as relational attributes.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments are real relations, not just ideas in the mind.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali refutes the notion that the rational judgments of possibility, impossibility, and necessity are nothing more than mental constructs. He argues that these attributes have a genuine basis in reality, independent of human perception, and are in fact relational in nature. This is a foundational point in Islamic theology and philosophy, grounding objective truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without translating, to get a sense of Ghazali's rhythm and argument structure. Then reread with the translation.
Notice how Ghazali uses the terms 'imkan', 'imtina', and 'wujub'.
2
Understand Ghazali's defense of possibility
Ghazali first argues that possibility (imkan) is known, but it is not identical to knowledge. It remains even if all minds ignore it.
He uses the example of possible things being possible in themselves, independent of intellects.
3
Analyze impossibility as a relation
Next, Ghazali explains that impossibility (imtina') is a relational attribute requiring a subject and an attribute. He gives the example of whiteness making blackness impossible.
This shows impossibility is not just a mental judgment but a real relation in the world.
4
Consider necessity
Finally, Ghazali notes that necessity (wujub) is clearly relational, as it is attributed to the Necessary Existent (God).
This completes the argument that all three are relational, not merely mental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنا إلا العلم بالإمكان ، فالإمكان معلوم ، وهو غير العلم ، بل العلم يحيط به ويتبعه ويتعلق به على ما هو عليه ، والعلم لو قدر عدمه ، لم ينعدم المعلوم ، والمعلوم إذا قدر انتفاؤه ، انتفى العلم ، فالعلم والمعلوم أمران اثنان ، أحدهما تابع والآخر متبوع ، ولو قدرنا إعراض العقلاء عن تقدير الإمكان ، وغفلتهم نقول : لا يرتفع الإمكان ، بل الممكنات في أنفسها ممكنات في أنفسها ، ولكن العقول غفلت عنها ، ولو عدمت العقول والعقلاء لبقى الإمكان لا محالة وأما الأمور الثلاثة فلا حجة فيها ، فإن الامتناع أيضاً وصف إضافى ، يستدعى موجوداً ، يضاف إليه ، ومعنى الممتنع الجمع بين الضدين ، فإذا كان المحل أبيض ، كان ممتنعاً عليه أن يسود مع وجود البياض ، فلابد من موضوع يشار إليه ، موصوف بصفة ، فعند ذلك يقال : ضده ممتنع عليه ، فيكون الامتناع وصفاً إضافياً قائماً بموضوع مضافاً إليه . وأما الوجوب فلا يخفى أنه مضاف إلى الموجود الواجب
But we have only knowledge of possibility: possibility is known, and it is not knowledge; rather knowledge encompasses it, follows it, and relates to it as it is. If knowledge were supposed to cease, the known would not cease; but if the known were supposed to be negated, knowledge would be negated. So knowledge and the known are two things, one following and the other followed. And if we suppose the rational beings to turn away from estimating possibility and to be heedless, we say: possibility does not cease; rather the possible things are in themselves possible in themselves, but the intellects are heedless of them. And if intellects and rational beings were non-existent, possibility would necessarily remain. As for the three things (impossibility, necessity, possibility), there is no argument in them: for impossibility is also a relational attribute, requiring an existent to which it is related. The meaning of 'impossible' is the conjunction of two opposites; so if the substrate is white, it is impossible for it to become black while whiteness exists. Thus there must be a subject that is pointed to, described by an attribute; at that point it is said: its opposite is impossible for it. So impossibility is a relational attribute subsisting in a subject and related to it. As for necessity, it is clear that it is related to the necessary existent.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is an argument that the three attributes—possibility (imkan), impossibility (imtina'), and necessity (wujub)—are relational attributes (sifat idafiyya) and not merely mental constructs. Ghazali begins by addressing a previous point: he says that we have only knowledge of possibility, not that possibility is identical to knowledge. Knowledge depends on the known, not vice versa. Then he argues that possibility is a real attribute of things in themselves, independent of the minds of rational beings; even if all intellects were heedless of possibility, possibility would still obtain in the possible things themselves. Next, he addresses the three attributes collectively, claiming that they are not mere mental judgments. He explains impossibility: it is a relation that requires a subject and an attribute; for example, it is impossible for something white to turn black while whiteness remains, so impossibility is predicated of a subject. As for necessity, it is clearly a relation to the necessary existent (God). Thus Ghazali establishes that these attributes have a real basis in existence, not just in the mind.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الامتناع
الوجوب
صفة إضافية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three rational judgments (imkan, wujub, imtina') are purely mental constructs with no basis in external reality.
Correction: Ghazali argues that possibility remains even if all minds ignore it, impossibility requires a real subject and attribute, and necessity relates to the Necessary Existent, proving they are real relations.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Ghazali establishes that the three rational attributes—possibility, impossibility, and necessity—are real extra-mental relations, not just mental constructs.

?
If all rational beings were heedless of possibility, would you still believe that your choices and the world's events have real potential? Does the objectivity of possibility ground your hope in God's power to change your situation?
?
Consider something you believe is impossible (e.g., God being unjust). Does this lesson's claim that impossibility is a real relational attribute deepen your trust in God's justice, or does it expose a place where you doubt His perfection? How will you act on that realization today?

Mental Propositions and the Universals Analogy
Ghazali defends mental propositions via universals analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the example of universals like 'color-ness' to defend the validity of mental propositions for possibility, necessity, and impossibility.

Core Idea
Mental propositions are valid even without external objects, just like universal concepts.

In this passage, Ghazali responds to a key objection against reducing the three rational judgments to mental propositions. The objection is that knowledge requires a known, so mental propositions would lack an object. Ghazali turns to the philosophers' own admission that universals exist only in the mind, not externally, to show that mental propositions are perfectly valid. This lesson unpacks his argument using the example of 'color-ness'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and the response
Read the passage carefully to understand the objection: that knowledge requires a known object. Note how Ghazali immediately responds by asserting that reducing the three to mental propositions is correct.
The objection is implicit in the phrase 'knowledge requires a known'.
2
Understand the analogy with universals
Focus on the example of 'color-ness' (al-lawniyya). Notice that it is a universal concept that exists in the mind but has no external instance that is pure color without specific hue. This demonstrates that mental propositions can be meaningful without external referents.
The philosophers themselves agree that universals exist only in the mind, not in external individuals.
3
Apply the analogy to the three judgments
Ghazali concludes that if the universals analogy holds, then the same applies to possibility, necessity, and impossibility. They are mental propositions abstracted from particular instances, not requiring external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أن رد الإمكان والوجوب والامتناع ، إلى قضايا عقلية، صحيح. وما ذكروه من أن معنى قضاء العقل علمه ، والعلم يستدعى معلوماً ، فنقول له : معلوم كما أن اللونية والحيوانية وسائر القضايا الكلية، ثابتة" في العقل عندهم، وهى علوم لا يقال لا معلوم لها ، ولكن لا وجود لمعلوماتها في الأعيان ، حتى صرح الفلاسفة بأن الكليات موجودة في الأذهان لا في الأعيان ، وإنما الموجود في الأعيان جزئيات شخصية ، وهى محسوسة غير معقولة، ولكنها سبب لأن ينتزع العقل منها قضية مجردة عن المادة عقلية ، فإذن اللونية قضية مفردة في العقل سوى السوادية والبياضية ، ولا يتصور فى الوجود لون ، ليس بسواد ولا بياض ولا غيره الألوان ، وتثبت فى العقل صورة اللونية من غير تفصيل ، ويقال : هي من صورة وجودها في الأذهان لا فى الأعيان ، فإن لم يمتنع هذا لم يمتنع ما ذكرناه
The answer: that reducing possibility, necessity, and impossibility to mental propositions is correct. As for their statement that the meaning of the judgment of the intellect is its knowledge, and knowledge requires a known, we say to them: it is known just as 'color-ness' and 'animal-ness' and all universal propositions are established in the mind according to them; they are knowledge of which it is not said that they have no known, but their knowns have no existence in external individuals. Indeed, the philosophers have stated that universals exist in the minds, not in external individuals; what exists in external individuals are particular individuals, which are sensed, not intelligible, but they are the cause for the intellect to abstract from them a proposition free of matter, intellectual. So then 'color-ness' is a single proposition in the mind apart from 'black-ness' and 'white-ness'; and in existence, a color that is neither black nor white nor any other color is inconceivable. And the image of 'color-ness' is established in the mind without detail, and it is said: it is among those whose existence is in the minds, not in external individuals. So if this is not impossible, then what we have mentioned is not impossible.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection. Ghazali defends the position that the three attributes (imkan, wujub, imtina') can be reduced to mental propositions (qadaya 'aqliyya). The objection had been: if the judgment of the intellect is knowledge, and knowledge requires a known object, then these propositions would have no known object if they are merely mental. Ghazali replies using the example of universals: concepts like 'color-ness' (al-lawniyya) and 'animal-ness' (al-hayawaniyya) exist in the mind as universal propositions, yet their knowns (the universal natures) have no existence in external individuals. The philosophers themselves affirm that universals exist only in the mind, not in the external world. Externally, only particular individuals exist which are sensed. The intellect abstracts from them a universal concept. Thus 'color-ness' as a concept in the mind is distinct from 'black-ness' or 'white-ness'; externally, no color exists that is not a specific color. If this is possible, then the same applies to possibility, necessity, and impossibility as mental propositions with no corresponding external individuals.

Key Terms
قضايا عقلية
اللونية
الكلية
الأذهان
الأعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A mental judgment must have a corresponding external object to be valid.
Correction: Ghazali shows that universal concepts like 'color-ness' exist in the mind without external instances, so the three rational judgments can also be mental propositions.

Checkpoint

B) The concept of color-ness
C) The concept of possibility
D) The concept of the soul

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends the validity of mental propositions for possibility, necessity, and impossibility by analogy with universals like 'color-ness' that exist only in the mind.

?
The philosophers agree that universals exist only in the mind. This means that many of your most important concepts (justice, mercy, necessity) are mental. Are you willing to let your mind be the final authority for what is true about the unseen, or do you still seek external validation? The Parallel Between Possibility and Universals
Ghazali equates possibility with universals regarding mind-independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali turns the philosophers' objection about possibility against them by showing its parallel with universal propositions.

Core Idea
If universals are merely mental, then so is possibility.

In this passage, Ghazali responds to the claim that possibility would not cease even if no rational minds existed. He cleverly compares possibility to universal propositions (genera and species) and forces the philosophers to admit inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an objection, a counter-question, two possible replies, and a conclusion.
Pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers argue that possibility would not cease even if rational beings were absent or heedless. This is an objection to Ghazali's view that possibility is mental.
3
Trace Ghazali's counter-argument
Ghazali asks: if rational beings were absent, would universal propositions (genera and species) also cease? He then presents two possible answers from the philosophers and shows that both lead to the same conclusion about possibility.
This is a dilemma: either universals are merely mental or they exist in God's knowledge.
4
Understand the entailment
If the philosophers say universals cease (because they are mental), then the same applies to possibility. If they say universals remain in God's knowledge, then possibility likewise can remain. Thus possibility is parallel to universals.
Ghazali's point is they cannot inconsistently treat the two differently.
5
Grasp the purpose
The goal is to demonstrate the inconsistency in the philosophers' speech. They cannot claim possibility is mind-independent while universals are not.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم : لو قدر عدم العقلاء أو غفلتهم ما كان الإمكان ينعدم ، فنقول ولو قدر عدمهم ، هل كانت القضايا الكلية وهى الأجناس والأنواع تنعدم ؟ ! فإذا قالوا : نعم، إذ لا معنى لها إلا قضية في العقول، فكذلك قولنا في الإمكان، ولا فرق بين البابين ، وإن زعموا أنها تكون باقية في علم الله سبحانه ، فكذا القول في الإمكان ، فالإلزام واقع ، والمقصود إظهار تناقض كلامهم
As for their statement: 'If rational beings were supposed to be non-existent or heedless, possibility would not cease,' we say: and if they were non-existent, would the universal propositions—namely the genera and species—cease? If they say 'Yes, for they have no meaning except a proposition in minds,' then the same applies to our statement about possibility; there is no difference between the two cases. And if they claim that they remain in the knowledge of God, the Exalted, then the same applies to possibility. So the entailment is established, and the purpose is to demonstrate the inconsistency of their speech.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection: the philosophers argue that even if rational beings were absent or heedless, possibility would not cease, implying that possibility is not merely mental. Ghazali turns the argument against them: if rational beings were non-existent, would the universal propositions (like genera and species) cease? If the philosophers admit that universals are merely mental and would cease, then the same applies to possibility. If they insist that universals remain in God's knowledge, then the same can be said for possibility. Thus Ghazali demonstrates an inconsistency in their position: they cannot consistently deny mental status to possibility while affirming it for universals. The purpose is to show the contradiction in their speech.

Key Terms
القضايا الكلية
الأجناس
الأنواع
التناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is mind-independent while universals are not.
Correction: Ghazali shows they must be treated consistently: both are either mental or both are in God's knowledge.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali respond to the claim that possibility would not cease if rational beings were absent?

C) He denies that possibility is real.
D) He argues that rational beings are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that possibility and universals must be treated equally, exposing inconsistency in the philosophers' stance.

Universals and Possibility: Defending the Analogy
Ghazali argues that possibility is a mental universal like color-ness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the philosophers' own admission about universals to argue that possibility is a mental concept, thereby defending his analogy.

Core Idea
Possibility is a mental universal, just like color-ness.

Ghazali faces an objection: if possibility depends on the mind, what if all minds ceased? He cleverly turns this against the philosophers by pointing out that they already admit universals like color-ness exist only in the mind. In this passage, he argues that possibility is exactly analogous—a universal concept that remains in God's knowledge if minds disappear. This lesson unpacks his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' view on universals
Read the first part of the passage where Ghazali states that universals (color-ness, animal-ness) are established in the mind, and their objects do not exist externally. This is a key premise the philosophers accept.
Notice the phrase 'موجودة في الأذهان لا في الأعيان'.
2
Examine the example of color-ness
Focus on the specific example: color-ness is a single mental concept apart from blackness or whiteness, but no color exists in reality without being a specific color. This shows how a universal can be real in the mind without external instantiation.
Ghazali uses this to build his analogy.
3
Apply the analogy to possibility
Consider how possibility (imkan) is similar: it is a universal concept that applies to contingent things. Just as color-ness is abstracted from particular colors, possibility is abstracted from particular contingent things.
4
Address the objection about the absence of minds
Read Ghazali's response: If the philosophers claim universals would cease if minds vanish, then possibility would also cease. If they claim universals remain in God's knowledge, then so does possibility. Either way, their objection is inconsistent.
This is the crux of the refutation.
5
Recognize the purpose
Understand that Ghazali's goal is to expose a contradiction in the philosophers' own position, not to deny the reality of possibility. He uses their own principles against them.
The phrase 'إظهار تناقض كلامهم' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معلوم كما أن اللونية والحيوانية وسائر القضايا الكلية ثابتة في العقل عندهم، وهي علوم لا يقال لا معلوم لها، ولكن لا وجود لمعلوماتها في الأعيان، حتى صرح الفلاسفة بأن الكليات موجودة في الأذهان لا في الأعيان، وإنما الموجود في الأعيان جزئيات شخصية، وهي محسوسة غير معقولة، ولكنها سبب لأن ينتزع العقل منها قضية مجردة عن المادة عقلية، فإذن اللونية قضية مفردة في العقل سوى السوادية والبياضية، ولا يتصور في الوجود لون ليس بسواد ولا بياض ولا غيره الألوان، وتثبت في العقل صورة اللونية من غير تفصيل، ويقال: هي من صورة وجودها في الأذهان لا في الأعيان، فإن لم يمتنع هذا لم يمتنع ما ذكرناه. وأما قولهم: لو قدر عدم العقلاء أو غفلتهم ما كان الإمكان ينعدم، فنقول ولو قدر عدمهم، هل كانت القضايا الكلية وهي الأجناس والأنواع تنعدم؟! فإذا قالوا: نعم، إذ لا معنى لها إلا قضية في العقول، فكذلك قولنا في الإمكان، ولا فرق بين البابين، وإن زعموا أنها تكون باقية في علم الله سبحانه، فكذا القول في الإمكان، فالإلزام واقع، والمقصود إظهار تناقض كلامهم.
It is known that color-ness and animal-ness and all universal propositions are established in the intellect according to them, and they are sciences of which it is not said that they have no object, but their objects have no existence in external things, until the philosophers declared that universals exist in minds not in external things. Rather, what exists in external things are particular individuals, and they are sensible, not intelligible, but they are a cause for the intellect to extract from them an intellectual proposition abstracted from matter. So then, color-ness is a single proposition in the intellect apart from blackness and whiteness, and it is not conceivable in existence a color that is neither black nor white nor any other color. And the form of color-ness is established in the intellect without detail. And it is said: it is among the forms whose existence is in minds, not in external things. If this is not impossible, then what we have mentioned is not impossible. As for their saying: 'If the absence or heedlessness of intellects is supposed, would not possibility cease to exist?' We say: If their absence is supposed, would the universal propositions, which are the genera and species, cease to exist? If they say 'Yes, for they have no meaning except as a proposition in intellects,' then likewise our statement concerning possibility, and there is no difference between the two cases. And if they claim that they remain in the knowledge of God, then the same is said about possibility. So the objection stands, and the purpose is to show the contradiction in their speech.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by analogy. Ghazali first establishes that universals (kulliyat) such as 'color-ness' exist only in the mind, not in external reality—a point the philosophers themselves admit. Then he argues by parity that possibility (imkan), the modal status of a contingent thing, is also a universal concept existing in the mind. Therefore, if the philosophers object that possibility would vanish if all minds ceased, Ghazali responds: the same would apply to all universals, which they admit persist in God's knowledge. Thus their objection is self-contradictory. The purpose is to expose the inconsistency of their position.

Key Terms
قضايا كلية (qadaya kulliyya)
لونية (lawniyya)
إمكان (imkan)
كليات (kulliyat)
جزئيات (juz'iyyat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ghazali is denying the reality of possibility; rather he argues it is a mental universal, not a concrete entity.
Correction: Ghazali does not claim possibility is unreal; he claims it is a universal mental concept analogous to 'color-ness', which the philosophers accept as real in the mind but not externally.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of Ghazali's analogy between universals and possibility?
A) To prove that universals exist in the external world.
B) To show that possibility is a mental concept like universals.
C) To argue that possibility depends on human minds.
D) To refute the existence of God.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's argument shows that possibility is a universal concept existing in the mind and not dependent on the existence of concrete entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you judge something as 'possible' or 'impossible', do you treat that judgment as a fact about the world out there, or as a concept in your mind? How does that distinction affect the certainty you feel in making that judgment?
?

?
Ghazali shows that philosophers accept universals like 'color-ness' exist only in minds, but they object to possibility being the same. Are you ever inconsistent like that—accepting a principle in one area but denying it in another when it's uncomfortable? What would it cost you to be consistent?

The Meaning of 'Pure Intellect' and Begging the Question
Ghazali questions the definition of 'pure intellect' and exposes a begging-the-question fallacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the philosophers' claim that God is a 'pure intellect' does not automatically prove He knows all things.

Core Idea
Defining 'pure intellect' as knowledge of all things simply assumes the point at issue.

Ghazali begins his refutation by accepting the philosophers' premise that God is not in matter, but he immediately challenges the next step. By questioning what 'pure intellect' means, he exposes a hidden assumption: either the term already contains the conclusion, or it requires additional proof.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's concession and question
Notice that Ghazali first concedes that God is not in matter as the philosophers claim. Then he asks what 'pure intellect' means. Read carefully to see the two possible interpretations he presents.
2
Identify the fallacy of begging the question
If 'pure intellect' means 'knows all things', then the philosophers are using the conclusion as a premise. Ghazali points this out. Reflect on why this makes their argument circular.
3
Consider Ibn Sina's unique claim
Ghazali notes that even if 'pure intellect' means 'knows itself', the inference to knowledge of others is not necessary and was only held by Ibn Sina. Ask yourself: why does this weaken the argument?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : قولكم : إن الأول موجود لا فى مادة ، إن كان المعنى به ، أنه ليس بجسم ولا منطبع في جسم ، بل هو قائم بنفسه ، من غير تحيز ، واختصاص بجهة ؛ فهو مسلم . فيبقى قولكم : وما هذه صفته ، فهو عقل مجرد ، فماذا يعنى بالعقل ؟ ! إن عنيتم به ما يعقل سائر الأشياء ، فهو نفس المطلوب ، وموضع النزاع ؛ فكيف أخذته في مقدمات قياس المطلوب ؟! و إن عنيتم به غيره ، وهو يعقل نفسه ، فربما يسلم لك إخوانك من الفلاسفة ذلك ؛ ولكن يرجع حاصله ، إلى أن ما يعقل نفسه ، يعقل غيره ؛ فيقال : ولم ادعيت هذا ؟ ! وليس ذلك بضروری ؛ وقد انفرد به ابن سينا عن سائر الفلاسفة ، فكيف تدعيه ضرورياً ؟ ! و إن كان نظريا ، فما البرهان عليه ؟ ! فإن قيل : لأن المانع من إدراك الأشياء ، المادة ، ولا مادة . فنقول : نسلَّم أنها مانع ، ولا نسلم أنها المانع فقط، وينتظم قياسها ، على شكل القياس الشرطي وهو أن يقال : إن كان هذا في مادة ، فهو لا يعقل الأشياء ، ولكنه ليس فى المادة ، فإذن يعقل الأشياء ؛ فهذا استثناء نقيض المقدم واستثناء نقيض المقدم غير منتج بالاتفاق ؛ وهو كقول القائل : إن كان هذا إنساناً ، فهو حيوان ، لكنه ليس بإنسان ، فإذن ليس بحيوان ؛ وهذا لا يلزم ، إذ ربما لا يكون إنساناً ، ويكون فرساً ، فيكون حيواناً ، نعم استثناء نقيض المقدم ، ينتج نقيض التالى ، على ما ذكر في المنطق ، بشرط ، هو ثبوت انعكاس التالى على المقدم وذلك بالحصر ، وهو كقولهم إن كانت الشمس طالعة ، فالنهار موجود ، لكن الشمس ليست بطالعة ، فالنهار غير موجود ، لأن وجود النهار لا سبب له سوى طلوع الشمس ، فكان أحدهما منعكساً على الآخر وبيان هذه الأوضاع والألفاظ ، يفهم في كتاب « معيار العلم » الذي صنفناه مضموماً إلى هذا الكتاب فإن قيل : نحن ندعى التعاكس ، وهو أن المانع محصور في المادة ، فلا مانع سواها قلنا : هذا تحكم ، فما الدليل عليه ؟ !
We say: Your statement that the First exists not in matter—if by that is meant that He is not a body nor impressed in a body, but is self-subsistent without occupying space and without being confined to a direction—that is conceded. Then your statement remains: 'Whatever has this attribute is a pure intellect.' So what is meant by 'intellect'? If you mean by it that which knows all other things, that is the very thing being sought and the point of dispute; so how did you take it as a premise in your syllogism for what is sought? And if you mean by it something else, namely that He knows Himself, then perhaps your fellow philosophers would concede that to you; but the upshot of it is that what knows itself knows others. So it is said: Why do you claim this? That is not necessary. Ibn Sina was alone among the philosophers in this; so how can you claim it as necessary? And if it is theoretical, what is the proof for it? If it is said: Because the only obstacle to apprehending things is matter, and there is no matter. We say: We concede that it is an obstacle, but we do not concede that it is the only obstacle. And your syllogism takes the form of a conditional syllogism: namely, one says: 'If this is in matter, it does not apprehend things; but it is not in matter, therefore it apprehends things.' This is an exception of the negation of the antecedent, and the exception of the negation of the antecedent is not productive by consensus. It is like one who says: 'If this is a human, it is an animal; but it is not a human, therefore it is not an animal.' This does not follow, for it might not be a human but be a horse, and thus be an animal. Indeed, the exception of the negation of the antecedent produces the negation of the consequent, as mentioned in logic, under the condition that the consequent is convertible with the antecedent, that is, by exclusive limitation. It is like their saying: 'If the sun has risen, then daytime exists; but the sun has not risen, therefore daytime does not exist,' because daytime has no cause other than sunrise; thus one of them is convertible with the other. The explanation of these conventions and expressions is understood in the book 'Mi'yār al-ʿIlm' which we have composed as an appendix to this book. If it is said: We claim conversion, i.e., that the obstacle is confined to matter, there being no obstacle other than it. We say: This is arbitrary, so what is the proof for it?
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the philosophers' argument that God, being immaterial, must know all things. Ghazali begins by accepting that God is not in matter in the sense of not being a body. Then he questions what is meant by 'pure intellect.' If it means He knows all things, that is the very point under dispute and cannot be assumed. If it means He knows Himself, that may be granted by some philosophers, but it does not logically entail knowledge of others; this was a view unique to Ibn Sina and is not necessarily true. Ghazali then analyzes the logical form of their argument: they claim that if something is in matter, it does not know; God is not in matter; therefore He knows. This is a conditional syllogism with an exception of the negation of the antecedent, which is invalid unless the consequent is convertible (i.e., the only cause of knowledge absence is matter). Ghazali gives examples to show the invalidity. He then notes that the philosophers might claim conversion, but this is an arbitrary assertion without proof.

Key Terms
عقل مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if God is not in matter, He must automatically know all things because He is a 'pure intellect'.
Correction: The meaning of 'pure intellect' is ambiguous; if it means knowledge of all things, the claim is circular; if it means self-knowledge, the extension to knowledge of others requires proof.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali reject the interpretation that 'pure intellect' means 'knows all things'?
A) Because it contradicts revelation.
B) Because it is a necessary truth.
C) Because it assumes the very point that needs to be proven.
D) Because matter is not an obstacle to knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' use of 'pure intellect' either begs the question or relies on an unproven claim that self-knowledge implies knowledge of all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that because something seems logical to you, it must be true? Have you ever used a word like 'pure intellect' without defining it, just to win an argument? How will you guard against begging the question in your own reasoning about God?
?
When you claim that God knows everything, is that because you have a proof, or because it is comfortable? Would you be willing to hold your belief open to scrutiny, as Ghazali did with the philosophers?
?
Ghazali challenges even the revered philosophers. Is there any authority—a scholar, a teacher, your own reasoning—that you are afraid to question? Will you have the courage to examine even your most cherished beliefs for logical flaws?

The Logical Fallacy of the Conditional Syllogism
Ghazali analyzes the logical form and exposes the fallacy of negating the antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the philosophers' syllogism 'if in matter then not know, not in matter, therefore knows' is invalid unless the obstacle of matter is exclusive.

Core Idea
Denying the antecedent does not prove the consequent without exclusive causation.

After questioning the definition of 'pure intellect', Ghazali turns to the logical form of the philosophers' argument. He shows it is a conditional syllogism that commits a common fallacy: negating the antecedent. He explains that for such an inference to be valid, the consequent must be convertible—meaning matter must be the only possible obstacle to knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' syllogism
The philosophers argue: If something is in matter, it does not know; God is not in matter; therefore God knows all things. Write this out and identify the antecedent and consequent.
2
Learn the exception of the negation of the antecedent
Ghazali calls this 'استثناء نقيض المقدم' (excepting the negation of the antecedent). He gives an example: If it is a human, it is an animal; not a human; so not an animal. This is invalid because the consequent (animal) can exist without the antecedent (human). Notice the parallel.
3
Discover the condition of conversion (ta'ākus)
The syllogism only works if the only possible cause of the consequent is the antecedent. Ghazali gives the example of sunrise and daytime: if sun rises, daytime; no sunrise, no daytime—because only sunrise causes daytime. The philosophers must prove that matter is the only obstacle to knowledge.
4
Assess the philosophers' claim of conversion
At the end, Ghazali anticipates that the philosophers might claim conversion. He responds that this is arbitrary and demands proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter arguments about Allah's attributes, do you accept them simply because they sound logical, or do you seek to verify that no hidden fallacies undermine them? In your own study of Aqeedah, are you willing to wait for proof of exclusivity before accepting a conclusion, or do you rush to embrace comfortable ideas?
?

Have you ever assumed that because you removed one obstacle to drawing closer to Allah (like following a certain practice), you automatically achieve closeness? What other 'obstacles' might remain that you haven't considered? Commit today to examining your heart for hidden barriers.
?
The human/animal example shows that denying a condition does not negate the consequent. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes think, 'I am not in a state of sin, so I must be righteous'? Or 'I am not ignorant, so I must know the truth'? Reflect on a specific area where you might be committing this fallacy and resolve to seek genuine evidence of your state before Allah.

Defining the Impossible: Objection and Partial Reply
An objector asks for a definition of the impossible, and Ghazali begins to reply by linking it to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's challenge to define the impossible and the implications of defining it as logical contradiction.

Core Idea
The impossible is not merely what God does not do, but what entails contradiction.

In this passage, an objector agrees with Ghazali on the shared premise that everything possible is within God's power. However, he presses for a precise definition of the impossible, suggesting that it reduces to the conjunction of negation and affirmation. This sets the stage for a crucial discussion about the boundaries of divine power and logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objector's tone: he starts by establishing agreement before asking for a definition.
2
Identify the shared premise
The objector states that both sides agree: every possible is within God's power, and the impossible is not. Why is this premise important?
This premise is foundational to the debate.
3
Examine the proposed definition
The objector suggests defining the impossible as the conjunction of negation and affirmation. Consider why this definition might be problematic.
The text cuts off, but later reasoning would show that this definition is too broad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فنحن نساعدكم ، على أن كل ممكن مقدور لله تعالى ، وأنتم تساعدون على أن كل محال ، فليس بمقدور ، ومن الأشياء ما تعرف استحالته ، ومنها ما يعرف إمكانه ، ومنها ما يقف العقل عنده ، فلا يقضى فيه باستحالة ولا بإمكان . فالآن ما حد المحال عندكم ، فإن رجع إلى الجمع بين النفي والإثبات ، في شيء واحد ، فقولوا : إن كل شيئين ليس هذا ذاك ، ولا ذاك هذا ، فلا
If it is said: 'We agree with you that everything possible is within the power of God, may He be exalted, and you agree that everything impossible is not within power. Among things, some are known to be impossible, some are known to be possible, and some the intellect stops at, not judging them as impossible or possible. So now, what is your definition (hadd) of the impossible? If it reduces to the conjunction of negation and affirmation in one thing, then say: that every two things such that this is not that and that is not this, then not...'
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a new objection and the beginning of a reply. The objector states a shared premise: everything possible is within God's power, and the impossible is not. Then the objector asks for a definition (hadd) of the impossible. He suggests a possible definition: that the impossible is the conjunction of negation and affirmation in one thing. Then he says: 'if that is the case, then say that every two things such that this is not that and that is not this, then not...' The text cuts off, but the implication is that this definition is too broad. The objection sets up a discussion about the limits of divine power and what constitutes the genuinely impossible.

Key Terms
ممكن (possible)
مقدور (within power)
محال (impossible)
حد (definition)
الجمع بين النفي والإثبات (conjunction of negation and affirmation)

What definition of the impossible does the objector suggest?

C) Whatever is not possible
D) Any two distinct things

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of something must be defined by logical contradiction, not by mere lack of divine action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is one area of your life where you have assumed something is 'impossible' for you to change, without checking whether it truly involves a logical contradiction? Today, commit to re-examining that assumption knowing that God's power extends to all that is possible.
?
Do you ever limit God by thinking He 'cannot' do something simply because it is difficult for you to imagine? How does this lesson's definition of impossibility as contradiction correct your mental picture of divine power?
?
When you pray for something, do you worry that it might be 'impossible' for God? Ponder on the difference between what is truly impossible (a contradiction) and what is merely unlikely. Spend five minutes listing things you have given up on that are actually possible, and ask God for them again.

Defining the Impossible: Ghazali's Reply to Absurdity Objections
Ghazali defines the impossible and defends divine power against absurdity objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of the impossible and how it refutes the objection that his view leads to absurdities.

Core Idea
The impossible is only logical self-contradiction, not what is contrary to natural custom.

A critic objects that if only logical contradictions are outside God's power, then many absurd consequences follow—like a dead person writing or substances turning into accidents. Ghazali responds by precisely defining the impossible as three types of contradiction. This lesson unpacks that definition and shows why the critic's examples fail, clarifying the scope of divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the full objection
Read the objection carefully to understand the critic's challenge. The critic agrees that only the impossible is beyond God's power but then lists examples that would become possible on Ghazali's view, leading to absurdities.
Notice the examples: a dead hand writing, will without knowledge, changing genera.
2
Identify Ghazali's three-part definition
Find the three categories of the impossible that Ghazali lists. They are: (1) affirming a thing with its negation, (2) affirming the specific while negating the general, and (3) affirming two while negating one.
This is the core of his answer.
3
See how Ghazali applies the definition
Ghazali explains why black and white cannot coexist: affirming black in a locus entails negating white. Similarly, being in one place entails not being elsewhere. These fit his definition.
These examples show the definition in action.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the critic's examples (e.g., a dead person writing) do not fall under this definition—they are not logically contradictory. Thus God can do them, even if they seem unnatural.
This preserves both God's power and rational coherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فنحن نساعدكم على أن كل ممكن مقدور لله تعالى وأنتم تساعدون على أن كل محال فليس بمقدور ومن الأشياء ما تعرف استحالته ومنها ما يعرف إمكانه ومنها ما يقف العقل عنده فلا يقضى فيه باستحالة ولا بإمكان . فالآن ما حد المحال عندكم فإن رجع إلى الجمع بين النفي والإثبات في شيء واحد فقولوا إن كل شيئين ليس هذا ذاك ولا ذاك هذا فلا يستدعى وجود أحدهما وجود الآخر وقولوا إن الله تعالى يقدر على خلق إرادة من غير علم بالمراد وخلق علم من غير حياة ويقدر على أن يحرك يد ميت ويقعده ويكتب بيده مجلدات ويتعاطى صناعات وهو مفتوح العين محدق بصره نحوه ولكنه لا يرى ولا حياة فيه ولا قدرة له عليه وإنما هذه الأفعال المنظومة يخلقها الله تعالى مع تحريك يده والحركة من جهة الله تعالى . وبتجويز هذا يبطل الفرق بين الحركة الاختيارية وبين الرعدة فلا يدل الفعل المحكم على العلم ولا على قدرة الفاعل . وينبغي أن يقدر على قلب الأجناس فيقلب الجوهر عرضاً ويقلب العلم قدرة والسواد بياضاً والصوت رائحة كما اقتدر على قلب الجماد حيواناً والحجر ذهباً ويلزم عليه أيضاً من المحالات ما لا حصر له . والجواب : أن المحال غير مقدور عليه والمحال إثبات الشيء مع نفيه أو إثبات الأخص مع نفي الأعم أو إثبات الاثنين مع نفي الواحد وما لا يرجع إلى هذا فليس بمحال وما ليس بمحال فهو مقدور . أما الجمع بين السواد والبياض فمحال لأنا نفهم من إثبات صورة السواد في المحل نفي ماهية البياض ووجود السواد فإذا صار نفي البياض مفهوماً من إثبات السواد كان إثبات البياض مع نفيه محالاً . وإنما لا يجوز كون الشخص الواحد في مكانين لأنا نفهم من كونه في البيت عدم كونه في غير البيت فلا يمكن تقديره في غير البيت كونه في البيت المفهم لنفيه عن غير البيت
If it is said: 'We agree with you that every possible is within God's power, and you agree that every impossible is not within power. Among things, some are known to be impossible, some known to be possible, and some the intellect stops at, not judging them impossible or possible. Now, what is your definition of the impossible? If it reduces to joining affirmation and negation of one thing, then say: every two things that are not this that nor that this [do not entail each other]. And say that God is able to create will without knowledge of the willed, and create knowledge without life, and He is able to move a dead hand, make it sit, write volumes with it, and engage in crafts while it is open-eyed staring but does not see, and there is no life or power in it. These well-ordered acts are created by God with the movement of the hand, and the movement is from God. By permitting this, the distinction between voluntary movement and tremor disappears, so a well-ordered act does not indicate knowledge or power of the agent. He should also be able to change genera, turning substance into accident, knowledge into power, black into white, sound into smell, just as He turned a lifeless thing into an animal and stone into gold. This entails endless impossibilities.' The answer: The impossible is not within power. The impossible is affirming a thing with its negation, or affirming the specific while negating the general, or affirming two while negating one. Whatever does not reduce to this is not impossible, and whatever is not impossible is within power. As for joining black and white, it is impossible because from affirming the form of black in a locus we understand the negation of the essence of white. Thus when the negation of white is understood from affirming black, affirming white with its negation is impossible. A single person cannot be in two places because from his being in the house we understand his not being elsewhere. Thus it cannot be supposed that he is in the house while being not in the house, which implies his negation elsewhere.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer debate. The objector first establishes agreement that only the impossible is outside God's power, then asks for a definition of the impossible. He suggests that if it is merely self-contradiction, then many things that Ghazali would consider possible (like creating will without knowledge, or moving a corpse in an orderly manner) would become possible, thereby erasing the distinction between voluntary and involuntary movement. This would undermine using ordered actions as evidence of an agent's knowledge and power. Furthermore, he argues it would allow changing genera, leading to countless impossibilities. Ghazali's answer defines the impossible precisely as: (1) affirming a thing with its negation, (2) affirming the specific while negating the general, or (3) affirming two while negating one. Anything not falling under these is possible. He illustrates with black and white: affirming black in a locus necessarily negates white, so affirming both is impossible. Similarly, being in one place negates being elsewhere. Thus the examples given by the objector (will without knowledge, etc.) are not inherently impossible because they do not entail self-contradiction; they are merely unusual. Ghazali's point is that God's power is not limited by natural regularities but only by logical absurdity. The objection's force is thus deflected by clarifying that the impossible is logically defined, not empirically defined.

Key Terms
محال
ممكن
نفي
إثبات
خاص
عام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali's definition of the impossible is too narrow, allowing absurdities like a dead person writing.
Correction: Ghazali's definition only excludes logical contradictions, not empirical impossibilities. A dead person writing is not logically contradictory, so God can do it; it only seems absurd due to our experience of natural regularities.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's answer, what is the definition of the impossible?
A) Whatever is not customary in nature is impossible
B) Affirming a thing with its negation, affirming the specific while negating the general, or affirming two while negating one
C) Anything that the intellect cannot conceive
D) Any action that does not indicate knowledge in the agent

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's precise definition of the impossible as logical contradiction refutes objections and preserves God's power over all that is non-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that something is 'impossible' simply because it defies your experience or expectations, rather than because it is truly contradictory? When have you done that in your understanding of God's power?
?
If you truly believed that God can do anything that is not logically contradictory, how would that change the way you pray or ask for help in seemingly hopeless situations?
?
The objection in this passage assumed that without a living agent, orderly actions cannot happen. Where in your life do you assume that God cannot bring about order without natural means? Will you ask Him directly today for something that seems 'impossible' but is not contradictory?

The Two Premises and the First Objection
Ghazali enumerates two premises and states the first objection from the mutakallimun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two premises about divisibility and the first objection from the mutakallimun that knowledge resides in an indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Knowledge is indivisible, but the atomists claim it inhabits an indivisible atom.

This lesson lays the foundation of Ghazali's critique of the philosophers. He states two premises establishing that knowledge cannot be divided, then presents the first objection from the mutakallimun who assert that the locus of knowledge is an indivisible atom. This sets up the debate over whether knowledge can reside in a simple substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the premises carefully
Identify the two premises: the first about states in something divisible, and the second about the indivisibility of a single knowledge. Notice how Ghazali argues that knowledge cannot have parts.
2
Examine the objection
The mutakallimun object by proposing that the locus of knowledge could be an indivisible atom. Note that this is presented as a known doctrine, not a proof.
3
Reflect on the structure
Ghazali frames the objection as the first of two stages. This lesson covers only the statement of the objection.
The response will follow in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول، قولنا: إن كل حال في منقسم، ينقسم لا محالة بفرض القسمة في محله، وهو أولى لا يمكن التشكك فيه. والثاني، قولنا: إن العلم الواحد يحل في الآدمي، وهو لا ينقسم، لأنه لو انقسم إلى غير نهاية كان محالا، وإن كانت له نهاية فيشتمل على آحاد لا محالة لا تنقسم، وعلى الجملة نحن نعلم أشياء، ولا نقدر أن نفرض زوال بعضها وبقاء البعض، من حيث إنه لا بعض لها. والاعتراض على مقامين: المقام الأول: أن يقال، بم تنكرون على من يقول: محل العلم جوهر فرد متحيز لا ينقسم، وقد عرف هذا من مذهب المتكلمين. ولا يبقى بعده إلا الاستبعاد، وهو أنه كيف تحل العلوم كلها في جوهر فرد، وتكون جميع الجواهر المطيفة به معطلة وهي مجاورة؟ والاستبعاد لا خير فيه، إذ يتوجه على مذهبهم أيضاً، أنه كيف تكون النفس شيئاً واحداً لا يتحيز، ولا يشار إليه، ولا يكون داخل البدن، ولا خارجه، ولا متصلا بالجسم ولا منفصلا عنه؟ إلا أننا لا نؤثر هذا المقام، فإن القول في مسألة الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ طويل، ولهم فيه أدلة هندسية يطول الكلام عليها، ومن جملتها قولهم: جوهر فرد، بين جوهرين، هل يلاقي أحد الطرفين منه عين ما يلاقيه الآخر؟ أو غيره؟ فإن كان عينه فهو محال، إذ يلزم منه تلاقي الطرفين، فإن ملاقي الملاقي ملاق، وإن كان ما يلاقيه غيره، ففيه إثبات التعدد والانقسام، وهذه شبهة يطول حلها، وبنا غنية عن الخوض فيها، فلنعدل إلى مقام آخر.
The first: our statement: Every state existing in something divisible is necessarily divisible by positing division in its locus, and this is too obvious to be doubted. The second: our statement: A single knowledge (al-ilm al-wahid) subsists in a human being, and it is not divisible, because if it were divisible ad infinitum it would be impossible, and if it had an end it would necessarily contain indivisible units. In sum, we know things, but we cannot suppose the removal of some of them while others remain, because they have no parts. The objection is in two stages: The first stage: It may be said: Why do you deny those who say that the locus of knowledge is an individual atom (jawhar fard mutahayyiz) that is not divisible? This is known from the doctrine of the mutakallimun. Nothing remains after this except the argument of improbability (isti'bad), namely: How can all sciences subsist in a single atom, while all the surrounding atoms be free of them and unoccupied? But improbability is of no avail, for their own doctrine also faces the objection: How can the soul be a single entity that is neither located, nor pointed to, nor inside the body, nor outside it, nor connected to the body, nor separate from it? However, we do not prefer this stage, for the discussion about the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza') is lengthy, and they have geometrical proofs that would take long to discuss. Among them is their statement: A single atom between two atoms: does one of its two sides exactly meet what the other side meets, or something else? If it is the same, it is impossible, because it would entail that the two sides meet—since whatever meets what meets something is itself a meet-er. If what it meets is something else, then that entails multiplicity and divisibility. This is a confusing argument whose resolution would take long, and we have no need to delve into it. So let us turn to another stage.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents two premises of a syllogism about the indivisibility of knowledge, then introduces the first objection from the mutakallimun regarding the indivisible atom. Ghazali shows that the objection relies on an improbability argument (isti'bad) which can be turned back against the philosophers' own doctrine of the soul. He then declines to pursue this line because the issue of the indivisible part is complex and would require lengthy geometrical proofs. He gives one example of such a proof regarding an atom between two atoms, but says it is not necessary for his current argument. So he moves on to the second stage.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد (jawhar fard)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the second premise that Ghazali states before the objection?
A) Knowledge is divisible into infinite parts.
B) A single knowledge in a human is not divisible.
C) The locus of knowledge is an indivisible atom.
D) All things are divisible.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali establishes that knowledge is indivisible and then presents the objection that its locus could be an indivisible atom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to possess knowledge of the Creator—the most important knowledge you have. If this knowledge is indivisible as Ghazali argues, does that mean you cannot lose any part of it without losing the whole? Examine your own heart: is your conviction whole, or are there doubts you have compartmentalized? What would it cost you to be honest about those doubts today?
?
Ghazali presents the first premise: attributes in divisible objects are divisible. Your body is divisible—your cells, your limbs. Yet you experience the unity of your consciousness. Do you truly believe your consciousness is a product of divisible matter, or does this argument point to something within you that must be indivisible? How does the answer affect your daily actions and your sense of accountability to the One who created that indivisible essence?

Ghazali's Response and Strategic Bypass
Ghazali critiques the improbability argument and decides to bypass lengthy geometrical discussions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the improbability argument and his decision to bypass the lengthy discussion of the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
The improbability argument cuts both ways, so Ghazali chooses brevity over geometrical complexity.

Ghazali now responds to the first objection. He argues that the improbability (isti'bad) of knowledge residing in an atom is not a strong refutation, because it can be turned back against the philosophers' own view of the soul. He then declines to pursue the matter further, noting that the indivisible atom debate involves long geometrical proofs, and he would rather move on to a different stage.

Guided Path
1
Read the response carefully
Notice how Ghazali dismisses the improbability argument as weak, then turns it against the philosophers' concept of the soul.
2
Understand the counter-argument
The philosophers' own doctrine of the soul faces a similar improbability: a non-spatial entity that is neither inside nor outside the body. This shows the argument from improbability is not decisive.
3
See why Ghazali moves on
He acknowledges that the indivisible atom topic is lengthy and involves complex geometrical proofs. He gives one example but decides not to delve into it, as it is unnecessary for his present purpose.
This strategic move keeps the discussion focused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The first: our statement: Every state existing in something divisible is necessarily divisible by positing division in its locus, and this is too obvious to be doubted. The second: our statement: A single knowledge (al-ilm al-wahid) subsists in a human being, and it is not divisible, because if it were divisible ad infinitum it would be impossible, and if it had an end it would necessarily contain indivisible units. In sum, we know things, but we cannot suppose the removal of some of them while others remain, because they have no parts. The objection is in two stages: The first stage: It may be said: Why do you deny those who say that the locus of knowledge is an individual atom (jawhar fard mutahayyiz) that is not divisible? This is known from the doctrine of the mutakallimun. Nothing remains after this except the argument of improbability (isti'bad), namely: How can all sciences subsist in a single atom, while all the surrounding atoms be free of them and unoccupied? But improbability is of no avail, for their own doctrine also faces the objection: How can the soul be a single entity that is neither located, nor pointed to, nor inside the body, nor outside it, nor connected to the body, nor separate from it? However, we do not prefer this stage, for the discussion about the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza') is lengthy, and they have geometrical proofs that would take long to discuss. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali rejects the indivisible atom outright.
Correction: Ghazali does not reject it; he chooses not to engage with the intricate proofs, bypassing the issue for brevity.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali decide to move on from the first objection?
A) Because it is impossible to refute
B) Because the discussion of the indivisible atom is lengthy and unnecessary
C) Because he agrees with the mutakallimun
D) Because it is invalid

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the improbability argument is weak and can be turned against the philosophers, then chooses to avoid lengthy geometrical proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult objection to your beliefs, do you tend to get lost in endless debates, or do you step back and consider whether the objection even matters? What would it take for you to follow Ghazali's example and 'bypass' a distracting argument for a more fruitful approach?
?
Ghazali turned the improbability argument against the philosophers by pointing to the soul. Are there criticisms you level at others that could just as easily apply to your own worldview? When have you used a double standard out of convenience rather than truth?
?
The philosophers' geometrical proof about the atom seems intricate and impressive, yet Ghazali deemed it unnecessary to resolve. Are there intellectual puzzles you cling to as a way to avoid confronting simpler, more uncomfortable spiritual truths? What might you be hiding from by over-analyzing?

The Indivisible Meaning: Sheep's Perception Refutes Divisibility
Ghazali presents the sheep's perception as a counterexample to the philosophers' divisibility claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the indivisible nature of perceived meanings like enmity to challenge the philosophers' claim that every state in a body must be divisible.

Core Idea
A single meaning like enmity has no parts, yet it is perceived by a corporeal power.

Ghazali begins his second objection by focusing on the philosophers' premise that every state in a body must be divisible. He points to the sheep's perception of the wolf's enmity—a single, indivisible meaning—as a counterexample. This passage sets up the core of his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Ghazali states the philosophers' claim and then introduces the sheep example.
2
Identify the two sides
Identify the philosophers' premise (all states in a body are divisible) and Ghazali's counterexample (sheep perceives an indivisible enmity). Reflect on why enmity cannot have parts.
Enmity is a 'meaning' (ma'na) that is not conditioned to be in matter.
3
Understand the implication
Ghazali says the philosophers cannot posit division in these meanings. Ask yourself: why does this refute their claim?
The philosophers hold that animal souls are corporeal, so if enmity is indivisible, their premise fails.
