المقام الثاني، أن نقول: ما زعمتموه من أن كل حال في جسم، فينبغي أن ينقسم، باطل عليكم بما تدركه القوة من الشاة حكم شيء واحد، لا يتصور تقسيمه، إذ ليس للعداوة بعض، حتى يقدر إدراك بعضه، وزوال بعضه، وقد حصل إدراكها في قوة جسمانية عندكم، فإن نفس البهائم منطبعة في الأجسام، لا تبقى بعد الموت، وقد اتفقوا عليه، فإن أمكنهم أن يتكلفوا تقدير الانقسام في المدركات بالحواس الخمس، وبالحس المشترك وبالقوة الحافظة للصور، فلا يمكنهم تقدير الانقسام في هذه المعاني، التي ليس من شرطها أن تكون في مادة. فإن قيل: الشاة لا تدرك العداوة المطلقة المجردة عن المادة، بل تدرك عداوة الذئب المعين المشخص، مقروناً بشخصه وهيكله، والقوة العاقلة تدرك الحقائق مجردة عن المواد والأشخاص. قلنا: الشاة قد أدركت لون الذئب وشكله، ثم عداوته، فإن كان اللون ينطبع في القوة الباصرة، وكذا الشكل، وينقسم بانقسام محل البصر، فالعداوة بماذا تدركها؟ فإن أدركتها بجسم، فلينقسم، وليت شعري ما حال ذلك الإدراك إذا قسم؟! وكيف يكون بعضه؟! أهو إدراك لبعض العداوة؟! فكيف يكون لها بعض؟! أو كل قسم أدرك كل العداوة؟! فتكون العداوة معلومة مراراً، بثبوت إدراكها في كل قسم من أقسام المحل. فإذن هذه شبهة مشككة لهم في برهانهم، فلابد من الحل. فإن قيل: هذه مناقضة في المعقولات، والمعقولات لا تنقض، فإنكم مهما لم تقدروا على الشك في المقدمتين، وهو أن العلم الواحد لا ينقسم، وأن ما لا ينقسم لا يقوم بجسم منقسم، لم يمكنكم الشك في النتيجة. والجواب، أن هذا الكتاب ما صنفناه إلا لبيان التهافت والتناقض في كلام الفلاسفة، وقد حصل، إذ انتفض به أحد الأمرين: إما ما ذكروه في النفس الناطقة، وإما ما ذكروه في القوة الوهمية.
The second stage: We say: What you have claimed—that every state in a body must be divisible—is refuted by what the power of the sheep perceives: a single judgment (hukm) that cannot be divided, since enmity has no parts such that one could conceive of perceiving part of it and the other part ceasing. And this perception occurs, according to you, in a corporeal power, for the soul of animals is imprinted in bodies and does not survive after death, and they have agreed on this. So if they can attempt to posit division in the things perceived by the five senses, by the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak), and by the power that preserves images (al-quwwa al-hafiza li-s-suwar), they cannot posit division in these meanings (al-ma'ani) that are not conditioned to be in matter. If it is said: The sheep does not perceive absolute enmity stripped of matter, but rather perceives the enmity of a specific, particular wolf, conjoined with its person and form; and the rational power perceives realities stripped of matter and individuals. We say: The sheep has perceived the color of the wolf and its form, then its enmity. So if color is imprinted in the power of sight, and likewise the form, and they are divided by the division of the locus of sight, then by what does it perceive the enmity? If it perceives it by a body, then let it be divided—and I wonder what would be the state of that perception if it is divided? How would some of it be? Would it be perception of some of the enmity? But how can enmity have parts? Or would each division perceive the entire enmity? Then enmity would be known repeatedly, given that its perception subsists in every part of the locus. So this is a confusing doubt (shubha mushakkika) for them concerning their proof, and a solution is necessary. If it is said: This is a contradiction in the intelligibles (al-ma'qulat), and intelligibles are not contradicted, for as long as you cannot doubt the two premises—that a single knowledge is not divisible, and that what is not divisible cannot subsist in a divisible body—you cannot doubt the conclusion. The answer: This book was only composed to show the incoherence (al-tahafut) and contradiction (al-tanaqud) in the statements of the philosophers, and this has been achieved, for one of two things has been undermined by it: either what they have said about the rational soul, or what they have said about the estimative power (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya).
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' argument that every state in a body must be divisible. Ghazali uses the example of a sheep's perception of a wolf's enmity: enmity is a single meaning with no parts, yet it is perceived by an animal. Since the philosophers hold that animal souls are corporeal and imprinted in bodies, they must explain how such an indivisible meaning can be perceived in a divisible locus. Ghazali then handles an objection that the sheep perceives only particular enmity, not universal, but he counters by asking how the enmity is perceived along with color and shape. He concludes that this creates a doubt for the philosophers, and his purpose in the book is to show their incoherence. The final exchange dismisses the objection that the premises are certain, because Ghazali's goal is to show that at least one of their doctrines (about the rational soul or the estimative power) is undermined.

Key Terms
عداوة (adawa)
معاني (ma'ani)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what does the sheep's perception of the wolf's enmity demonstrate?
A) That enmity is divisible
B) That perception is incorporeal
C) That the philosophers' premise that all states in a body are divisible is false
D) That animals have rational souls

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' divisibility claim by showing that an indivisible meaning like enmity can be perceived by a corporeal power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The sheep instinctively perceives enmity without analysis. When you sense hostility or love from someone, do you trust that immediate perception, or do you demand further proof? What might you lose by always doubting your intuitive judgments?
?
If even a sheep can grasp an indivisible truth like enmity through a corporeal power, what does that say about your own soul's ability to perceive truths that go beyond physical division? What steps can you take today to become more receptive to such meanings?
?
Ghazali uses this example to show that rational claims can be overturned by simple, undeniable realities. Is there a belief you hold that you have never truly tested against experience? Will you allow reality to challenge your assumptions, even if it is uncomfortable?

The Objection of Particularity and the Doubt
Ghazali handles an objection and deepens the refutation, concluding with a doubt that undermines the philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali responds to the objection that the sheep perceives only particular enmity, and how he concludes that this creates a doubt in the philosophers' proof.

Core Idea
The sheep's perception of enmity, whether particular or absolute, remains a problem for the philosophers.

After presenting his counterexample, Ghazali anticipates an objection: the sheep perceives only the enmity of a specific wolf, not enmity in general. He then deepens his argument by asking how the enmity is perceived alongside color and shape, leading to a dilemma. This passage shows his method of creating doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage from 'fain qīla' onwards. Notice the objection that the sheep perceives particular enmity, and Ghazali's immediate response.
2
Analyze the dilemma
Ghazali asks: if the enmity is perceived by a body, then it must be divisible. But how? Either each part perceives a part of enmity (impossible, since enmity has no parts) or each part perceives the whole enmity (then enmity is known multiple times). Both are problematic.
This dilemma is the core of the doubt.
3
Understand the final exchange
The philosophers insist the premises are certain; Ghazali replies that his purpose is to show incoherence, and that at least one of their doctrines is undermined.
Note the reference to 'rational soul' and 'estimative power'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second stage: We say: What you have claimed—that every state in a body must be divisible—is refuted by what the power of the sheep perceives: a single judgment (hukm) that cannot be divided, since enmity has no parts such that one could conceive of perceiving part of it and the other part ceasing. And this perception occurs, according to you, in a corporeal power, for the soul of animals is imprinted in bodies and does not survive after death, and they have agreed on this. So if they can attempt to posit division in the things perceived by the five senses, by the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak), and by the power that preserves images (al-quwwa al-hafiza li-s-suwar), they cannot posit division in these meanings (al-ma'ani) that are not conditioned to be in matter. If it is said: The sheep does not perceive absolute enmity stripped of matter, but rather perceives the enmity of a specific, particular wolf, conjoined with its person and form; and the rational power perceives realities stripped of matter and individuals. We say: The sheep has perceived the color of the wolf and its form, then its enmity. So if color is imprinted in the power of sight, and likewise the form, and they are divided by the division of the locus of sight, then by what does it perceive the enmity? If it perceives it by a body, then let it be divided—and I wonder what would be the state of that perception if it is divided? How would some of it be? Would it be perception of some of the enmity? But how can enmity have parts? Or would each division perceive the entire enmity? Then enmity would be known repeatedly, given that its perception subsists in every part of the locus. So this is a confusing doubt (shubha mushakkika) for them concerning their proof, and a solution is necessary. If it is said: This is a contradiction in the intelligibles (al-ma'qulat), and intelligibles are not contradicted, for as long as you cannot doubt the two premises—that a single knowledge is not divisible, and that what is not divisible cannot subsist in a divisible body—you cannot doubt the conclusion. The answer: This book was only composed to show the incoherence (al-tahafut) and contradiction (al-tanaqud) in the statements of the philosophers, and this has been achieved, for one of two things has been undermined by it: either what they have said about the rational soul, or what they have said about the estimative power (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya).

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' argument that every state in a body must be divisible. Ghazali uses the example of a sheep's perception of a wolf's enmity: enmity is a single meaning with no parts, yet it is perceived by an animal. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ghazali is trying to prove the immateriality of the soul.
Correction: Ghazali is not proving the soul is immaterial; he is showing a contradiction in the philosophers' own doctrines.

What does Ghazali say is the purpose of his book?

Ghazali uses the sheep example to create a doubt that undermines at least one of the philosophers' doctrines, whether about the rational soul or the estimative power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali shows that a confident philosophical position can unravel when you follow its implications to a contradiction. What belief do you currently hold with certainty, and have you ever truly considered a challenge that could expose an inconsistency within it? Write down one such belief and commit to examining it from the perspective of an honest critic.
?
The philosophers insisted on their premises and dismissed Ghazali's doubt as impossible. When your own deeply held views are questioned, do you react defensively, or do you allow the doubt to genuinely unsettle you? Recall a recent instance and identify one change you need to make in how you respond.
?
If the demonstration of a flaw in your reasoning could bring you closer to the truth, are you willing to actively seek out those flaws? What is one area of your faith or practice where you have avoided looking too closely for fear of finding contradiction? Name it and decide one step you will take this week to investigate it.

The Sheep's Perception as a Counterexample to Divisibility
Ghazali presents the divisibility claim and refutes it using the sheep's enmity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the sheep's perception of enmity to challenge the philosophers' claim that every state in a body is divisible.

Core Idea
Enmity is a meaning that cannot be divided, even when perceived by a bodily faculty.

In this lesson, we examine Ghazali's clever counterexample against the philosophers' argument that every state in a divisible body must itself be divisible. He points to the sheep's perception of a wolf's enmity—a single, indivisible meaning that yet inheres in the animal's bodily faculty. This challenges the very foundation of their proof. We will follow the structure of his objection and reply to a potential counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on Ghazali's initial claim and the subsequent objection and reply.
There are three main parts: the presentation of the philosophers' view, the counterexample, and the response to an objection.
2
Identify the philosophers' premise
Find the sentence where Ghazali states what the philosophers claim: that every state in a body must be divisible. Understand this premise.
3
Analyze the counterexample
Examine how the sheep's perception of enmity refutes the premise. Note that enmity has no parts, yet it is perceived by a bodily faculty.
Ghazali argues that if enmity were divisible, then perceiving it would be impossible as it is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثانى، أن نقول: ما زعمتموه من أن كل حال في جسم فينبغي أن ينقسم، باطل عليكم بما تدركه القوة من الشاة من عداوة الذئب فإنها في حكم شيء واحد، لا يتصور تقسيمه، إذ ليس للعداوة بعض، حتى يقدر إدراك بعضه، وزوال بعضه، وقد حصل إدراكها فى قوة جسمانية عندكم فإن نفس البهائم منطبعة فى الأجسام، لا تبقى بعد الموت، وقد اتفقوا عليه، فإن أمكنهم أن يتكلفوا تقدير الانقسام، في المدركات بالحواس الخمس، وبالحس المشترك وبالقوة الحافظة للصور، فلا يمكنهم تقدير الانقسام في هذه المعانى، التي ليس من شرطها، أن تكون في مادة

فإن قيل: الشاة لا تدرك العداوة المطلقة المجردة عن المادة، بل تدرك عداوة الذئب المعين المشخص، مقروناً بشخصه وهيكله، والقوة العاقلة تدرك الحقائق مجردة، عن المواد والأشخاص

قلنا: الشاة قد أدركت لون الذئب، وشكله، ثم عداوته، فإن كان اللون ينطبع فى القوة الباصرة، وكذا الشكل، وينقسم بانقسام محل البصر، فالعداوة بماذا تدركها؟! فإن أدركتها بجسم، فلينقسم، وليت شعرى ما حال ذلك الإدراك إذا قسم؟! وكيف يكون بعضه؟! أهو إدراك لبعض العداوة؟! فكيف يكون لها بعض؟! أو كل قسم أدرك كل العداوة؟! فتكون العداوة معلومة مراراً، بثبوت إدراكها في كل قسم من أقسام المحل

فإذن هذه شبهة مشككة لهم في برهانهم، فلابد من الحل.

فإن قيل: هذه مناقضة في المعقولات، والمعقولات لا تنقض، فإنكم مهما لم تقدروا على الشك في المقدمتين، وهو أن العلم الواحد لا ينقسم، وأن ما لا ينقسم لا يقوم بجسم منقسم، لم يمكنكم الشك في النتيجة.

والجواب، أن هذا الكتاب ما صنفناه، إلا لبيان التهافت والتناقض، فى كلام الفلاسفة، وقد حصل، إذ انتفض به أحد الأمرين: إما ما ذكروه في النفس الناطقة وإما ما ذكروه في القوة الوهمية

ثم نقول: هذه المناقضة، تبين أنهم غفلوا عن موضع تلبيس فى القياس، ولعل موضع الالتباس قولهم: إن العلم منطبع في الجسم، انطباع اللون في المتلون، وينقسم اللون بانقسام المتلون، فينقسم العلم بانقسام محله، والخلل في لفظ الانطباع، إذ يمكن ألا تكون نسبة العلم إلى محله، كنسبة اللون إلى المتلون، حتى يقال: إنه منبسط عليه ومنطبع فيه ومنتشر في جوانبه، فينقسم بانقسامه، فلعل نسبة العلم إلى محله، على وجه آخر، وذلك الوجه لا يجوز فيه الانقسام، عند انقسام المحل، بل نسبته إليه، كنسبة إدراك العداوة إلى الجسم. ووجوه نسبة الأوصاف إلى محالها، ليست محصورة فى فن واحد، ولا هي معلومة التفاصيل لنا، علماً نثق به، فالحكم عليه دون الإحاطة بتفصيل النسب حكم غير موثوق به. وعلى الجملة لا ينكر أن ما ذكروه، مما يقوى (١) الظن، ويغلبه، وإنما ينكر كونه معلوماً يقيناً، علماً لا يجوز الغلط فيه، ولا يتطرق إليه الشك، وهذا القدر مشكك فيه
The second station: that we say: What you have claimed, that every state in a body must be divisible, is refuted against you by what the faculty perceives from the sheep of the enmity of the wolf, for it is in the ruling of a single thing that cannot be imagined to be divided, since enmity has no part such that it could be imagined to perceive some of it and some of it to cease. And its perception has occurred in a bodily faculty according to you, for the soul of beasts is impressed in bodies, and does not remain after death, and they have agreed upon that. If they can undertake to estimate division in the perceptions by the five senses, and by the common sense, and by the faculty that preserves forms, they cannot estimate division in these meanings which are not conditioned to be in matter.

If it is said: The sheep does not perceive absolute enmity abstracted from matter, but rather perceives the enmity of the specific determined wolf, conjoined with its person and form, while the rational faculty perceives realities abstracted from matters and individuals.

We say: The sheep has perceived the color of the wolf and its shape, then its enmity. So if the color is impressed in the faculty of sight, and likewise the shape, and divides by the division of the place of sight, then by what does it perceive enmity? For if it perceives it by a body, let it divide. Would that I knew what the state of that perception is when divided? And how would some of it be? Is it perception of some of the enmity? Then how could enmity have a part? Or does every part perceive all of the enmity? Then enmity would be known repeatedly, by the establishment of its perception in every part of the parts of the place.

So this is a skeptical objection to their proof, so a solution is necessary.

If it is said: This is a contradiction in the intelligibles, and the intelligibles are not contradicted. For as long as you are unable to doubt the two premises—that knowledge does not divide, and that what does not divide cannot subsist in a divisible body—you cannot doubt the conclusion.

The answer: This book was only composed to show the incoherence and contradiction in the speech of the philosophers, and it has occurred, since one of two things has been overturned by it: either what they mentioned about the rational soul, or what they mentioned about the estimative faculty.

Then we say: This contradiction shows that they were heedless of a point of confusion in the syllogism. Perhaps the point of confusion is their saying: knowledge is impressed in the body like the impression of color in the colored object, and color divides by the division of the colored object, so knowledge divides by the division of its locus. The defect is in the term 'impression', for it is possible that the relation of knowledge to its locus is not like the relation of color to the colored object, such that it is said to be spread over it, impressed in it, and diffused in its sides, so that it divides by its division. Perhaps the relation of knowledge to its locus is in another manner, and that manner does not permit division upon the division of the locus; rather its relation to it is like the perception of enmity to the body. The aspects of the relation of attributes to their loci are not confined to one genus, nor are they known to us in detail with a knowledge we trust. So judging it without encompassing the details of relations is an unreliable judgment. In summary, it is not denied that what they mentioned may strengthen probability and prevail, but it is denied that it is known with certainty, a knowledge in which error is not allowed and into which doubt does not enter. And this much is subject to doubt.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage contains a multi-part argument and refutation. It begins by presenting the philosophers' claim that every state in a body must be divisible. Ghazali counters with the example of a sheep's perception of a wolf's enmity, which is a single, indivisible meaning. He then addresses the objection that the sheep perceives a particular enmity, not an abstract one, and argues that even so, the enmity itself is not divisible. This creates a skeptical challenge for the philosophers. When the philosophers respond that this contradicts two axiomatic premises—that knowledge is indivisible and that what is indivisible cannot inhere in a divisible body—Ghazali replies that his book is designed to show incoherence, and that the philosophers' own statements about the rational soul or the estimative faculty are undermined. Finally, he pinpoints the confusion in their use of the term 'impression' (inṭibāʿ). He explains that the relation of knowledge to its locus may differ from the relation of color to a colored object, and that without detailed knowledge of such relations, their claim cannot be certain, only probable.

Correction: Ghazali's argument is limited to showing that the philosophers' universal claim fails for at least one clear case, not that no state is divisible.

The Philosophers' Response and Ghazali's Final Refutation
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' counter-objection and exposes the confusion in the concept of impression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the philosophers' counter-objection and exposes the confusion in their concept of 'impression' (inṭibāʿ), concluding that their argument is not certain.

Core Idea
Knowledge's relation to its locus may differ from color's impression, making their claim merely probable, not certain.

After Ghazali's initial refutation, the philosophers respond that his objection contradicts two undeniable premises: that knowledge is indivisible and that what is indivisible cannot reside in a divisible body. Ghazali answers by pointing out that his book aims to show the incoherence of the philosophers' system, and then he pinpoints the real issue: the assumption that knowledge relates to its locus like color to a colored object. He shows that this assumption is unjustified, and therefore the philosophers' conclusion lacks certainty. Let's explore this final stage of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the philosophers' objection and Ghazali's reply.
The passage contains an objection, an answer, and a detailed critique of the term 'impression'.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the two premises the philosophers claim are undeniable: that one knowledge does not divide, and that what does not divide cannot subsist in a divisible body.
These are key points of contention.
3
Understand Ghazali's response
Ghazali says the philosophers' own statements about the rational soul or estimative faculty are overturned. Then he explains the confusion lies in the word 'impression'.
He argues that the relation of knowledge to its locus might be different from color's impression.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that the philosophers' argument is not certain knowledge but only probable, because we do not have detailed knowledge of the relations of attributes to their loci.
This is a key point for understanding the limitations of their demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثانى، أن نقول: ما زعمتموه من أن كل حال في جسم فينبغي أن ينقسم، باطل عليكم بما تدركه القوة من الشاة من عداوة الذئب فإنها في حكم شيء واحد، لا يتصور تقسيمه، إذ ليس للعداوة بعض، حتى يقدر إدراك بعضه، وزوال بعضه، وقد حصل إدراكها فى قوة جسمانية عندكم فإن نفس البهائم منطبعة فى الأجسام، لا تبقى بعد الموت، وقد اتفقوا عليه، فإن أمكنهم أن يتكلفوا تقدير الانقسام، في المدركات بالحواس الخمس، وبالحس المشترك وبالقوة الحافظة للصور، فلا يمكنهم تقدير الانقسام في هذه المعانى، التي ليس من شرطها، أن تكون في مادة

فإن قيل: الشاة لا تدرك العداوة المطلقة المجردة عن المادة، بل تدرك عداوة الذئب المعين المشخص، مقروناً بشخصه وهيكله، والقوة العاقلة تدرك الحقائق مجردة، عن المواد والأشخاص

قلنا: الشاة قد أدركت لون الذئب، وشكله، ثم عداوته، فإن كان اللون ينطبع فى القوة الباصرة، وكذا الشكل، وينقسم بانقسام محل البصر، فالعداوة بماذا تدركها؟! فإن أدركتها بجسم، فلينقسم، وليت شعرى ما حال ذلك الإدراك إذا قسم؟! وكيف يكون بعضه؟! أهو إدراك لبعض العداوة؟! فكيف يكون لها بعض؟! أو كل قسم أدرك كل العداوة؟! فتكون العداوة معلومة مراراً، بثبوت إدراكها في كل قسم من أقسام المحل

فإذن هذه شبهة مشككة لهم في برهانهم، فلابد من الحل.

فإن قيل: هذه مناقضة في المعقولات، والمعقولات لا تنقض، فإنكم مهما لم تقدروا على الشك في المقدمتين، وهو أن العلم الواحد لا ينقسم، وأن ما لا ينقسم لا يقوم بجسم منقسم، لم يمكنكم الشك في النتيجة.

والجواب، أن هذا الكتاب ما صنفناه، إلا لبيان التهافت والتناقض، فى كلام الفلاسفة، وقد حصل، إذ انتفض به أحد الأمرين: إما ما ذكروه في النفس الناطقة وإما ما ذكروه في القوة الوهمية

ثم نقول: هذه المناقضة، تبين أنهم غفلوا عن موضع تلبيس فى القياس، ولعل موضع الالتباس قولهم: إن العلم منطبع في الجسم، انطباع اللون في المتلون، وينقسم اللون بانقسام المتلون، فينقسم العلم بانقسام محله، والخلل في لفظ الانطباع، إذ يمكن ألا تكون نسبة العلم إلى محله، كنسبة اللون إلى المتلون، حتى يقال: إنه منبسط عليه ومنطبع فيه ومنتشر في جوانبه، فينقسم بانقسامه، فلعل نسبة العلم إلى محله، على وجه آخر، وذلك الوجه لا يجوز فيه الانقسام، عند انقسام المحل، بل نسبته إليه، كنسبة إدراك العداوة إلى الجسم. ووجوه نسبة الأوصاف إلى محالها، ليست محصورة فى فن واحد، ولا هي معلومة التفاصيل لنا، علماً نثق به، فالحكم عليه دون الإحاطة بتفصيل النسب حكم غير موثوق به. وعلى الجملة لا ينكر أن ما ذكروه، مما يقوى (١) الظن، ويغلبه، وإنما ينكر كونه معلوماً يقيناً، علماً لا يجوز الغلط فيه، ولا يتطرق إليه الشك، وهذا القدر مشكك فيه
The second station: that we say: What you have claimed, that every state in a body must be divisible, is refuted against you by what the faculty perceives from the sheep of the enmity of the wolf, for it is in the ruling of a single thing that cannot be imagined to be divided, since enmity has no part such that it could be imagined to perceive some of it and some of it to cease. And its perception has occurred in a bodily faculty according to you, for the soul of beasts is impressed in bodies, and does not remain after death, and they have agreed upon that. If they can undertake to estimate division in the perceptions by the five senses, and by the common sense, and by the faculty that preserves forms, they cannot estimate division in these meanings which are not conditioned to be in matter.

If it is said: The sheep does not perceive absolute enmity abstracted from matter, but rather perceives the enmity of the specific determined wolf, conjoined with its person and form, while the rational faculty perceives realities abstracted from matters and individuals.

We say: The sheep has perceived the color of the wolf and its shape, then its enmity. So if the color is impressed in the faculty of sight, and likewise the shape, and divides by the division of the place of sight, then by what does it perceive enmity? For if it perceives it by a body, let it divide. Would that I knew what the state of that perception is when divided? And how would some of it be? Is it perception of some of the enmity? Then how could enmity have a part? Or does every part perceive all of the enmity? Then enmity would be known repeatedly, by the establishment of its perception in every part of the parts of the place.

So this is a skeptical objection to their proof, so a solution is necessary.

If it is said: This is a contradiction in the intelligibles, and the intelligibles are not contradicted. For as long as you are unable to doubt the two premises—that knowledge does not divide, and that what does not divide cannot subsist in a divisible body—you cannot doubt the conclusion.

The answer: This book was only composed to show the incoherence and contradiction in the speech of the philosophers, and it has occurred, since one of two things has been overturned by it: either what they mentioned about the rational soul, or what they mentioned about the estimative faculty.

Then we say: This contradiction shows that they were heedless of a point of confusion in the syllogism. Perhaps the point of confusion is their saying: knowledge is impressed in the body like the impression of color in the colored object, and color divides by the division of the colored object, so knowledge divides by the division of its locus. The defect is in the term 'impression', for it is possible that the relation of knowledge to its locus is not like the relation of color to the colored object, such that it is said to be spread over it, impressed in it, and diffused in its sides, so that it divides by its division. Perhaps the relation of knowledge to its locus is in another manner, and that manner does not permit division upon the division of the locus; rather its relation to it is like the perception of enmity to the body. The aspects of the relation of attributes to their loci are not confined to one genus, nor are they known to us in detail with a knowledge we trust. So judging it without encompassing the details of relations is an unreliable judgment. In summary, it is not denied that what they mentioned may strengthen probability and prevail, but it is denied that it is known with certainty, a knowledge in which error is not allowed and into which doubt does not enter. And this much is subject to doubt.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage contains a multi-part argument and refutation. It begins by presenting the philosophers' claim that every state in a body must be divisible. Ghazali counters with the example of a sheep's perception of a wolf's enmity, which is a single, indivisible meaning. He then addresses the objection that the sheep perceives a particular enmity, not an abstract one, and argues that even so, the enmity itself is not divisible. This creates a skeptical challenge for the philosophers. When the philosophers respond that this contradicts two axiomatic premises—that knowledge is indivisible and that what is indivisible cannot inhere in a divisible body—Ghazali replies that his book is designed to show incoherence, and that the philosophers' own statements about the rational soul or the estimative faculty are undermined. Finally, he pinpoints the confusion in their use of the term 'impression' (inṭibāʿ). He explains that the relation of knowledge to its locus may differ from the relation of color to a colored object, and that without detailed knowledge of such relations, their claim cannot be certain, only probable.

النفس الناطقة
القوة الوهمية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ghazali completely rejects the principle that indivisible things cannot inhere in divisible bodies, but he only shows that the relation is not as assumed.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny the principle; he questions the assumption that knowledge is related to the body by impression, which would make it divisible.

Second Proof: The Three Possibilities for Knowledge's Relation to its Locus
Ghazali presents the philosophers' second proof examining the relation of knowledge to the knower.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second proof that knowledge must have a relation to the knower and that this relation falls into one of three categories, starting with the first.

Core Idea
Knowledge cannot exist without a relation to the knower; that relation must be one of three kinds.

This passage presents the second argument from the philosophers in the Tahafut. They move from the term 'impression' to the concept of 'relation' (nisba) and argue that cutting off the relation would lead to absurdity. The argument then enumerates three possible relations, though only the first is mentioned before the page cuts off.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the shift from the conditional about 'impression' to the question of 'relation'.
The text uses technical terms like 'العلم' (knowledge) and 'النسبة' (relation).
2
Understand the necessity of a relation
Focus on the argument that a relation must exist between knowledge and the knower. If cut off, anyone would equally be knower, which is absurd.
This is the key move: without relation, knowledge would not be tied to a specific knower.
3
Identify the first of three divisions
The passage states that if a relation exists, it must be one of three. Only the first is given: the relation is to every part of the locus.
The other two are not written here but are implied by the structure of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ثان

قالوا: إن كان العلم بالمعلوم الواحد العقلى – وهو المعلوم المجرد عن المواد منطبعاً في المادة، انطباع الأعراض في الجواهر الجسمانية، لزم انقسامه بالضرورة بانقسام الجسم كما سبق، وإن لم يكن منطبعاً فيها، ولا منبسطاً عليها. وإن استكره لفظ الانطباع، فلنعدل إلى عبارة أخرى، ونقول: هل للعلم نسبة إلى العالم به، أم لا؟! ومحال قطع النسبة، لأنه إن قطعت النسبة عنه؛ فكونه عالماً به، لم صار أولى به، من كون غيره عالماً به؟! وإن كان له نسبة، فلا يخلو من ثلاثة أقسام: إما أن تكون النسبة لكل جزء من أجزاء المحل.
Second proof: They said: If knowledge of a single intelligible—which is the intelligible abstracted from matters—is impressed in matter, like the impression of accidents in bodily substances, then its division necessarily follows from the division of the body as previously. If it is not impressed in it nor spread over it—and if one finds the term 'impression' objectionable, let us change to another expression and say: Does knowledge have a relation to the knower or not? It is impossible to cut off the relation, because if the relation is cut off from him, then why is he more deserving of being knowing of it than someone else? And if it has a relation, it does not avoid three divisions: either the relation is to every part of the parts of the locus.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof from the philosophers. It first states a conditional: if knowledge is impressed in matter, it must divide as previously argued (since matter is divisible). Then it offers an alternative analysis, setting aside the term 'impression' and instead asking about the relation (nisba) between knowledge and the knower. The argument contends that a relation must exist; otherwise, any knower would be equally entitled to know any object, which is absurd. If a relation exists, it must fall into one of three categories. However, the text cuts off after mentioning the first category: that the relation is to every part of the locus. The implication is that either the relation is to every part (which would still lead to divisibility) or it is not, and each option has consequences that the philosophers would have to address. Ghazali is here laying out the philosophers' own reasoning to later critique it.

Key Terms
العلم
النسبة
المعلوم العقلى
الانطباع
الأعراض
الجواهر الجسمانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali endorses the philosophers' three divisions as valid.
Correction: Ghazali is merely presenting their argument to show its internal problems; he does not accept it.

C) Because the philosophers have proven it in their first proof
D) Because the term 'impression' requires a relation

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that knowledge must have a relation to the knower, otherwise anyone could claim it; this relation can only be one of three types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Are you using it to grow closer to the Truth, or are you letting it sit idle, unattached to your actions?
?
The passage forces you to consider: if you cut off the relation between yourself and your knowledge, you become interchangeable with anyone. The Three Possibilities for Knowledge's Relation to Parts
Ghazali enumerates and refutes three possibilities for the relationship of knowledge to parts of a divisible body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's logical argument that knowledge cannot be related to parts of a divisible body by examining three impossible possibilities.

Core Idea
Knowledge of a single object cannot be divided across physical parts.

In this passage, Ghazali systematically dismantles any attempt to locate knowledge in a divisible physical subject. He presents three logical possibilities for how the relationship might hold—to some parts, to no parts, or to every part—and shows that each leads to absurdity. This sets the stage for his broader critique of the philosophers' theory of perception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possibilities
Read the passage and list the three possible relationships Ghazali considers for knowledge to a divisible body.
They are: relation to some parts, to no parts, or to every part.
2
Follow the refutations
For each possibility, understand why Ghazali deems it impossible. Note his reasoning for each.
The refutations rely on logical consequences like infinite regress or division of knowledge.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Conclude that knowledge cannot inhere in a divisible subject, as all logical options fail.
This drives Ghazali's larger argument against the philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو تكون لبعض أجزاء المحل دون بعض . أو لا يكون لواحد من الأجزاء نسبة إليه . وباطل أن يقال : لا نسبة لواحد من الأجزاء ، فإنه إذا لم يكن للآحاد نسبة ، لم يكن للمجموع نسبة ، فإن المجتمع من المباينات ، مباين . وباطل أن يقال : النسبة للبعض ، فإن الذى لا نسبة له ، ليس هو من معناه فى شيء ، وليس كلامنا فيه . وباطل أن يقال : لكل جزء مفروض نسبة إلى الذات ، لأنه إن كانت النسبة إلى ذات العلم بأسره ، فمعلوم كل واحد من الأجزاء ليس هو جزءاً من المعلوم ، بل هو المعلوم كما هو ، فيكون معقولا مرات لا نهاية لها بالفعل ، وإن كان كل جزء له نسبة أخرى غير النسبة التي للجزء الآخر إلى ذات العلم ، فذات العلم إذن منقسمة في المعنى ، وقد بينا أن العلم بالمعلوم الواحد من كل وجه ، ينقسم في المعنى ، وإن كانت نسبة كل واحد إلى شيء من ذات العلم، غير ما إليه نسبة الآخر ، فانقسام ذات العلم بهذه الطريقة أظهر . يتبين أن المحسوسات المنطبعة فى الحواس الخمس ، لا تكون إلا أمثلة ، لصور جزئية منقسمة ، فإن الإدراك معناه حصول مثال المدرك ، في نفس المدرك ، ويكون لكل جزء من مثال المحسوس ، نسبة إلى جزء من الآلة الجسمانية . والاعتراض على هذا ما سبق ، فإن تبديل لفظ الانطباع بلفظ النسبة ، لا يدرأ الشبهة ، فيما ينطبع في القوة الوهمية للشاة ، من عداوة الذئب ، كما ذكروه ، فإنه إدراك لا محالة ، وله نسبه إليه ، ويلزم في تلك النسبة ما ذكرتموه . فإن العداوة ليست أمراً مقدراً ، له كمية مقدارية ، حتى ينطبع مثالها في مقدر ، وتنتسب أجزاؤها إلى أجزائه ، وكون شكل الذئب مقدراً ، لا يكفى ، فإن الشاة أدركت شيئاً سوى شكله ، وهو المخالفة والمضادة والعداوة ، والزيادة على الشكل من العداوة ، ليس لها مقدار ، وقد أدركتها بجسم مقدر . فهذه الصورة مشككة فى هذا البرهان ، كما في الأول .
Or it [the relationship] is for some parts of the subject without others. Or there is no relationship to any part. And it is false to say: there is no relationship for any part, because if there is no relationship for the individuals, there is no relationship for the aggregate, for the aggregate of distinct things is distinct. And it is false to say: the relationship is to some [parts], because that which has no relationship has nothing to do with its meaning, and we are not speaking about it. And it is false to say: for every supposed part there is a relationship to the essence, because if the relationship is to the essence of knowledge as a whole, then the known of each part is not a part of the known, but it is the known as it is, so it would be actually intelligible an infinite number of times. And if each part has another relationship different from the relationship of the other part to the essence of knowledge, then the essence of knowledge is thereby divided in meaning, and we have already shown that knowledge of a single thing in every respect is divided in meaning. And if the relationship of each one is to something of the essence of knowledge different from that to which the other has a relationship, then the division of the essence of knowledge in this way is more evident. It becomes clear that sense perceptions impressed in the five senses are nothing but examples of particular divisible forms, for perception means the obtaining of an example of the perceived in the perceiving self, and each part of the example of the sensed has a relationship to a part of the bodily organ. And the objection to this is what preceded: for replacing the word 'impression' with the word 'relationship' does not remove the doubt concerning what is impressed in the estimative faculty of the sheep of the enmity of the wolf, as they mentioned. For it is perception without doubt, and it has a relationship to it, and what you mentioned follows in that relationship. For enmity is not a measurable thing having quantitative magnitude so that its example would be impressed in something measurable and its parts would relate to its parts. And the fact that the wolf's shape is measurable is not sufficient, for the sheep perceived something other than its shape, namely opposition, contradiction, and enmity, and the addition to shape from enmity has no magnitude, yet it has perceived it by a measurable body. So this case is problematic in this proof, as in the first.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument against the possibility that knowledge (or perception) can be related to parts of a divisible body. The author examines three possible relationships: either the relationship is to some parts only, to no parts, or to every part. He rejects each possibility: (1) If it is to some parts, then the parts without relationship are excluded from the meaning of knowledge, which is unacceptable. (2) If it is to no parts, then since the aggregate gets its relationship from its parts, the whole would have no relationship. (3) If it is to every part, then either each part relates to the whole essence of knowledge, leading to infinite multiplicity (each part knows the whole known as it is), or each part has a distinct relationship, which divides the essence of knowledge—this is also problematic. Then he extends the argument to sensory perception, using the example of the sheep's perception of the wolf's enmity. Even if we replace 'impression' with 'relationship', the problem remains: enmity has no spatial magnitude, yet it is perceived by a measurable body (the sheep's brain). Thus the proof is problematic.

Key Terms
الجزء
المحل
النسبة
ذات العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge can be located in a physical organ like the brain and distributed across its parts.
Correction: Knowledge of a single object is indivisible, so it cannot reside in divisible matter. Any such relation leads to logical absurdities.

Checkpoint
In the third scenario, what does Ghazali say about the relationship if each part relates to the whole essence of knowledge?
A) It leads to infinite multiplicity of the known object.
B) It leads to division of knowledge into parts.
C) It is the only consistent option.
D) It is impossible because parts are separate.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of a single object cannot be divided across physical parts, as it would lead to logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your knowledge of Allah cannot be divided or compartmentalized, in what areas of your life have you allowed your belief to be only partial, treating it as if it doesn't apply to certain habits or thoughts? Will you commit today to making your faith wholly present in every part of your life?
?
Ghazali shows that dividing knowledge leads to absurdity—either infinite multiple knowings or a divided essence. Have you ever tried to 'hold' contradictory beliefs about the same truth, thinking you could keep them separate? What would it mean for you to seek a unified understanding of God's oneness, even when it demands change?
?
The argument proves that the relationship between knowledge and its subject must be all-or-nothing. Are there aspects of Islamic teachings that you accept in theory but not in practice, as if knowledge could be partial? How does this lesson challenge you to either fully embrace or honestly reject—but not to compromise?

The Problem of Perceiving Non-Quantitative Qualities
Ghazali argues that the problem of relating non-measurable qualities to divisible organs persists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali's argument against the philosophers' theory of perception remains problematic even when 'impression' is replaced with 'relationship', using the example of a sheep perceiving a wolf's enmity.

Core Idea
Immaterial qualities like enmity cannot be related to divisible bodily organs.

Ghazali extends his critique to sensory perception, arguing that even if we replace the term 'impression' with 'relationship', the same difficulty arises. He uses the striking example of a sheep perceiving the enmity of a wolf: enmity is a non-quantitative quality, yet it is perceived by a measurable bodily organ (the sheep's brain). This undermines the philosophers' theory that perception occurs through the impression of forms in a divisible organ.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sensory perception
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the sheep-wolf example.
Notice how Ghazali transitions from the general argument to this specific case.
2
Understand the sheep-wolf example
Identify why the perception of enmity is problematic for the philosophers' theory: enmity has no spatial magnitude.
The wolf's shape is measurable, but the perceived enmity is not.
3
Connect to the earlier argument
Relate this example back to the earlier logical argument about knowledge and divisibility: both show the impossibility of relating non-quantitative entities to divisible matter.
This demonstrates the consistency of Ghazali's critique.

Or it [the relationship] is for some parts of the subject without others. Or there is no relationship to any part. And it is false to say: there is no relationship for any part, because if there is no relationship for the individuals, there is no relationship for the aggregate, for the aggregate of distinct things is distinct. And it is false to say: the relationship is to some [parts], because that which has no relationship has nothing to do with its meaning, and we are not speaking about it. And it is false to say: for every supposed part there is a relationship to the essence, because if the relationship is to the essence of knowledge as a whole, then the known of each part is not a part of the known, but it is the known as it is, so it would be actually intelligible an infinite number of times. And if each part has another relationship different from the relationship of the other part to the essence of knowledge, then the essence of knowledge is thereby divided in meaning, and we have already shown that knowledge of a single thing in every respect is divided in meaning. And if the relationship of each one is to something of the essence of knowledge different from that to which the other has a relationship, then the division of the essence of knowledge in this way is more evident. It becomes clear that sense perceptions impressed in the five senses are nothing but examples of particular divisible forms, for perception means the obtaining of an example of the perceived in the perceiving self, and each part of the example of the sensed has a relationship to a part of the bodily organ. And the objection to this is what preceded: for replacing the word 'impression' with the word 'relationship' does not remove the doubt concerning what is impressed in the estimative faculty of the sheep of the enmity of the wolf, as they mentioned. For it is perception without doubt, and it has a relationship to it, and what you mentioned follows in that relationship. For enmity is not a measurable thing having quantitative magnitude so that its example would be impressed in something measurable and its parts would relate to its parts. And the fact that the wolf's shape is measurable is not sufficient, for the sheep perceived something other than its shape, namely opposition, contradiction, and enmity, and the addition to shape from enmity has no magnitude, yet it has perceived it by a measurable body. So this case is problematic in this proof, as in the first.

He rejects each possibility: (1) If it is to some parts, then the parts without relationship are excluded from the meaning of knowledge, which is unacceptable. Figurative Language Refutes a Proof
Ghazali refutes a proof using figurative language.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that attributing a quality like knowledge to the whole person does not prove it is not seated in a specific part, because such attribution is often figurative.

Core Idea
Attributing knowledge to the whole person is figurative, not literal.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a philosophical objection that claims if knowledge were located in a specific part of the body, then only that part would be called 'knower', not the whole person. He responds by pointing out that language itself is full of such figurative attributions, as when we say someone is 'in Baghdad' even if they are only in one district. This lesson explores how Ghazali uses linguistic analysis to refute a logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
Notice the sharp dismissal 'nonsense'.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent claims that if knowledge were in a part, only that part would be the knower, but the whole person is called knower, so knowledge cannot be in a part.
This is a logical inference based on language.
3
Examine Ghazali's examples
Ghazali gives two examples: calling a person 'seeing' (sight in the eye) and saying someone is 'in Baghdad' (only in a district). These show that attributing to the whole is common figurative speech.
The Baghdad example is key.
4
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: Why does the opponent's inference fail? Because language allows us to attribute to the whole what is actually true of a part.
This is a type of synecdoche.
5
Summarize the lesson
Write down in one sentence: Ghazali refutes the proof by showing that attributing knowledge to the whole is just figurative language, not a literal fact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ثالث . قولهم : إن العلم لو كان فى جزء من الجسم ، لكان العالم ذلك الجزء ، دون سائر أجزاء الإنسان ، والإنسان يقال له عالم ، والعالمية صفة له على الجملة ، من غير نسبة إلى محل مخصوص . وهذا هوس ، فإنه يسمى ، مبصراً وسامعاً ، وذائقاً ، وكذا البهيمة توصف به ، وذلك لا يدل ، على أن إدراك المحسوسات ليس بالجسم ؛ بل هو نوع من التجوز ، كما يقال : فلان فى « بغداد » وإن كان هو في جزء من جملة بغداد » لا فى جميعها ، ولكن يضاف إلى الجملة
Third proof. Their statement: If knowledge were in a part of the body, then that part would be the knower, to the exclusion of the other parts of man, yet man is called 'knower', and knowledgeableness is an attribute of him as a whole, without reference to a particular locus. This is nonsense, for he is called 'seeing', 'hearing', 'tasting', and likewise the beast is described as such, and that does not indicate that perception of sensibles is not by the body; rather it is a kind of figurative speech, just as it is said: 'So-and-so is in Baghdad' even if he is in a part of the whole of Baghdad, not in all of it, but he is attributed to the whole.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a proof presented by opponents. They argue that if knowledge were in a part of the body, then only that part would be the knower, not the whole person. But since we attribute knowledge to the whole person, knowledge cannot be in a part. Ghazali dismisses this as 'haws' (nonsense), because we commonly use figurative speech: we call a person 'seeing' even though sight is in the eye, and we say someone is 'in Baghdad' even if they are in one neighborhood. Thus, attributing an attribute to the whole is a common linguistic phenomenon, not a proof that the attribute is not seated in a part.

Correction: Attributing knowledge to the whole is a figurative expression; it does not mean knowledge is literally in every part, as shown by similar examples like seeing (sight in the eye) or being in Baghdad (only in a district).

Refuting the Impossibility of Coexisting Knowledge and Ignorance
Ghazali refutes the fourth proof on impossibility of coexisting opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' proof that knowledge and ignorance cannot coexist in different parts of the body.

Core Idea
Opposites can coexist in different loci, so knowledge and ignorance can be in different parts.

The philosophers argue that since knowledge and ignorance are opposites, they must occupy the same locus; otherwise a person could be both knowing and ignorant simultaneously—an impossibility. Ghazali rejects this by showing that opposites can coexist in different parts of the same entity, just as black and white can be in different parts of the eye. He also explains why the attribution of 'knower' to the whole person is metaphorical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the structure: the philosophers' proof, then Ghazali's counterexamples and explanation.
The passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the completed argument.
2
Identify the philosophers' proof
Find where the philosophers claim that if knowledge is in one part, ignorance could be in another, making simultaneous knowledge and ignorance possible—an impossibility.
3
Analyze Ghazali's counterexamples
Look at the examples of piebaldness in a horse and black and white in different parts of the eye. How do they show that opposites can coexist in different loci?
4
Understand the metaphorical attribution
Notice how Ghazali explains that calling a person 'knower' is metaphorical, like saying 'he is in Baghdad' when only in part of it. The judgment of being a knower applies only to the locus of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن كان العلم يحل جزءاً من القلب أو الدماغ مثلا ، فالجهل ضده، فينبغى أن يجوز قيامه بجزء آخر من القلب أو الدماغ ، ويكون الإنسان في حال واحدة عالماً وجاهلاً بشيء واحد ، فلما استحال ذلك ، تبين أن محل الجهل ، هو محل العلم ، وأن ذلك المحل واحد ، يستحيل اجتماع الضدين فيه ، فإنه لو كان منقسما ، لما استحال قيام الجهل ببعضه ، والعلم ببعضه ، لأن الشيء في محل لا يضاده ضده ، في محل آخر ، كما يجتمع البلق ، في الفرس الواحد والسواد والبياض فى العين الواحدة ، ولكن في محلين ولا يلزم هذا فى الحواس ، فإنه لا ضد لإدراكاتها ، ولكنه قد يدرك وقد لا يدرك ، فليس بينهما ألا تقابل الوجود والعدم، فلا جرم نقول : يدرك ببعض أجزائه كالعين والأذن ، ولا يدرك بسائر بدنه ، وليس فيه تناقض . ولا يغنى عن هذا قولكم : إن العالمية مضادة للجاهلية ، والحكم عام لجميع البدن ، إذ يستحيل أن يكون الحكم في غير محل العلة ، فالعالم هو المحل الذي قام العلم به ، فإن أطلق الاسم على الجملة فبالمجاز ، كما يقال : هو في بغداد وإن كان هو في بعضها ، وكما يقال : هو مبصر ، وإن كنا بالضرورة نعلم ، أن حكم الإبصار لا يثبت للرجل واليد ، بل يختص بالعين ، وتضاد الأحكام كتضاد العلل ، فإن الأحكام تقتصر على محل العلل ولا يخلص من هذا قول القائل : إن المحل المهيأ لقبول العلم والجهل من الإنسان واحد ، فيتضادان عليه ، فإن عندكم أن كل جسم فيه حياة فهو قابل
If knowledge resides in a part of the heart or brain, for example, then ignorance is its opposite; it should be possible for it to subsist in another part of the heart or brain, and thus a person could be simultaneously knowing and ignorant of one same thing. But this is impossible. So it becomes evident that the locus of ignorance is the same as the locus of knowledge, and that locus is one, it being impossible for two opposites to coexist in it. For if it were divided, it would not be impossible for ignorance to subsist in some part and knowledge in another, because a thing in one locus is not opposed by its opposite in another locus, just as piebaldness (balq) occurs in a single horse, and black and white in a single eye, but in two loci. This does not apply to the senses, for there is no opposite to their perceptions; a sense may perceive or not perceive. The only relation between them is that of existence and non-existence. So we say without contradiction: a thing perceives by some of its parts, like the eye and ear, and does not perceive by the rest of its body. Nor will your saying suffice: that the property of being a knower (al-ʿālamiyya) is opposed to ignorance (al-jāhiliyya), and the judgment applies to the whole body, for it is impossible for a judgment to be in a locus other than that of its cause. Thus the knower is the locus in which knowledge subsists; if the name is applied to the whole, it is metaphorical, just as it is said: 'He is in Baghdad' even though he is only in part of it, and as it is said: 'He is seeing', even though we know necessarily that the judgment of sight is not affirmed for the leg and hand, but is restricted to the eye. The opposition of judgments is like the opposition of causes, for judgments are confined to the locus of their causes. You will not be saved from this by the statement that the locus disposed to receive knowledge and ignorance in a human being is one, so that the two opposites cannot apply to it, because in your view every living body is receptive...
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' fourth proof and Ghazali's detailed refutation. The proof argues: if knowledge and ignorance are opposites, and knowledge is in a part of the heart/brain, then ignorance could be in another part, making a person simultaneously knowledgeable and ignorant of the same thing—an impossibility. Therefore the locus of both must be the same and indivisible. Ghazali first compares this to piebaldness in a horse or black and white in a single eye—these opposites can coexist if in different loci. However, he notes that the senses do not have opposites, only presence/absence, so they are not a problem. He then reiterates the metaphorical nature of attributing 'knower' to the whole person, using the Baghdad analogy and the example of sight. The opposition of judgments (like being knowing vs. ignorant) follows the opposition of causes; the judgment applies only to the locus of the cause. Finally, he mentions that the objector might say the locus for knowledge and ignorance is one, but Ghazali cuts off, likely continuing the argument. The core is that the philosophers' claim that the locus must be single and indivisible does not hold because opposites can coexist in different parts, and the attribution to the whole is metaphorical.

ضِدّ
عَالَمِيَّة
جَاهِلِيَّة
عِلَّة
تَجَوُّز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Opposites cannot coexist in any form; thus knowledge and ignorance must occupy the same locus.
Correction: Opposites can coexist in different loci of the same thing, as shown by black and white in different parts of the eye.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali counter the argument that knowledge and ignorance cannot coexist in different parts of the body?
A) He denies that ignorance is the opposite of knowledge.
B) He gives examples of opposites coexisting in different loci, such as black and white in different parts of the eye.
C) He argues that both knowledge and ignorance are located in the soul, not the body.
D) He claims that the heart is indivisible.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' proof by showing that opposites can coexist in different loci, and that attributing properties to the whole is metaphorical.

Ghazali's Refutation of the Philosophers on Bodily Perception
Ghazali answers the philosophers' objection that the intellect cannot be in a body because it does not always perceive it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali responds to the philosophers' demonstration that the intellect cannot be located in a body because it would then have to perceive that body constantly, which it does not.

Core Idea
The self always has a general awareness of its body, even without detailed perception of organs.

In this passage, Ghazali engages with a philosophical objection against the soul's embodiment. The philosophers argue by demonstration that if the intellect were located in the heart or brain, it would have to perceive them constantly, which it does not. Ghazali responds by distinguishing between general bodily awareness and detailed perception, using the analogy of smell.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
First, read the philosophers' objection: if the intellect is in a body (heart/brain), it should always perceive that body, just as it always perceives itself. Since we sometimes do not perceive our heart/brain, the intellect cannot be there.
Notice the philosophers rely on 'demonstration' (al-burhan), not mere induction.
2
Identify Ghazali's key analogy
Find where Ghazali compares bodily awareness to the sense of smell. He says we always know we smell with our body, but we do not have a precise image of the olfactory organ. Similarly, we always have a general sense of our body but not detailed knowledge of organs like the heart.
This analogy shows that constant awareness can be general, not necessarily detailed.
3
Reflect on the reframing
Consider how Ghazali reframes the problem: lack of detailed perception does not disprove location. Instead, the self's continuous sense of its own body (as a substratum) is always present, even when specific organs are not consciously perceived.
This undermines the philosophers' assumption that constant perception means constant detailed knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لسنا نعول على مجرد الاستقراء للحواس ، بل نعول على البرهان ، ونقول : لو كان القلب أو الدماغ ، هو نفس الإنسان ، لكان لا إدراكهما ، حتى لا يخلو عن أن يعقلهما جميعاً (١) ، كما أنه لا يخلو عن إدراك نفسه ، فإن أحداً لا ذاته يعزب عن ذاته ، بل يكون مثبتاً لنفسه ، في نفسه أبداً ، والإنسان ما لم يسمع حديث القلب والدماغ ، أو لم يشاهما بالتشريح ، من إنسان آخر ، لا يدركهما ولا يعتقد وجودهما ، فإن كان العقل حالا في جسم ، فينبغي أن يعقل (۲) ذلك الجسم أبداً ، أو لا يدركه (۳) أبداً ، وليس واحد من الأمرين بصحيح ، بل يعقل في حالة ، ولا يعقل في حالة . وهذا تحقيق ، وهو أن الإدراك الحال فى محل ، إنما يدرك المحل لنسبة له إلى المحل ، ولا يتصور أن تكون له نسبة إليه ، سوى الحلول فيه ، فليدركه أبداً وإن كانت هذه النسبة لا تكفى ، فينبغى ألا يدرك أبداً ، إذ لا يمكن أن تكون له نسبة أخرى إليه ، كما أنه لما كان يعقل نفسه ، عقل نفسه أبداً ولم يغفل عنها بحال . كغفلته عن قلنا : الإنسان ما دام يشعر بنفسه ، ولا يغفل عنها ، فإنه يشعر بجسده وجسمه ، نعم لا يتعين له اسم القلب وصورته وشكله ، ولكنه يثبت نفسه جسما ، حتى يثبت نفسه فى ثيابه ، وفى بيته ، والنفس التى ذكروها ، لا تناسب البيت والثوب . فإثباته لأصل الجسم ملازم له ، وغفلته عن شكله واسمه ، محل « الشم » وأنهما زائدتان فى مقدم الدماغ ، شبيهتان بحلمتي الثدى ، فإن كل إنسان يعلم أنه يدرك الرائحة بجسمه ، ولكن محل الإدراك لا يتشكل له ولا يتعين ، وإن كان يدرك أنه إلى الرأس ، أقرب منه إلى العقب ، ومن جملة الرأس إلى داخل الأنف ، أقرب منه إلى داخل الأذن ، فكذلك يشعر الإنسان بنفسه ، ويعلم أن هويته التى بها قوامه ، إلى قلبه وصدره أقرب منها ، إلى رجله ، فإنه يقدر نفسه باقياً مع عدم الرجل ، ولا يقدر على تقدير نفسه ، باقياً مع عدم القلب . فما ذكروه من أنه يغفل عن الجسم تارة ، وتارة لا يغفل عنه ، فليس كذلك
If it is said: We do not rely on mere induction from the senses, but we rely on demonstration, and we say: If the heart or the brain were the soul of man, then he would have perception of them both, so that he would not fail to conceive them both (1), just as he does not fail to perceive himself; for no one's self is absent from his self; rather, he is always affirming himself in his self. Man, unless he has heard talk of the heart and brain or seen them by dissection from another man, does not perceive them or believe in their existence. If the intellect is located in a body, it ought either to conceive (2) that body always or not perceive (3) it always. Neither of the two is correct; rather, it conceives in one state and does not conceive in another. This is a verification: a perception that is located in a locus perceives the locus because of a relation to it; it is inconceivable that it have a relation to it other than being located in it. So it ought to perceive it always, or if this relation is not sufficient, it ought never to perceive it, for it cannot have another relation to it, just as since it conceives itself, it conceives itself always and is never oblivious to it. As for his oblivion: we say: Man, as long as he is conscious of himself and not oblivious to himself, is conscious of his body and his frame. Yes, the name, form, and shape of the heart are not specified to him, but he affirms himself as a body, to the extent that he affirms himself in his clothes and in his house. The soul that they mentioned is not suitable for house and clothes. So his affirmation of the bodily substratum is inseparable from him, and his obliviousness to its shape and name is like the sense of smell: there are two protrusions at the front of the brain, like two nipples. Every man knows that he perceives smell by his body, but the locus of perception is not formed or specified to him, although he perceives that it is closer to the head than to the heel, and from the head as a whole it is closer to the inside of the nose than to the inside of the ear. Similarly, man is conscious of himself and knows that his identity, by which he subsists, is closer to his heart and chest than to his foot. For he can consider himself as remaining while the foot is absent, but he cannot consider himself as remaining while the heart is absent. So what they mentioned—that he is oblivious to the body sometimes and not oblivious at other times—is not so.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer dialogue. The objector (philosopher) argues by demonstration: if the intellect were located in a body (heart or brain), it would have to perceive that body always, just as it always perceives itself. But we sometimes do not perceive our heart or brain (unless we learn of them), so the intellect cannot be in a body. Ghazali answers by distinguishing two types of perception: the self always has a general awareness of its body (as a substratum), even if unaware of specific details like shape or name. He compares this to the sense of smell: we know we smell with something in the nose, but we do not have a precise image of the olfactory organ. Similarly, we always feel our body as a whole, but not its specific parts. Moreover, we can imagine ourselves without a foot but not without the heart, showing that the self is more closely tied to the heart area. Thus, the claim that we are sometimes oblivious to the body is false; we are always aware of it in a general sense.

Correction: Ghazali shows that constant perception can be general and non-detailed; we always have a general awareness of our body, though specific details may be unknown.

Checkpoint

D) Because the heart and brain are hidden inside the body.

Refuting the Objection from Faculty Variation
Ghazali refutes an objection about faculty variation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the objection that variation in faculties undermines rational certainty by appealing to the regularity of habitual patterns.

Core Idea
Variation in faculties follows habitual patterns; referring to habit yields certainty.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a key objection: if faculties like smell and sight vary in strength, how can we rely on them for certain knowledge? He responds by showing that such variation is not random but follows observable patterns (the course of habit). By grounding knowledge in these regular patterns, certainty remains intact. This lesson walks through the objection and Ghazali's multi-layered refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection in the passage: the claim that faculties increase and decrease due to countless causes, so no constancy can be assumed.
Notice the objector's tactic: they point to variability to undermine any claim of certainty.
2
Identify Ghazali's examples
Ghazali counters with specific examples: smell strengthens after forty while sight weakens, and these differ across animals. See how these examples show pattern, not randomness.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يبعد' (it is not far) – Ghazali is showing plausibility, not proving a definitive cause.
3
Understand the analogy of sight and intellect
Ghazali notes that sight is active from birth while intellect matures later, explaining why sight weakens earlier. This analogy grounds variation in natural development.
Compare with the gray hair analogy: hair on the head turns gray before beard hair because it is older.
4
Grasp the key principle: habit as foundation
Ghazali concludes that if we do not refer these matters to the course of habit (مجارى العادات), no reliable knowledge can be built. This is the core of his refutation.
‘Course of habit’ means regular patterns observed in nature, which provide practical certainty.
5
Connect to certainty
The objection fails because it ignores that variation itself is patterned; habitual regularity is sufficient for certainty in practical and rational matters.
This passage defends the reliability of empirical knowledge against skeptical doubts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض أن نقول : نقصان القوى وزيادتها لها أسباب كثيرة لا تنحصر ، فقد يقوى بعض القوى ، فى ابتداء العمر ، وبعضها فى الوسط ، وبعضها في الآخر ، وأمر العقل أيضاً كذلك ، فلا يبقى إلا أن يدعى الغالب ولا بعد فى أن يختلف الشم والبصر ، فى أن الشم يقوى بعد الأربعين ، والبصر يضعف ، وإن تساويا في كونهما حالين في الجسم ، كما تتفاوت هذه القوى في الحيوانات ، فيقوى الشم فى بعضها ، والسمع في بعضها ، والبصر في بعضها ، لاختلاف فى أمزجتها لا يمكن الوقوف على ضبطه. فلا يبعد أن يكون مزاج الآلات أيضاً يختلف ، في حق الأشخاص ، وفى حق الأحوال ، ويكون أحد الأسباب فى سبق الضعف إلى البصر ، دون العقل، أن البصر أقدم ، فإنه مبصر في أول فطرته ، ولا يتم عقله إلا بعد خمس عشرة سنة ، أو زيادة ، على ما يشاهد اختلاف الناس فيه ، حتى قيل : إن الشيب إلى شعر الرأس ، أسبق منه إلى شعر اللحية ، لأن شعر الرأس أقدم . فهذه الأسباب إن خاض الخائض فيها ، ولم يرد هذه الأمور إلى مجارى العادات ، فلا يمكن أن يبنى عليها علماً موثوقاً به ، لأن جهات الاحتمال يقوى أو يضعف ، لا تنحصر ، فلا يورث شيء من ذلك يقيناً .
And the objection is that we say: The decrease and increase of faculties have many causes that are not limited. For some faculties may strengthen at the beginning of life, some in the middle, and some at the end, and the matter of the intellect is also like that. So nothing remains except to claim the predominant. And there is no improbability in that smell and sight differ, in that smell strengthens after forty, while sight weakens, even though they are equal in being states in the body, just as these faculties vary in animals: smell strengthens in some, hearing in some, and sight in some, due to differences in their temperaments which cannot be precisely determined. So it is not far that the temperament of the instruments also differs, regarding persons and regarding states, and one of the reasons for weakness preceding sight over the intellect is that sight is older, for it sees from the beginning of its creation, while its intellect is not completed until after fifteen years or more, according to the observed differences among people, even it is said that gray hair reaches the hair of the head earlier than the hair of the beard because the hair of the head is older. So if one delves into these causes and does not refer these matters to the course of habit, then a reliable knowledge cannot be built upon them, because the aspects of probability of strengthening or weakening are not limited, so none of that yields certainty.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector argues that the faculties (quwa) of the body, including the intellect, increase and decrease due to many causes, so one cannot rely on their constancy. In response, Ghazali first notes that such variation is not random but follows patterns: some faculties strengthen at certain ages, others weaken. He gives examples: smell strengthens after forty while sight weakens, and these differences occur across animals due to temperament. He further argues that the precedence of weakness in sight over intellect is because sight is present from birth while intellect develops later. The conclusion is that if one delves into such particular causes without referring them to habitual patterns (majārī al-‘ādāt), no certain knowledge can be built, because the possibilities are endless. Hence this objection does not undermine rational certainty.

مجارى العادات
اليقين
العقل

البصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali denies all variation in faculties, claiming they are constant.
Correction: Ghazali acknowledges variation but argues it follows regular patterns (habit), which actually provides certainty.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does the variability of faculties not undermine certainty?
A) Because all faculties are constant.
B) Because the variability is limited.
C) Because he refers the matter to habitual patterns.
D) Because the intellect is not a faculty.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends certainty by showing that even variable faculties follow regular habitual patterns.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you notice your own mental faculties fading or growing—memory weakening, understanding deepening—do you trust that God's habitual way of creating these changes is wise, or do you let the unpredictability disturb your certainty in His order?
?
Ghazali says that if we don't refer matters to the course of habit, we cannot build reliable knowledge. In what area of your life are you allowing endless possibilities to shake your trust in God's consistent, habitual guidance—and what would it take to anchor yourself in His custom?
?
The analogy of gray hair reminds us that what is older often shows decline first. Are you willing to let your intellect—which matures slowly—guide you even as your physical senses weaken, or do you cling to youthful faculties as your source of certainty?

The Ninth Proof: Soul's Independence from the Body
Ghazali presents the ninth proof for the soul's independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the soul has an existence separate from the body, based on the observation that the body's material composition changes completely over a lifetime while personal identity and memory persist.

Core Idea
Your body is entirely replaced over time, yet you remain you—so your soul is not your body.

Ghazali presents a powerful philosophical proof derived from everyday experience: our bodies undergo constant material turnover, yet we clearly identify ourselves as the same person from childhood to adulthood, retaining memories and knowledge. This suggests that the true self is not the physical body but an immaterial soul that uses the body as an instrument. In this lesson, we will unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice how Ghazali describes the continuous dissolution and replacement of bodily parts, and how he connects this to the persistence of personal identity and knowledge.
Pay attention to the rhetorical question at the start: 'How can a human being be the body with its accidents?'
2
Analyze the empirical premise
Identify the key observation: from infancy to adulthood, the body's original parts are completely replaced by new matter from food. Ghazali uses the example of a child growing and changing over forty years.
This is a factual claim about biological change, not a theoretical assumption.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
Despite this complete material change, we still refer to the individual as the same person, and knowledge from early childhood remains. Ghazali argues that this continuity cannot be explained by the body alone, so there must be a distinct entity—the soul—that persists.
The body is only an instrument (āla); the soul is the real self.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل تاسع. قالوا : كيف يكون الإنسان عبارة عن الجسم مع عوارضه ؟! وهذه الأجسام لا تزال تنحل ، والغذاء يسد مسد ما ينحل ، حتى إذا رأينا صبيا انفصل من أمه ، يمرض مراراً ثم يذبل ، ثم يسمن وينمو ، فيمكننا أن نقول : لم يبق فيه بعد الأربعين شيء من الأجزاء التى كانت موجودة عند الانفصال ، بل كان أول وجوده من أجزاء المنى فقط ، ولم يبق فيه شيء من أجزاء المنى بل انحل كل ذلك وتبدل بغيره ، فيكون هذا الجسم غير ذلك الجسم ، ونقول : هذا الإنسان ، هو عين ذلك الإنسان ، حتى إنه يبقى معه علوم من أول صباه ، ويكون قد تبدل جميع أجسامه ، فدل أن للنفس وجوداً سوى البدن ، البدن آلته.
Ninth proof. They said: How can a human being be the body with its accidents?! These bodies continuously dissolve, and food replaces what dissolves, so that when we see an infant separated from his mother, he becomes ill repeatedly, then withers, then becomes fat and grows, so we can say: After forty there remains none of the parts that existed at separation; rather, his first existence was only from the parts of the semen, and none of the parts of the semen remain, but all that dissolved and changed to something else, so this body is not that body. Yet we say: This human being is the very same human being, so that knowledge from his early childhood remains with him, though all his body has changed. This indicates that the soul has an existence other than the body, and that the body is its instrument.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage contains the ninth proof (al-dalīl al-tāsi‘) for the soul's independence from the body. The argument proceeds from the observed fact that the human body undergoes complete material turnover over a lifetime. Starting from infancy, the body's parts are entirely replaced by new matter from food. Yet the individual is considered the same person, retaining knowledge and identity. Therefore, the soul (nafs) must have an existence separate from the body, with the body serving merely as its instrument (āla). This is a classical argument for the substantiality of the soul.

Key Terms

البدن
الآلة
الأجزاء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies any bodily identity or that the body matters for personal identity.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny that we have bodies; rather he affirms that the soul is the seat of personal identity, while the body is its instrument and changes over time.

Checkpoint
