B) The persistence of memory despite bodily change.
C) The soul's ability to think.
D) The separation at death.

Key Takeaway
The complete material turnover of the body over a lifetime, contrasted with the persistence of personal identity and memory, proves that the soul is an immaterial entity distinct from the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
As you look at your own body—every cell replaced over years—what remains of the 'you' that you were at age seven? If your soul is that constant, have you been feeding it with knowledge and righteousness, or only feeding the body that will be discarded?
?
Your childhood memories are still with you, yet your body is physically different. If your identity rests in the soul, why do you still act as if only the body's pleasures and pains matter? What would need to change in your daily priorities to align with this truth?
?
When you face the mirror, you see a body that will one day be completely replaced or decayed. But the soul you see with the eye of insight—is it growing or withering? Before this lesson ends, what is one specific step you will take today to nourish your soul instead of your body?

Objection and Response: Identity Through Change
Ghazali answers an objection from animal/tree identity and memory persistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defends the existence of a soul distinct from the body by responding to objections from bodily change and identity.

Core Idea
Identity through change is grounded in persisting original parts, not a separate soul.

Ghazali's philosophical proof for the soul faced a strong objection: if material change doesn't destroy identity for animals and trees, why posit a soul for humans? The objector also questions the memory argument. In this passage, Ghazali crafts a clever response, arguing that some original parts always remain, and illustrates with a water-mixing analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening lines to see the two prongs of the objection: animals/trees and memory persistence. Notice how the objector tries to undercut Ghazali's proof by analogy.
The objector claims that the same reasoning applies to non-human entities, yet we don't posit a soul for them.
2
Understand Ghazali's counter
Ghazali argues that it is not true that all parts of the brain/body change. Some original parts from the sperm remain throughout life, grounding identity.
He challenges the assumption of complete material turnover.
3
Analyze the water example
The analogy of adding and removing water from a jar illustrates how original matter can persist despite many changes. Consider how this supports Ghazali's position.
The water example shows that identity can be preserved by a persistent core, not necessarily a soul.
4
Connect to the soul
Reflect on why this defense strengthens the case for an immaterial soul. Ghazali shows that identity requires something beyond mere material continuity.
The persistence of original parts doesn't refute the soul; it shows that the objection fails to disprove it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض: أن هذا ينتقض بالبهيمة والشجرة ، إذا قيست حال كبرهما بحال الصغر ، فإنه يقال : إن هذا ذاك بعينه ، كما يقال في الإنسان وليس يدل ذلك ، على أن له وجوداً غير الجسم . وما ذكر في العلم يبطل بحفظ الصور المتخيلة ، فإنها تبقى في الصبيان إلى الكبر ، وإن تبدل سائر أجزاء الدماغ . فإن زعموا أنه لم يتبدل سائر أجزاء الدماغ ، فكذا سائر أجزاء القلب وهما من البدن ، فكيف يتصور أن يتبدل الجميع ؟ ! بل نقول : الإنسان وإن عاش مائة سنة مثلا ، فلابد أن يكون قد بقى فيه أجزاء من النطفة ، فأما أن تمحى عنه فلا ، فهو ذلك الإنسان باعتبار ما بقى كما أنه يقال : هذا ذاك الشجر ، وهذا ذاك الفرس ؛ ويكون بقاء المنى مع كثرة التحلل والتبدل . مثاله ما إذا صب فى موضع رطل من الماء ، ثم صب عليه رطل آخر ، حتى ثم أخذ منه رطل ، ثم صب عليه رطل آخر ، ثم أخذ منه رطل اختلط به .
The objection: That this is refuted by the animal and the tree, when comparing their state of bigness with the state of smallness, for it is said: 'This is that very one,' as is said of man, but that does not indicate that it has an existence other than the body. And what was mentioned about knowledge is invalidated by the preservation of imagined forms (al-ṣuwar al-mutakhayyila), for they remain in children until old age, even if all other parts of the brain change. For if they claim that not all parts of the brain change, then likewise all parts of the heart—and both are from the body—so how can it be imagined that all is changed?! Rather we say: Even if a man lives a hundred years, for example, there must remain in him some parts from the sperm; they are not completely effaced. So he is that man by virtue of what remains, just as it is said: 'This is that tree, this is that horse.' And the persistence of the sperm with much dissolution and change. Its example is when a pint of water is poured in a place, then another pint is poured into it until a pint is taken, then another pint is poured, then a pint is taken, and it is mixed.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This lesson contains both an objection to the ninth proof and Ghazali's response. The objector argues that the same reasoning applies to animals and trees: we say they are the same individual despite material change, yet we do not posit a soul for them. Also, the persistence of memories does not prove a soul because imagined forms might persist in the brain despite partial changes. Ghazali responds by arguing that it is not true that all parts of the brain change; some original parts remain. He then advances a positive argument: over a lifetime, some parts from the original sperm remain, ensuring identity. He illustrates with a water example: successive additions and removals can still leave some original water mixed in. Thus the identity is based on remaining original parts, not a separate soul. This refutes the objection that the soul is unnecessary.

الصور المتخيلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali denies any persistent bodily parts; he actually insists on the persistence of some original parts to ground identity.
Correction: Ghazali argues that some parts from the original sperm remain throughout life, ensuring identity.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali respond to the objection that animals and trees also preserve identity without a soul?
A) He denies animals have identity.
B) He says animals have souls too.
C) He argues that in humans some original parts persist.
D) He accepts the objection and revises his proof.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the objection by showing that in humans, original parts persist, and uses the water analogy to illustrate how identity can be preserved through material change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own life, what original 'parts' from your beginning have remained? What does Ghazali's insistence on persistence teach you about your own identity and accountability to God?
?
Ghazali argues that not all parts change. How does this affect your view of your body as an amanah from Allah? Are you treating the body as a temporary vessel or as something with lasting significance?
?
Reflect on the analogy of water: what 'original drop' from your birth remains? How should this shape your awareness of your own history and your ultimate return to Allah?

First Objection: The Soul's Survival Depends on an Unproven Premise
Ghazali gives the first objection: the argument depends on an unsupported premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection Ghazali raises against the philosophers' claim that the soul does not die with the body.

Core Idea
The soul's survival is based on an unproven premise about the soul not being a state in a body.

Ghazali begins his critique by pointing out that the philosophers' argument for the soul's immortality rests on a crucial premise: that the soul is not a state (ḥāllah) in a body. This premise was disputed in an earlier discussion, and Ghazali states that it is not conceded. This short objection is a powerful reminder that theological arguments often depend on foundational assumptions that may themselves be questionable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the concise style of the objection.
2
Identify the premise
The objection is built on the idea that the soul is not a state (ḥāllah) in a body. Understand what 'state in a body' means—a quality or accident that inheres in a body.
The philosophers claim the soul is a substance, not an accident.
3
Understand the dependency
Ghazali says the whole argument for the soul's survival depends on this premise. If the premise is not accepted, the conclusion does not follow.
This is a common method in disputation: showing an argument's reliance on an unproven assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض عليه من وجوه : الأول : أنه بناء على أن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن ، لأنها ليست حالة في جسم ، وهو بناء على المسألة الأولى ، فقد لا نسلم ذلك الثاني : أنه مع أنها لا تحل البدن عندهم ، فلها علاقة بالبدن ، حتى لم تحدث إلا بحدوث البدن ، وهذا ما اختاره ابن سينا والمحققون منهم وأنكروا على أفلاطن قوله : إن النفس قديمة ، ويعرض لها الاشتغال بالأبدان ، بمسلك برهاني محقق أن النفوس قبل الأبدان ، إن كانت واحدة ، فكيف انقسمت ؟ ! وهو ما لا حجم له ولا مقدار ، لا يعقل انقسامه ، وإن زعم أنها لم تنقسم فهو محال ، إذ يعلم ضرورة أن نفس زيد ، غير نفس عمرو ، ولو كانت واحدة ، لكانت معلومات زيد ، معلومة لعمرو ، فإن العلم من صفات ذات النفس ، وصفات الذات تدخل مع الذات فى كل إضافة ، وإن كانت النفوس متكثرة فماذا تكثرت ؟! ولم تتكثر بالمواد ، ولا بالأماكن ، ولا بالأزمنة ، ولا بالصفات ؛ إذ ليس فيها ما يوجب اختلاف الصفة . بخلاف النفوس بعد موت البدن ، فإنها تتكثر باختلاف الصفات ، عند من يرى بقاءها ، لأنها استفادت من الأبدان هيئات مختلفة ، لا تتماثل نفسان منها ، فإن هيئاتها تحصل من الأخلاق ، والأخلاق لا تتماثل قط ، كما أن الخلق الظاهر لا يتماثل قط ولو تماثل لاشتبه علينا زيد بعمرو
And the objection to it from aspects: First: that it is based on the soul not dying with the death of the body, because it is not a state (ḥāllah) in a body, and it is based on the first issue, and we may not accept that. Second: that even though it does not inhabit (taḥullu) the body according to them, it has a relation (‘alāqah) to the body, such that it did not originate except with the origination of the body, and this is what Ibn Sīnā and the verifiers among them chose, and they denied against Plato his statement that the soul is eternal (qadīmah) and that it happens upon it the occupation with bodies, by a verified demonstrative path (maslak burhānī muḥaqqaq) that: the souls before bodies, if they were one, how did they become divided? And it is what has no size (ḥajm) nor magnitude (miqdār), its division is not conceivable, and if he claims that it did not divide, then it is impossible (muḥāl), for it is known necessarily that the soul of Zayd is not the soul of ‘Amr, and if it were one, then Zayd's knowledge would be known to ‘Amr, for knowledge is among the attributes of the soul's essence, and attributes of the essence enter with the essence in every relation. And if the souls are multiple, then from what did they become multiple? And they did not become multiple by matters (māddah), nor by places (amākin), nor by times (azminah), nor by attributes (ṣifāt), for there is nothing in them that necessitates difference in attribute. Unlike the souls after the death of the body, for they become multiple by difference of attributes, according to those who see their survival, because they acquired from the bodies different dispositions (hay’āt), such that no two souls are similar, for their dispositions arise from character traits (akhlāq), and character traits are never similar, just as the outward character (khalq ẓāhir) is never similar, and if they were similar, Zayd would be confused with ‘Amr for us.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the philosophers' claim that the soul does not die with the body. The first objection is that this claim depends on the idea that the soul is not a state (ḥāllah) in a body, which is the first issue (i.e., the previous discussion), and that is not conceded. The second objection is that even if the soul does not inhabit the body, it has a relation to the body, such that it originates only with the body's origination—this is the view of Ibn Sīnā and the verifiers, who reject Plato's view of the soul's pre-existence. They provide a demonstrative proof: if souls were one before bodies, how could they become multiple? Something with no size or magnitude cannot be divided. If one claims they did not divide, that is impossible because we know necessarily that Zayd's soul is different from ‘Amr's. If they were one, their knowledge would be the same. If souls are multiple, what made them multiple? They cannot be multiplied by matter, place, time, or attributes, because there is nothing in them to necessitate difference in attributes. In contrast, souls after the death of the body become multiple by different attributes acquired from bodies, such as character traits, which are never similar, just as outward appearances are never similar.

Key Terms
حال (ḥāllah)
نفس (nafs)
بدن (badan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the premise that Ghazali says is not conceded?
A) The soul is a body.
B) The soul is not a state in a body.
C) The soul dies with the body.
D) The soul is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The first objection shows that the philosophers' argument for the soul's survival depends on a premise that may be rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief about the afterlife without examining the foundational premises it rests on? This objection shows that an entire argument can collapse if its starting point is not conceded. What is one belief you hold that needs closer scrutiny, and when will you examine it?
?
The philosophers' argument for the soul's survival failed because it relied on an unproven premise. In your own life, are there any assumptions about your faith that you have not critically examined—not to doubt, but to strengthen? Will you take time today to ask, 'What is my evidence for this?'

Second Objection: The Proof Against Pre-Existence of the Soul
Ghazali presents the second objection and the proof against the soul's pre-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second objection and the demonstrative proof against the soul's pre-existence.

Core Idea
If souls were one before bodies, they could not become multiple and distinct.

Ghazali's second objection goes deeper, engaging with the philosophers' own view that the soul has a relation to the body. He presents a proof, attributed to Ibn Sīnā and the verifiers, against Plato's idea that the soul pre-exists. The proof shows that if all souls were originally one, they could not have become multiple without some principle of individuation. Ghazali then explains how souls become distinct after death through acquired dispositions.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the structure: first the philosophers' view, then the proof, then the alternative explanation.
2
Understand the proof against pre-existence
The proof asks: if souls were one before bodies, how did they become multiple? Something without size or magnitude cannot be divided. And if they remained one, then all souls would share the same knowledge, which is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Consider the principle of individuation
The proof also argues that if souls are multiple, what made them multiple? Not matter, place, time, or attributes, since they lack these. So pre-bodily multiplicity is impossible.
This shows the philosophers' own criteria for individuation.
4
Examine the post-mortem multiplicity
After death, souls are distinguished by dispositions (hay’āt) acquired from the body, like character traits (akhlāq), which are never identical.
This is consistent with the view that the soul survives but becomes distinct through its earthly experiences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What is the key argument against souls being one before bodies?

Key Takeaway
The second objection provides a rigorous proof that the soul cannot pre-exist the body, because pre-bodily multiplicity is impossible without individuating principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul only becomes uniquely distinct through the body you inhabit, how does that change the way you view your physical existence? Are you using your body as a means to cultivate a character that will distinguish your soul for eternity, or are you neglecting this responsibility?
?
Ghazali’s proof shows that before bodies, souls are unified and cannot be multiple. How does this confront your sense of individual identity? Do you take pride in being 'different' from others, yet realize that your difference is entirely shaped by your body and actions? What does that mean for how you treat the similarities you share with others?
?
The argument says that if souls were one, knowledge would be shared. Have you ever felt a deep, unspoken understanding with another person that seemed to transcend individual minds? Does this proof challenge you to see that true unity of knowledge is only possible through a shared essential attribute, not through physical means? How can you pursue a unity of hearts and minds in your community, knowing that your separate souls are only temporarily distinct?

The Third Objection: Annihilation by Divine Power
Ghazali presents the third objection: annihilation by God's power alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the philosophers' enumeration of annihilation: that God can directly annihilate the soul without any secondary cause.

Core Idea
God's power alone suffices to annihilate anything, bypassing all natural causes.

Sanusi now presents the third objection to the philosophers' claim that the soul cannot be annihilated. The philosophers had enumerated several ways non-existence occurs, all requiring a cause or change. Ghazali counters simply but powerfully: why not direct annihilation by God's will? This objection draws on a principle already established in the discussion on the eternity of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ghazali frames it as 'not far-fetched' (لا يبعد) – he is asserting its plausibility, not proving it from scratch.
The passage references a previous discussion (قررناه). If you've studied that, recall the argument.
2
Understand the logical challenge
Ghazali is pointing out that the philosophers' enumeration of ways of annihilation is incomplete because it omits the direct power of God. This is a theological objection based on divine omnipotence.
3
Connect to the eternity of the world debate
The phrase 'as we established in the issue of the eternity of the world' indicates that Ghazali previously argued that God's power is unrestricted by natural or logical necessity. Recall that point to see the consistency.
This objection relies on a broader theological framework.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this objection matters: if accepted, it collapses the philosophers' argument that the soul must be immortal because it cannot be annihilated by their listed means.
It shows that philosophical reasoning must account for divine agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثالث، هو أنه لا يبعد أن يقال : تنعدم بقدرة الله تعالى قررناه في مسألة سرمدية العالم كما
The third objection: it is not far-fetched to say: it becomes non-existent by the power of God, as we established in the issue of the eternity of the world.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection to the philosophers' argument for the immortality of the soul. The objection is that the soul could simply be annihilated by God's direct power, without needing any of the enumerated ways of non-existence that the philosophers propose. Ghazali references his earlier discussion on the eternity of the world where this point was established. The objection thus challenges the completeness of the philosophers' classification of annihilation.

Key Terms
قدرة الله (power of God)
عدم (non-existence)
سرمدية العالم (eternity of the world)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ghazali is merely asserting divine intervention without argument.
Correction: He is instead pointing to a recognized theological principle: God's omnipotence is a sufficient cause for annihilation, making the philosophers' enumeration incomplete.

Checkpoint

A) The soul cannot be annihilated because it is simple.
B) The soul might be annihilated by God's power without needing any other cause.
C) The enumeration of ways of non-existence is incomplete.
D) The soul is material and thus subject to decay.

Key Takeaway
God's direct power can annihilate anything, rendering the philosophers' enumeration of annihilation causes incomplete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you sin, do you assume you will have time to repent, or do you realize that annihilation is always a possibility? What step will you take today to turn back to God before it is too late?
?
This objection shows that God's power is beyond all human categories. Does that make you fear Him more, or trust Him more? What needs to change in your heart so that both fear and hope are balanced?

The Fourth Objection: Incomplete Enumeration
Ghazali objects that the three ways of non-existence are not exhaustive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's objection that the philosophers' enumeration of the ways of non-existence is not proven to be exhaustive.

Core Idea
Enumeration without demonstration is not proof.

In this passage, Ghazali launches his fourth objection against the philosophers' argument for the soul's immortality. He concedes that they have identified three ways of non-existence, but he challenges the assumption that these are the only possibilities. This objection highlights a fundamental requirement of logical proof: that any division must be shown to be exhaustive.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Ghazali begins by conceding ('wa huwa musallam') before challenging the philosophers.
2
Identify the key logical move
Find where Ghazali asks for the proof ('ma al-dalil') that non-existence cannot occur except through these three ways. This is the heart of the objection.
3
Understand the principle of exhaustive division
Ghazali states that if the division is not exhaustive between negation and affirmation, it could exceed three or four. Consider what makes a division logically complete.
An exhaustive division covers all possibilities without overlap.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that confining the ways to three is not known by demonstration (burhan). Ask yourself: what would constitute a demonstration that the list is complete?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الرابع، هو أن يقال : ذكرتم أن هذه الطرق الثلاث في العدم، وهو مسلم، فما الدليل على أن عدم الشيء، لا يتصور إلا بطريق من هذه الطرق الثلاث ؟ ! فإن التقسيم إذا لم يكن دائراً بين النفى والإثبات فلا يبعد أن يزيد على الثلاث والأربع، فلعل للعدم طريقاً رابعاً، وخامساً، سوى ما ذكرتموه ، فحصر الطرق فى هذه الثلاث غير معلوم بالبرهان.
The fourth objection: it may be said: you have mentioned that these three ways are [the ways] of non-existence, and that is granted. So what is the proof that the non-existence of a thing is not conceivable except by one of these three ways? For if the division is not exhaustive between negation and affirmation, it is not unlikely that it could exceed three or four. Perhaps non-existence has a fourth way, and a fifth, besides what you have mentioned. So confining the ways to these three is not known by demonstration.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection to the philosophers' argument. Ghazali concedes that the philosophers have enumerated three ways in which something can become non-existent. However, he challenges the claim that this enumeration is exhaustive. He argues that unless the division is logically complete (covering both affirmation and negation), it is possible that there are additional ways beyond the three or four mentioned. The objection is that the philosophers have not provided a demonstration (burhan) proving that no other ways exist. Therefore, the conclusion that the soul cannot be annihilated because it does not fit into these three ways is unsupported.

Key Terms
طرق العدم
حصر
برهان
تقسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ghazali is denying the possibility of an exhaustive division altogether.
Correction: Ghazali is not denying exhaustive division; he is requiring a proof that the division is exhaustive. The objection is about the lack of demonstration, not the impossibility of completeness.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the fourth objection?
A) The three ways of non-existence are contradictory.
B) The enumeration of ways of non-existence is not proven to be exhaustive.
C) The soul is not subject to any of the three ways.
D) The philosophers have not proven that the soul exists.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's fourth objection underscores that an argument based on an unproven enumeration is not a valid demonstration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume that the options in front of you are the only possibilities, have you ever stopped to ask, 'What proof do I have that this list is complete?' Think of a major decision you are facing. Write down one assumption you are making about the available paths. Now, demand from yourself the same rigor Ghazali demands: is that assumption proven, or simply inherited? Commit to investigating at least one hidden possibility today.
?
Your certainty about the afterlife—does it rest on a demonstration, or on an enumeration that feels complete? Reflect on the basis of your hope. If the philosophers' argument for the soul's immortality is vulnerable to Ghazali's critique, what about your own inner conviction? Is it built on a proof that can withstand the same questioning? Spend a moment in sincere self-examination and ask Allah to grant you a faith rooted in truth, not assumption.
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly exhaustive list can be incomplete. What if your understanding of the states of the heart—like sincerity, hypocrisy, or love for Allah—is similarly limited to a few categories? Could there be a 'fourth way' of spiritual reality that you have ignored because it didn't fit your mental map? Ask yourself: What am I failing to consider because it doesn't fall into my habitual three boxes? Pray for the humility to accept that 'there is no proof of completeness.'

The Second Proof: Annihilation Requires Composition
Ghazali presents the philosophers' argument linking annihilation to composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that any annihilated thing must be composed of a part that perishes and a part that remains to bear the potentiality of non-existence.

Core Idea
Annihilation implies composition; simple things cannot be annihilated.

This passage presents the core of the philosophers' second proof for the soul's immortality. They argue that for anything to be annihilated, it must have a potentiality for non-existence that inheres in a permanent substrate—just as existence requires a matter that receives forms. This argument sets up a crucial premise: the soul, being simple, lacks such composition and therefore cannot cease to exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's opening claim
Start by reading from 'دليل ثان' to 'فيستحيل عليه العدم'. Notice the strong claim: every substance not in a subject cannot be annihilated.
This is the conclusion they aim to prove.
2
Follow the potentiality analogy
Read the section comparing possibility of existence and non-existence. Understand that both require a prior potentiality that inheres in something.
The key is that potentiality (قوة) is relational and needs a subject.
3
Analyze the matter-form analogy
Read the part where they say the receiver of non-existence must remain, like matter remains when forms change. This leads to composition.
If something is annihilated, it must have a part that perishes and a part that remains—contradicting simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ثان، وعليه تعويلهم وعليه تعويلهم، أن قالوا : كل جوهر ليس في محل فيستحيل عليه العدم، بل البسائط لا تنعدم قط، وهذا الدليل، يثبت فيه أولا أن انعدام البدن، لا يوجب انعدام النفس لما سبق، فبعد ذلك يقال : يستحيل أن ينعدم بسبب آخر، لأن كل ما ينعدم بسبب ما، ــ أي سبب كان ــ ففيه قوة الفساد، قبل الفساد، أى إمكان العدم سابق على العدم، كما أن ما يطرأ وجوده من الحوادث، فيكون إمكان الوجود سابقاً على الوجود، ويسمى إمكان الوجود قوة الوجود، وإمكان العدم قوة الفساد، وكما أن إمكان الوجود وصف إضافي، لا يقوم إلا بشيء، حتى يكون إمكاناً بالإضافة إليه، فكذلك إمكان العدم؛ ولذلك قيل : إن كل حادث، مفتقر إلى مادة سابقة، يكون فيها إمكان وجود الحادث وقوته، كما سبق في مسألة قدم العالم، فالمادة التي فيها قوة الوجود، قابلة للوجود الطارئ، والقابل غير المقبول، فيكون القابل موجوداً مع المقبول عند طريانه، وهو غيره، فكذلك قابل العدم، ينبغي أن يكون كما وجد فيه شيء، ويكون موجوداً عند طريان العدم، حتى يعدم منه شيء، ما عدم غير ما بقى، ويكون ما بقى هو الذى فيه قوة العدم، وقبوله وإمكانه، كما أن ما بقى عند طريان الوجود، يكون غير ما طرأ، وقد كان ما فيه قوة قبول الطارئ. فيلزم أن يكون الشيء الذي طرأ عليه العدم، مركباً من شيء انعدم، ومن قابل للعدم بقى مع طريان العدم، وقد كان هو حامل قوة العدم، قبل طريان العدم ويكون حامل قوة العدم كالمادة، والمنعدم منها كالصورة.

فإن النفس بسيطة، وهى صورة مجردة عن المادة، لا تركيب فيها، فرض فيها تركيب من صورة ومادة، فنحن ننقل البيان إلى المادة، التي هي السنخ (1) والأصل الأول، إذ لا بد أن ينتهى إلى أصل، فنحيل العدم على ذلك الأصل، وهو المسمى نفساً، كما نحيل العدم على مادة الأجسام، فإنها أزلية أبدية، وإنما تحدث عليها الصور، وتنعدم منها الصور، وفيها قوة طريان الصور عليها، وقوة طريان انعدام الصور منها، فإنها قابلة للضدين على السواء . وقد ظهر من هذا أن كل موجود أحد الذات، يستحيل عليه العدم.

ويمكن تفهيم هذا بصيغة أخرى، وهى أن قوة الوجود للشيء تكون قبل وجود الشيء، فتكون لغير ذلك الشيء، ولا يكون نفس قوة الوجود. بيانه. أن الصحيح البصر، يقال : إنه بصير بالقوة، أى فيه قوة الإبصار، ومعناه أن الصفة التي لابد منها في العين، ليصح الإبصار، موجودة، فإن تأخر الإبصار، فلتأخر شرط آخر، فتكون قوة الإبصار للسواد مثلا، موجودة للعين قبل إبصار السواد بالفعل، فإن حصل إبصار السواد بالفعل، لم تكن قوة إبصار ذلك السواد موجودة عند وجود ذلك الإبصار، إذ لا يمكن أن يقال : مهما حصل الإبصار، فهو مع كونه موجوداً بالفعل، موجود بالقوة، بل قوة الوجود، لا تضام حقيقة الوجود الحاصل بالفعل أبداً.

وإذا ثبتت هذه المقدمة فنقول : لو انعدم الشيء البسيط، لكان إمكان (1) السنخ هو الأصل.
A second proof, and upon it they rely, that they said: Every substance not in a subject is impossible to be non-existent; indeed, simple things are never annihilated. This proof first establishes that the annihilation of the body does not necessitate the annihilation of the soul, as previously. Then it is said: It is impossible that it be annihilated by another cause, because everything that is annihilated by a cause — whatever cause — has in it a potentiality of corruption before corruption, i.e., possibility of non-existence preceding non-existence, just as what comes into existence among accidents has possibility of existence preceding existence, and possibility of existence is called potentiality of existence, and possibility of non-existence potentiality of corruption. And just as possibility of existence is a relational attribute that subsists only in something such that it is a possibility relative to it, likewise possibility of non-existence. Therefore it was said: Every contingent event depends on a prior matter in which is the possibility of existence of the event and its potentiality, as previously in the issue of the eternity of the world. So the matter in which is the potentiality of existence is receptive to the accidental existence, and the receiver is not the received, so the receiver exists with the received when it occurs, and it is other than it. Likewise the receiver of non-existence should be as something in which a thing exists, and it exists at the occurrence of non-existence, so that something perishes from it, and what perishes is other than what remains, and what remains is that in which is the potentiality of non-existence, its receptivity, and its possibility, just as what remains at the occurrence of existence is other than what occurred, and that which has the potentiality of receiving the accidental was before. So it follows that the thing which non-existence befell is composed of something that perished and a receiver of non-existence that remained with the occurrence of non-existence, and it was the bearer of the potentiality of non-existence before the occurrence of non-existence, and the bearer of the potentiality of non-existence is like matter, and the perished from it is like form.

But the soul is simple, and it is a form abstracted from matter, having no composition. If composition of form and matter were assumed in it, we transfer the demonstration to the matter, which is the root and first principle, for it must end with a principle. Then we attribute non-existence to that principle, which is called soul, just as we attribute non-existence to the matter of bodies, for it is eternal perpetually, and only forms occur upon it and forms become non-existent from it, and in it is the potentiality of occurrence of forms upon it and the potentiality of occurrence of non-existence of forms from it, for it is receptive to both opposites equally. And from this it has appeared that every existent of simple essence is impossible to be non-existent.

And this can be made understandable in another formulation, namely that the potentiality of existence of a thing is before the existence of the thing, so it belongs to other than that thing, and it is not the same as the potentiality of existence. Its explanation: The sound-sighted person is said to be sighted potentially, i.e., in him is the potentiality of sight, and its meaning is that the attribute necessary in the eye for sight to be sound exists. If sight is delayed, it is due to the delay of another condition. So the potentiality of sight for blackness, for example, exists in the eye before actually seeing blackness. If actual seeing of blackness occurs, the potentiality of sight for that blackness is not present at the existence of that sight, for it cannot be said that whenever sight occurs, it is, while being actual, also potential. Rather, the potentiality of never coexists with the actual existence that has occurred.

And when this premise is established, we say: If the simple thing were annihilated, the possibility (1) al-sinkh is the root.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' second proof for the immortality of the soul. The proof argues that every simple substance (not in a subject) cannot be annihilated. The reasoning is that annihilation requires composition: something that is annihilated must have a potentiality for non-existence before it occurs, and that potentiality must inhere in something that remains after the annihilation, just as the potentiality for existence inheres in matter that remains after the form is acquired. Therefore, the thing that is annihilated must be composed of a part that perishes (like form) and a part that remains (like matter). But the soul is simple and has no composition. Even if one assumes composition in the soul, the demonstration reduces to the underlying principle (the soul itself), which would then be eternal and indestructible, like the matter of bodies. The proof concludes that every existent with a simple essence cannot be annihilated. Ghazali then provides an alternative explanation using the example of sight: the potentiality for sight exists before actual sight and does not coexist with it; similarly, potentiality for non-existence cannot coexist with actual existence. Therefore, if a simple thing were annihilated, its potentiality for non-existence would have to precede it, but since it is simple and has no parts, it cannot have such a potentiality in itself.

Key Terms
جوهر ليس في محل
بسائط
قوة الفساد
إمكان العدم
قوة الوجود
مادة
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Potentiality of non-existence is a real attribute that can exist without a substrate.
Correction: The argument insists that potentiality is relational and must inhere in something. Without a permanent subject, potentiality cannot be sustained.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what is necessary for something to be annihilated?
A) It must have a potentiality for non-existence that inheres in a permanent subject.
B) It must be caused by God's direct act.
C) It must be composed of matter and form.
D) It must be a simple substance.

Key Takeaway
Annihilation requires composition: a part that perishes and a part that remains to hold the potentiality of non-existence; therefore, simple substances cannot be annihilated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is a simple substance as the philosophers claim, then it cannot be annihilated. But do you live as if your soul is eternal, or do your daily choices reflect that you will perish? What needs to change in your priorities to align with this belief?
?
The argument depends on the concept of potentiality and composition. When you reflect on your own mortality, do you think of yourself as a composite being? What part of you do you believe will remain after death, and why? Are you willing to be honest about whether that belief comes from revelation or from reasoning like this?
?
The philosophers say that simple things cannot be annihilated because they lack composition. But what if God does not follow this logical necessity? Do you trust your own reason more than God's power? How does that tension make you feel, and what does it reveal about your reliance on rational proofs in matters of faith?

intermediate

Applying the Proof to the Soul
Ghazali applies the composition argument to the soul to prove its immortality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers apply the composition argument to the soul, concluding that it is simple and thus indestructible.

Core Idea
The soul, being simple and abstract, cannot be annihilated.

Having established that annihilation requires composition, the philosophers now apply this to the soul. The soul is simple—a form abstracted from matter with no parts. Even if one assumes composition, the underlying principle remains eternal. This lesson focuses on the direct application of the earlier principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the application to the soul
Read from 'فإن النفس بسيطة' to 'يستحيل عليه العدم'. Notice how they state the soul's simplicity and the impossibility of composition.
The soul is described as a form abstracted from matter.
2
Follow the reduction argument
Even if we suppose the soul has composition, the argument reduces to a root principle that cannot be annihilated, just like the matter of bodies.
This shows the argument is robust against objections.
3
Conclude the indestructibility
The final sentence: 'كل موجود أحد الذات يستحيل عليه العدم'—every existent of simple essence cannot be annihilated.
This is the conclusion of the proof.

فإن النفس بسيطة، وهى صورة مجردة عن المادة، لا تركيب فيها، فرض فيها تركيب من صورة ومادة، فنحن ننقل البيان إلى المادة، التي هي السنخ (1) والأصل الأول، إذ لا بد أن ينتهى إلى أصل، فنحيل العدم على ذلك الأصل، وهو المسمى نفساً، كما نحيل العدم على مادة الأجسام، فإنها أزلية أبدية، وإنما تحدث عليها الصور، وتنعدم منها الصور، وفيها قوة طريان الصور عليها، وقوة طريان انعدام الصور منها، فإنها قابلة للضدين على السواء . وقد ظهر من هذا أن كل موجود أحد الذات، يستحيل عليه العدم.

ويمكن تفهيم هذا بصيغة أخرى، وهى أن قوة الوجود للشيء تكون قبل وجود الشيء، فتكون لغير ذلك الشيء، ولا يكون نفس قوة الوجود. بيانه. أن الصحيح البصر، يقال : إنه بصير بالقوة، أى فيه قوة الإبصار، ومعناه أن الصفة التي لابد منها في العين، ليصح الإبصار، موجودة، فإن تأخر الإبصار، فلتأخر شرط آخر، فتكون قوة الإبصار للسواد مثلا، موجودة للعين قبل إبصار السواد بالفعل، فإن حصل إبصار السواد بالفعل، لم تكن قوة إبصار ذلك السواد موجودة عند وجود ذلك الإبصار، إذ لا يمكن أن يقال : مهما حصل الإبصار، فهو مع كونه موجوداً بالفعل، موجود بالقوة، بل قوة الوجود، لا تضام حقيقة الوجود الحاصل بالفعل أبداً.

وإذا ثبتت هذه المقدمة فنقول : لو انعدم الشيء البسيط، لكان إمكان (1) السنخ هو الأصل.
A second proof, and upon it they rely, that they said: Every substance not in a subject is impossible to be non-existent; indeed, simple things are never annihilated. This proof first establishes that the annihilation of the body does not necessitate the annihilation of the soul, as previously. Then it is said: It is impossible that it be annihilated by another cause, because everything that is annihilated by a cause — whatever cause — has in it a potentiality of corruption before corruption, i.e., possibility of non-existence preceding non-existence, just as what comes into existence among accidents has possibility of existence preceding existence, and possibility of existence is called potentiality of existence, and possibility of non-existence potentiality of corruption. And just as possibility of existence is a relational attribute that subsists only in something such that it is a possibility relative to it, likewise possibility of non-existence. Therefore it was said: Every contingent event depends on a prior matter in which is the possibility of existence of the event and its potentiality, as previously in the issue of the eternity of the world. So the matter in which is the potentiality of existence is receptive to the accidental existence, and the receiver is not the received, so the receiver exists with the received when it occurs, and it is other than it. Likewise the receiver of non-existence should be as something in which a thing exists, and it exists at the occurrence of non-existence, so that something perishes from it, and what perishes is other than what remains, and what remains is that in which is the potentiality of non-existence, its receptivity, and its possibility, just as what remains at the occurrence of existence is other than what occurred, and that which has the potentiality of receiving the accidental was before. So it follows that the thing which non-existence befell is composed of something that perished and a receiver of non-existence that remained with the occurrence of non-existence, and it was the bearer of the potentiality of non-existence before the occurrence of non-existence, and the bearer of the potentiality of non-existence is like matter, and the perished from it is like form.

But the soul is simple, and it is a form abstracted from matter, having no composition. If composition of form and matter were assumed in it, we transfer the demonstration to the matter, which is the root and first principle, for it must end with a principle. Then we attribute non-existence to that principle, which is called soul, just as we attribute non-existence to the matter of bodies, for it is eternal perpetually, and only forms occur upon it and forms become non-existent from it, and in it is the potentiality of occurrence of forms upon it and the potentiality of occurrence of non-existence of forms from it, for it is receptive to both opposites equally. And from this it has appeared that every existent of simple essence is impossible to be non-existent.

And this can be made understandable in another formulation, namely that the potentiality of existence of a thing is before the existence of the thing, so it belongs to other than that thing, and it is not the same as the potentiality of existence. Its explanation: The sound-sighted person is said to be sighted potentially, i.e., in him is the potentiality of sight, and its meaning is that the attribute necessary in the eye for sight to be sound exists. If sight is delayed, it is due to the delay of another condition. So the potentiality of sight for blackness, for example, exists in the eye before actually seeing blackness. If actual seeing of blackness occurs, the potentiality of sight for that blackness is not present at the existence of that sight, for it cannot be said that whenever sight occurs, it is, while being actual, also potential. Rather, the potentiality of never coexists with the actual existence that has occurred.

And when this premise is established, we say: If the simple thing were annihilated, the possibility (1) al-sinkh is the root.

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' second proof for the immortality of the soul. The proof argues that every simple substance (not in a subject) cannot be annihilated. The reasoning is that annihilation requires composition: something that is annihilated must have a potentiality for non-existence before it occurs, and that potentiality must inhere in something that remains after the annihilation, just as the potentiality for existence inheres in matter that remains after the form is acquired. Therefore, the thing that is annihilated must be composed of a part that perishes (like form) and a part that remains (like matter). But the soul is simple and has no composition. Even if one assumes composition in the soul, the demonstration reduces to the underlying principle (the soul itself), which would then be eternal and indestructible, like the matter of bodies. The proof concludes that every existent with a simple essence cannot be annihilated. Ghazali then provides an alternative explanation using the example of sight: the potentiality for sight exists before actual sight and does not coexist with it; similarly, potentiality for non-existence cannot coexist with actual existence. Therefore, if a simple thing were annihilated, its potentiality for non-existence would have to precede it, but since it is simple and has no parts, it cannot have such a potentiality in itself.

Checkpoint
Why does the simplicity of the soul guarantee its indestructibility according to the philosophers?
A) Because simple things cannot be divided, and annihilation requires division.
B) Because annihilation requires composition of a part that perishes and a part that remains, which a simple substance lacks.
C) Because the soul is made of a special immortal material.
D) Because God has decreed the soul to be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The soul, being simple and without composition, cannot be annihilated because annihilation requires a permanent subject that remains—a condition simplicity cannot fulfill.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the soul is truly simple and cannot be annihilated, what does that imply about the permanence of your spiritual state? Are you living as if your soul is imperishable, or as if it is temporary like your body?
?
The proof argues that composition allows change and potential for non-existence, but the soul, being simple, has no such potential. In what specific ways are you still acting as if your soul is changeable or perishable?
?
Given that simplicity implies impossibility of non-existence, how should this truth change your relationship with your soul today? What practical step can you take right now to honor its eternal nature?

Alternative Formulation: Potentiality Cannot Coexist with Actuality
Ghazali provides an alternative explanation using potentiality and actuality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second formulation using the example of sight to show that potentiality for non-existence cannot coexist with actual existence, reinforcing the soul's indestructibility.

Core Idea
Potentiality for a state cannot coexist with that state in actuality.

The philosophers provide an alternative explanation for their proof using a concrete example: the potentiality for sight. Before seeing blackness, the eye has the potentiality; once actual vision occurs, the potentiality for that particular sight is gone. Similarly, if a simple thing exists, it cannot have potentiality for non-existence. This lesson unpacks that analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative formulation
Read from 'ويمكن تفهيم هذا بصيغة أخرى' to the end. Understand the premise about potentiality for sight.
This part starts with 'ويمكن تفهيم' indicating an alternative explanation.
2
Grasp the sight analogy
The example: a person with healthy eyes has potentiality for sight before seeing; once they actually see blackness, the potentiality for that blackness is no longer present.
Potentiality and actuality for the same thing are mutually exclusive.
3
Apply to annihilation
If a simple thing were annihilated, it would need potentiality for non-existence before it exists, but since it already exists, such potentiality cannot coexist with its actual existence.
Contradiction implies the simple thing cannot be annihilated.

دليل ثان، وعليه تعويلهم وعليه تعويلهم، أن قالوا : كل جوهر ليس في محل فيستحيل عليه العدم، بل البسائط لا تنعدم قط، وهذا الدليل، يثبت فيه أولا أن انعدام البدن، لا يوجب انعدام النفس لما سبق، فبعد ذلك يقال : يستحيل أن ينعدم بسبب آخر، لأن كل ما ينعدم بسبب ما، ــ أي سبب كان ــ ففيه قوة الفساد، قبل الفساد، أى إمكان العدم سابق على العدم، كما أن ما يطرأ وجوده من الحوادث، فيكون إمكان الوجود سابقاً على الوجود، ويسمى إمكان الوجود قوة الوجود، وإمكان العدم قوة الفساد، وكما أن إمكان الوجود وصف إضافي، لا يقوم إلا بشيء، حتى يكون إمكاناً بالإضافة إليه، فكذلك إمكان العدم؛ ولذلك قيل : إن كل حادث، مفتقر إلى مادة سابقة، يكون فيها إمكان وجود الحادث وقوته، كما سبق في مسألة قدم العالم، فالمادة التي فيها قوة الوجود، قابلة للوجود الطارئ، والقابل غير المقبول، فيكون القابل موجوداً مع المقبول عند طريانه، وهو غيره، فكذلك قابل العدم، ينبغي أن يكون كما وجد فيه شيء، ويكون موجوداً عند طريان العدم، حتى يعدم منه شيء، ما عدم غير ما بقى، ويكون ما بقى هو الذى فيه قوة العدم، وقبوله وإمكانه، كما أن ما بقى عند طريان الوجود، يكون غير ما طرأ، وقد كان ما فيه قوة قبول الطارئ. فيلزم أن يكون الشيء الذي طرأ عليه العدم، مركباً من شيء انعدم، ومن قابل للعدم بقى مع طريان العدم، وقد كان هو حامل قوة العدم، قبل طريان العدم ويكون حامل قوة العدم كالمادة، والمنعدم منها كالصورة.

فإن النفس بسيطة، وهى صورة مجردة عن المادة، لا تركيب فيها، فرض فيها تركيب من صورة ومادة، فنحن ننقل البيان إلى المادة، التي هي السنخ (1) والأصل الأول، إذ لا بد أن ينتهى إلى أصل، فنحيل العدم على ذلك الأصل، وهو المسمى نفساً، كما نحيل العدم على مادة الأجسام، فإنها أزلية أبدية، وإنما تحدث عليها الصور، وتنعدم منها الصور، وفيها قوة طريان الصور عليها، وقوة طريان انعدام الصور منها، فإنها قابلة للضدين على السواء . وقد ظهر من هذا أن كل موجود أحد الذات، يستحيل عليه العدم.

ويمكن تفهيم هذا بصيغة أخرى، وهى أن قوة الوجود للشيء تكون قبل وجود الشيء، فتكون لغير ذلك الشيء، ولا يكون نفس قوة الوجود. بيانه. أن الصحيح البصر، يقال : إنه بصير بالقوة، أى فيه قوة الإبصار، ومعناه أن الصفة التي لابد منها في العين، ليصح الإبصار، موجودة، فإن تأخر الإبصار، فلتأخر شرط آخر، فتكون قوة الإبصار للسواد مثلا، موجودة للعين قبل إبصار السواد بالفعل، فإن حصل إبصار السواد بالفعل، لم تكن قوة إبصار ذلك السواد موجودة عند وجود ذلك الإبصار، إذ لا يمكن أن يقال : مهما حصل الإبصار، فهو مع كونه موجوداً بالفعل، موجود بالقوة، بل قوة الوجود، لا تضام حقيقة الوجود الحاصل بالفعل أبداً.

وإذا ثبتت هذه المقدمة فنقول : لو انعدم الشيء البسيط، لكان إمكان (1) السنخ هو الأصل.
A second proof, and upon it they rely, that they said: Every substance not in a subject is impossible to be non-existent; indeed, simple things are never annihilated. This proof first establishes that the annihilation of the body does not necessitate the annihilation of the soul, as previously. Then it is said: It is impossible that it be annihilated by another cause, because everything that is annihilated by a cause — whatever cause — has in it a potentiality of corruption before corruption, i.e., possibility of non-existence preceding non-existence, just as what comes into existence among accidents has possibility of existence preceding existence, and possibility of existence is called potentiality of existence, and possibility of non-existence potentiality of corruption. And just as possibility of existence is a relational attribute that subsists only in something such that it is a possibility relative to it, likewise possibility of non-existence. Therefore it was said: Every contingent event depends on a prior matter in which is the possibility of existence of the event and its potentiality, as previously in the issue of the eternity of the world. So the matter in which is the potentiality of existence is receptive to the accidental existence, and the receiver is not the received, so the receiver exists with the received when it occurs, and it is other than it. Likewise the receiver of non-existence should be as something in which a thing exists, and it exists at the occurrence of non-existence, so that something perishes from it, and what perishes is other than what remains, and what remains is that in which is the potentiality of non-existence, its receptivity, and its possibility, just as what remains at the occurrence of existence is other than what occurred, and that which has the potentiality of receiving the accidental was before. So it follows that the thing which non-existence befell is composed of something that perished and a receiver of non-existence that remained with the occurrence of non-existence, and it was the bearer of the potentiality of non-existence before the occurrence of non-existence, and the bearer of the potentiality of non-existence is like matter, and the perished from it is like form.

But the soul is simple, and it is a form abstracted from matter, having no composition. If composition of form and matter were assumed in it, we transfer the demonstration to the matter, which is the root and first principle, for it must end with a principle. Then we attribute non-existence to that principle, which is called soul, just as we attribute non-existence to the matter of bodies, for it is eternal perpetually, and only forms occur upon it and forms become non-existent from it, and in it is the potentiality of occurrence of forms upon it and the potentiality of occurrence of non-existence of forms from it, for it is receptive to both opposites equally. And from this it has appeared that every existent of simple essence is impossible to be non-existent.

And this can be made understandable in another formulation, namely that the potentiality of existence of a thing is before the existence of the thing, so it belongs to other than that thing, and it is not the same as the potentiality of existence. Its explanation: The sound-sighted person is said to be sighted potentially, i.e., in him is the potentiality of sight, and its meaning is that the attribute necessary in the eye for sight to be sound exists. If sight is delayed, it is due to the delay of another condition. So the potentiality of sight for blackness, for example, exists in the eye before actually seeing blackness. If actual seeing of blackness occurs, the potentiality of sight for that blackness is not present at the existence of that sight, for it cannot be said that whenever sight occurs, it is, while being actual, also potential. Rather, the potentiality of never coexists with the actual existence that has occurred.

And when this premise is established, we say: If the simple thing were annihilated, the possibility (1) al-sinkh is the root.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' second proof for the immortality of the soul. The proof argues that every simple substance (not in a subject) cannot be annihilated. The reasoning is that annihilation requires composition: something that is annihilated must have a potentiality for non-existence before it occurs, and that potentiality must inhere in something that remains after the annihilation, just as the potentiality for existence inheres in matter that remains after the form is acquired. Therefore, the thing that is annihilated must be composed of a part that perishes (like form) and a part that remains (like matter). But the soul is simple and has no composition. Even if one assumes composition in the soul, the demonstration reduces to the underlying principle (the soul itself), which would then be eternal and indestructible, like the matter of bodies. The proof concludes that every existent with a simple essence cannot be annihilated. Ghazali then provides an alternative explanation using the example of sight: the potentiality for sight exists before actual sight and does not coexist with it; similarly, potentiality for non-existence cannot coexist with actual existence. Therefore, if a simple thing were annihilated, its potentiality for non-existence would have to precede it, but since it is simple and has no parts, it cannot have such a potentiality in itself.

إبصار
قوة الإبصار

Checkpoint
According to the alternative formulation, why can a simple thing not be annihilated?
A) Because potentiality for non-existence would have to precede its existence and cannot coexist with actual existence, leaving no bearer for the potentiality.
B) Because God does not will annihilation of simple things.
C) Because simple things are material and hence eternal.
D) Because annihilation would require a cause, and no cause can act on simple things.

Key Takeaway
Potentiality for non-existence cannot coexist with actual existence; since a simple thing has no parts to host potentiality separately, it cannot be annihilated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe your soul is a simple substance that cannot be destroyed, how would you live today differently if you knew with certainty that every moment is eternal? What one action would you change right now?
?
The argument shows that potentiality for your non-existence cannot coexist with your actual existence. Does this comfort you or unsettle you? Why? And what does your reaction reveal about your relationship with your Creator?
?
Imagine standing before Allah with the proof from this lesson: your soul is immortal. Which of your current habits would you most regret if you realized you will carry its effects forever? What will you commit to doing about it today?

First Principle: Each Psychic Faculty Has Its Own Pleasure and Pain
Ibn Sina enumerates faculties and their specific pleasures and pains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every psychic faculty has its own specific pleasure and good, and its own harm and evil, and how this principle is foundational for understanding higher pleasures.

Core Idea
Every psychic faculty has its own unique pleasure and pain.

Ibn Sina begins his argument about the nature of pleasure by establishing a foundational principle: each psychic faculty has a pleasure and pain that is specific to it. He provides concrete examples from the appetitive, irascible, estimative, and memory faculties to illustrate how each one finds delight in its appropriate object. This principle will later be used to argue that intellectual pleasures are superior to sensory ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Notice the clear structure: a general statement followed by examples.
This passage is a principle, so pay attention to the foundational claim.
2
Identify the faculties and their pleasures
List the four psychic faculties mentioned and their specific pleasures: appetite (sensible quality), anger (victory), imagination (hope), memory (recollection).
3
Reflect on the shared aspect
Consider the concluding sentence: all faculties share in that the awareness of what is agreeable constitutes their pleasure, and the true agreeable is the attainment of actual perfection relevant to each faculty.
This ties the examples together into a universal principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجب أن تعلم أن لكل قوة نفسانية لذة وخيراً يخصها، وأذى وشراً يخصها. مثاله: أن لذة الشهوة وخيرها أن يتأدى إليها كيفية محسوسة ملائمة، ولذة الغضب الظفر، ولذة الوهم الرجاء، ولذة الحفظ تذكر الأمور الموافقة الماضية، وأذى كل واحد منها ما يضاده. وتشترك كلها نوعاً من الشركة، في أن الشعور بموافقها وملائمها هو الخير واللذة الخاصة بها، والموافق لكل واحد منها بالذات والحقيقة هو حصول الكمال الذي هو بالقياس إليه كمال بالفعل. فهذا أصل.
It is necessary that you know that every psychic faculty has a pleasure and a good that is specific to it, and a harm and an evil that is specific to it. Example: the pleasure of the appetite and its good is that a suitable sensible quality reaches it; the pleasure of anger is victory; the pleasure of imagination (wahm) is hope; the pleasure of memory is the recollection of agreeable past matters; and the harm of each one is its opposite. And they all share a kind of participation in that the awareness of what is agreeable and suitable to it is the good and the pleasure specific to it, and what is agreeable to each one in itself and in truth is the attainment of the perfection which in relation to it is actual perfection. So this is a principle.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is the first principle in Ibn Sina's argument about pleasures. He asserts that every psychic faculty has its own specific pleasure and good, as well as its own specific harm and evil. He gives examples: the pleasure of the appetitive faculty is receiving a suitable sensible quality (like taste), the pleasure of the irascible faculty is victory, the pleasure of the estimative faculty (wahm) is hope, and the pleasure of memory is recollection of agreeable past events. Their harms are their opposites. Then he notes that all these faculties share in that the perception (shu'ur) of what is agreeable and suitable (muwafiq wa mu'lim) is the good and pleasure specific to that faculty. The agreeable in itself and in truth is the attainment of the perfection which is actual perfection relative to that faculty. This is a foundational principle for understanding the hierarchy of pleasures.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pleasure is only physical or sensory.
Correction: Ibn Sina shows that each psychic faculty, including non-sensory ones like imagination and memory, has its own specific pleasure, indicating that pleasure can be intellectual and spiritual.

Checkpoint
According to the first principle, what is the good and pleasure specific to the faculty of imagination (wahm)?
A) Victory
B) Hope
C) Recollection
D) Receiving a sensible quality

Key Takeaway
Each psychic faculty has its own specific pleasure and pain, and the highest pleasure is the attainment of actual perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these four pleasures—appetite, victory, hope, or memory—do you find yourself most driven by? What does that reveal about the current state of your soul?
?
When you recall 'agreeable past matters,' is your memory bringing you closer to the actual perfection of your intellect, or is it merely a comfort that keeps you from pursuing higher pleasures?
?
If the true pleasure of each faculty is the attainment of its actual perfection, then what is the pleasure you are settling for? Are you willing to trade a lower pleasure today for the perfection your soul was made for?

Second Principle: Faculties Differ in Rank and Pleasure
Ibn Sina states that faculties differ in rank, and more perfect faculties have more perfect pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that the pleasure of a faculty depends on its rank and perfection, and why higher faculties experience more intense pleasure.

Core Idea
Pleasure is not equal—it increases with the perfection and rank of the faculty.

This passage presents Ibn Sina's second principle in his discussion of pleasure and pain. He argues that even though all faculties share the same basic capacity for pleasure, their ranks differ in reality. The more perfect, enduring, and intense a faculty's perfection and perception, the more intense and complete its pleasure. This establishes a hierarchy of pleasures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the list of aspects of perfection.
2
Identify the list of aspects
Find the six ways in which a faculty can be superior: more complete perfection, more abundant, more enduring, more reachable, more perfect in act, stronger perception.
Each aspect leads to a more intense pleasure.
3
Understand the conclusion
Ibn Sina concludes that the pleasure for a faculty with these superior qualities is necessarily more intense and more complete. This is a principle (أصل).
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this principle grounds the superiority of intellectual pleasures over sensory ones. Why might the intellect be considered a higher faculty?
This connects to the overall aim of showing the soul's pleasures surpass bodily ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فإن هذه القوى، وإن اشتركت فى هذه المعانى، فإن مراتبها فى الحقيقة مختلفة، فالذي كماله أتم وأفضل، والذي كماله أكثر، والذي كماله أدوم، والذي كماله أوصل إليه وأحصل له، والذي هو في نفسه أكمل فعلا وأفضل، والذي هو في نفسه أشد إدراكاً، فاللذة له أبلغ وأوفى لا محالة. وهذا أصل.
And also, these faculties, even if they share in these meanings, their ranks in reality are different. So that whose perfection is more complete and better, and that whose perfection is more abundant, and that whose perfection is more enduring, and that whose perfection is more reachable and attainable for it, and that which in itself is more perfect in act and better, and that which in itself is more intense in perception—the pleasure for it is necessarily more intense and more complete. And this is a principle.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage presents the second principle. Ibn Sina says that although all psychic faculties share the general characteristic of having pleasure and pain, their ranks are different in reality. He then lists several aspects of superiority: a faculty may have a more complete perfection, a more abundant perfection, a more enduring perfection, a more readily attainable perfection, a more perfect actuality, and a stronger perception. In each case, the pleasure corresponding to that faculty is more intense and more complete without doubt. This principle establishes that not all pleasures are equal; they vary with the nobility of the faculty.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All pleasures are qualitatively the same and can be compared solely by intensity or duration.
Correction: Ibn Sina shows that pleasures vary in kind based on the faculty. A higher faculty's pleasure is more intense and complete, regardless of duration or quantity.

Checkpoint
According to the second principle, what can be said about the pleasure of a faculty with a more perfect act and stronger perception?
A) It is less intense
B) It is necessarily more intense and more complete
C) It is equal to all other pleasures
D) It cannot be compared

Key Takeaway
The pleasure of a faculty is directly proportional to its rank and perfection; higher faculties experience more intense and complete pleasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pleasure, do you consider which faculty is being gratified? Which faculty do you most often feed—your intellect or your senses? What does that reveal about your pursuit of perfection?
?
This principle states that higher faculties yield necessarily more intense pleasure. If you truly believe this, why do you still chase fleeting bodily pleasures over the lasting joy of knowing truth? What fear or laziness holds you back?
?
Think of one specific intellectual pleasure you could pursue today (e.g., reading a meaningful book, pondering a proof of God's existence). Will you choose that over a quick sensory satisfaction? What commitment can you make to train your soul toward higher pleasures?

Known but Unimaginable Pleasures
Ibn Sina explains that some pleasures are known but not imagined, using examples and a Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that some pleasures are known to exist even though we cannot imagine them, and how this justifies belief in spiritual pleasures beyond sensory experience.

Core Idea
Lack of imagination does not negate reality.

Ibn Sina begins a crucial principle: the fact that we cannot imagine a pleasure does not mean it doesn't exist. Through vivid examples like the impotent man, the blind man, and the deaf man, he shows that we can be certain of something's goodness without direct experience. This lays the groundwork for affirming the pleasures of the afterlife and the bliss of spiritual beings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the passage and identify the central thesis: that some perfections are known to be pleasant even though their modality is unimaginable until experienced.
The key phrase is 'known that it exists and is pleasant, but one does not conceive its modality'.
2
Examine the three analogies
Study the examples of the impotent man, the blind man, and the deaf man. For each, note how they know a pleasure exists without being able to imagine it or desire it specifically.
The impotent man knows intercourse is pleasurable but does not desire it; the blind man cannot imagine visual beauty; the deaf man cannot imagine melodies.
3
Reflect on the Quranic verse
Consider the verse 'No soul knows what is hidden for them of joy of the eyes'. Ibn Sina uses it to show that afterlife pleasures are beyond imagination but real.
The verse emphasizes the hidden nature of these pleasures.
4
Understand the conclusion
Ibn Sina warns against reducing all pleasures to animalistic ones and asserts that higher spiritual beings have pleasures known through reason, not sensation.
He compares our ignorance of spiritual pleasures to a deaf person who knows music is pleasant without hearing it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فإنه قد يكون الخروج إلى الفعل، في كمال ما، بحيث يعلم أنه كائن ولذيد، ولا يتصور كيفيته، ولا يشعر باللذاذة ما لم يحصل؛ وما لم يشعر به، لم يشتق إليه ولم ينزع نحوه، مثل العنين، فإنه متحقق أن للجماع لذة، ولكنه لا يشتهيه ولا يحن نحوه، الاشتهاء والحنين، اللذان يكونان مخصوصين به، بل شهوة أخرى، كما يشتهى من يجرب، من حيث يحصل به إدراك، وإن كان مؤذياً، وفى الجملة فإنه لا يتخيله. وكذلك حال الأكمه عند الصور الجميلة، والأصم عند الألحان المنتظمة. وقوله تعالى: {فلا تعلم نفس ما أخفي لهم من قرة أعين} أي لا يعلم ذلك. ولهذا يجب أن لا يتوهم العاقل أن كل لذة فهي – كما للحمار - في بطنه وفرجه، وأن المبادئ الأولى المقربة عند رب العالمين عادمة للذة والغبطة، وأن رب العالمين عز وجل ليس له في سلطانه وخاصية البهاء الذي له وقوته غير المتناهية أمر في غاية الفضيلة والشرف والطيب، نجله عن أن لذة، ثم للحمار والبهائم حالة طيبة ولذيذة؟ كلا. بل أي نسبة تكون ما للمبادئ العالية إلى هذه الخسيسة، ولكنا نتخيل هذا ونشاهده، ولم نعرف ذلك بالاستشعار، بل بالقياس، فحالنا عنده كحال الأصم الذي لم يسمع قط في عمره ولا تخيل اللذة اللحنية، وهو متيقن لطيبها. وهذا أصل.
And also, it may happen that the actualization of some perfection is such that it is known that it exists and is pleasant, but one does not conceive its modality nor feel the pleasure until it occurs. And as long as one does not feel it, one does not desire it nor incline towards it, like an impotent man: he is certain that intercourse has pleasure, but he does not desire it nor long for it, the longing and yearning that are specific to it; rather, he has another desire, like one who desires from experience, insofar as he obtains perception even if it is harmful. In sum, he does not imagine it. Similarly is the case of the blind man with beautiful images, and the deaf man with harmonious melodies. And the Almighty said: 'No soul knows what is hidden for them of joy of the eyes' – i.e., it does not know that. Therefore, it is necessary that the intelligent person should not imagine that every pleasure is, like that of the donkey, in its belly and genitals, and that the first principles close to the Lord of the worlds are devoid of pleasure and bliss, and that the Lord of the worlds, exalted and majestic, has nothing in His dominion, the property of the majesty that is His, and His infinite power, that is in utmost virtue, honor, and goodness? We exalt Him above being [described as having] pleasure? Then for the donkey and beasts there is a good and pleasurable state? No! On the contrary, what proportion do the high principles have to these lowly things? But we imagine and observe this, and we do not know that by sensation, but by reasoning. So our state regarding it is like the state of the deaf man who has never heard in his life nor imagined the pleasure of melodies, yet he is certain of its goodness. And this is a principle.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is the third principle. Ibn Sina argues that sometimes the actualization (khuruj ila al-fi'l) of a perfection is such that one knows it is real and pleasant but cannot imagine its modality or feel the pleasure until it actually occurs. Without feeling, one does not desire it. He gives the example of an impotent man who knows intercourse is pleasurable but does not desire it; his desire is only some other desire. Similarly, a blind man cannot imagine beautiful images, and a deaf man cannot imagine harmonious melodies. He then cites the Quranic verse 'No soul knows what is hidden for them of joy of the eyes' to illustrate that the pleasures of the afterlife are beyond our imagination. He warns the intelligent person not to think that all pleasures are like that of a donkey (eating and sex), and that the high spiritual beings (al-mabadi' al-ula) are devoid of pleasure. On the contrary, we affirm through reason that higher pleasures exist, even though we cannot sense them, just as a deaf person knows that music is pleasant without hearing it. This principle establishes that lack of imagination does not negate the reality of higher pleasures.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some deny spiritual pleasures because they cannot imagine them.
Correction: Ibn Sina shows that lack of imagination does not negate existence; we can know a pleasure exists through reason, just as a deaf man knows music is pleasant.

Checkpoint
What example does Ibn Sina use to illustrate that something can be known to be pleasant without being imagined?
A) A sick person disliking sweet food
B) An impotent man knowing intercourse is pleasurable but not desiring it
C) A hungry animal not desiring food
D) A person with jaundice not tasting bitterness

Key Takeaway
Pleasures can be real and known even if we cannot imagine them, so spiritual pleasures should not be denied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that the pleasure of standing before Allah in prayer is real, but do you feel any longing for it? What is blocking your imagination from kindling that desire?
?
The impotent man did not desire intercourse because he could not imagine it. Are there acts of worship you know are good but do not desire? Will you ask Allah to open your heart to imagine their sweetness?
?
You believe in the rewards of Jannah, but if you are honest, does your life reflect certainty in them? What one change can you make today to act on that certainty, even while you cannot imagine the pleasure?

Fourth Principle: Hindrance Causes Aversion to Pleasure
Ibn Sina explains how an obstacle or distraction causes aversion to pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a hindrance or distraction can cause a person to dislike or not feel pleasure from something that is inherently agreeable, as explained by Ibn Sina.

Core Idea
A perceiver's obstacle can turn pleasure into aversion.

Ibn Sina continues his argument against the philosophers by showing that the absence of pleasure does not mean the pleasure is unreal. He introduces a fourth principle: a hindrance or preoccupation in the soul can cause aversion to agreeable things. This principle explains why some people dislike what is naturally good, like a sick person disliking sweet taste.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp Ibn Sina's main point.
Notice the example of sick people and the nuance between aversion and lack of pleasure.
2
Identify the three elements
Look for: the available perfection/agreeable matter, the obstacle or preoccupation, and the resulting aversion or lack of pleasure.
This helps you see the cause-effect structure.
3
Understand the nuance
Ibn Sina distinguishes between active aversion (كراهية) and mere lack of pleasure (عدم الاستلذاذ). Consider how the fearful example (though unclear) illustrates the latter.
This nuance is important for the overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فإن الكمال والأمر الملائم، قد يتيسر للقوة الداركة، وهناك مانع أو شاغل للنفس، فتكرهه، وتؤثر ضده عليه، مثل كراهية بعض المرضى، الطعم الحلو، وشهوتهم للطعوم الردية الكريهة بالذات، وربما لم تكن كراهية، ولكن كان عدم الاستلذاذ به، كالخائف أو اللذة، فلا يشعر بهما، ولا يستلذهما. وهذا أصل.
And also, perfection and the agreeable matter may be available to the perceiving power, but there is an obstacle or a preoccupation for the soul, so it dislikes it and prefers its opposite. Like the aversion of some sick people to sweet taste and their desire for repulsive tastes that are intrinsically bad, and perhaps there is no aversion, but rather a lack of pleasure in it, like the fearful or the pleasure? So he does not feel them nor delight in them. And this is a principle.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth principle. Ibn Sina states that sometimes the perfection and agreeable thing are accessible to the perceiving power (al-quwwa al-darika), but there is an obstacle (mani') or a distraction (shaghil) for the soul, so it hates it and prefers its opposite. He gives the example of sick people who dislike sweet food and desire foul tastes that are intrinsically bad. Sometimes there is no active aversion but just a lack of pleasure, like the fearful? (the text is unclear). The result is that the person does not feel the pleasure nor take delight in it. This principle shows that the absence of pleasure does not mean the pleasure itself is not real; it may be due to a hindrance in the perceiver.
