Fifth Principle: Perceiving Power and Its Obstacles
Ibn Sina explains the fifth principle with three examples of obstructed perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's principle that a faculty's failure to perceive pleasure or pain may be due to an affliction, not the absence of the stimulus.

Core Idea
Absence of pain does not mean the cause is absent — the perceiver may be blocked.

Ibn Sina continues his critique of the philosophers by presenting a psychological principle: what we perceive depends not only on external stimuli but also on the state of our faculties. He illustrates this with vivid examples showing that even extreme pain may go unfelt when the perceiving power is obstructed by its opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the three examples Ibn Sina provides.
The examples are key to grasping the principle.
2
Identify the principle
What is the common thread? The perceiving power is afflicted by something contrary to its perfection, so it does not feel the stimulus until the obstacle is removed.
Ibn Sina uses 'mamnuwa bi-didd' (afflicted with the opposite).
3
Connect to the broader argument
Consider why this principle matters for the debate on bodily resurrection. If the soul can be obstructed from perceiving, then the absence of pleasure/pain in the afterlife does not disprove its existence.
This is part of Ghazali's critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فإنه قد تكون القوة الداركة، ممنوة بضد ما هو كمالها، ولا تحس به ولا تنفر عنه، حتى إذا زال العائق، تأذت به، ورجعت إلى غريزتها، مثل الممرور، فربما لم يحس بمرارة فيه، إلى أن يصلح مزاجه، وتشفى أعضاؤه، فحينئذ ينفر عن الحال العارضة له. وكذلك قد يكون الحيوان، غير مشته للغذاء البتة، كارهاً له، وهو أوفق شيء له، ويبقى حتى عليه مدة طويلة، فإذا زال العائق، عاد إلى واجبه فى طبعه، فاشتد جوعه وشهوته للغذاء، لا يصبر عنه، ويهلك عند فقدانه، وقد يحصل سبب الألم العظيم، مثل إحراق النار، وتبريد الزمهرير، إلا أن الحس منوف، فلا يتأذى البدن به، حتى تزول الآفة، فيحس حينئذ بالألم العظيم.
And also, the perceiving power may be afflicted with the opposite of its perfection, and it does not feel it nor recoil from it, until when the obstacle is removed, it is harmed by it and returns to its nature. Like the one with jaundice (al-mamrur): perhaps he does not feel bitterness in it until his temperament is corrected and his organs are healed, then he recoils from the state that had befallen him. Similarly, an animal may not desire food at all, hating it, while it is the most suitable thing for it, and it remains for a long time; then when the obstacle is removed, it returns to its natural duty, so its hunger and desire for food intensify, it cannot endure without it, and it perishes when it is absent. And the cause of great pain may occur, like burning fire and freezing cold, but the sense is obstructed, so the body is not harmed by it, until the affliction is removed, then it feels great pain at that moment.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth principle. Ibn Sina says that the perceiving power (al-quwwa al-darika) may be afflicted (mamnuwa bi-didd) with the opposite of its perfection. As a result, it does not feel that opposite nor recoil from it until the obstacle is removed, after which it experiences harm and returns to its natural state. He gives three examples: (1) A person with jaundice (al-mamrur) does not taste bitterness until his temperament is corrected, then he recoils from the bitter state. (2) An animal may not desire food at all, even hating it, although food is most suitable; after the obstacle is removed, its hunger becomes intense and it cannot survive without food. (3) Great pain from fire or extreme cold may not be felt if the senses are obstructed; once the obstruction is removed, the pain becomes extreme. This principle illustrates that the absence of pain or pleasure sensation is not always due to the nonexistence of the thing itself, but often due to a temporary affliction in the perceiver.

ممنوة
ضد
عائق
الممرور
شهوة
ألم
إحراق النار
تبريد الزمهرير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If someone does not feel pain from fire, then fire is not painful or the pain does not exist.
Correction: Ibn Sina shows that the perceiver's faculty may be obstructed, so the absence of sensation does not imply absence of the cause.

Checkpoint
What example does Ibn Sina give for a condition where the perceiving power does not feel something until the obstacle is removed?
A) The sick person disliking sweet food
B) The impotent man's lack of desire
C) The person with jaundice not feeling bitterness until healed
D) The blind man not seeing images

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's principle shows that the absence of perceived pleasure or pain does not prove the absence of its cause; the perceiver's state may be the obstacle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area of your life where you have become numb to spiritual pain—where you no longer feel the bitterness of sin or the heat of disobedience? What obstacle in your heart might be blocking that sensitivity, and what will you do today to remove it?
?
Think of a time you avoided something you knew was good for you, like prayer or repentance, because you felt no desire for it. According to Ibn Sina's principle, what might be the 'affliction' in your soul that is suppressing that natural inclination, and how can you seek healing?
?
You have likely experienced moments when, after a trial, you suddenly felt the weight of your neglect of Allah. Why does it often take the removal of a worldly comfort for us to finally feel the pain of our distance from Him? What does that say about your current state?

The Rational Soul's Perfection as an Intellectual World
Ibn Sina describes the rational soul's perfection as becoming an intellectual world mirroring the universe.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's conception of the rational soul's ultimate perfection as becoming an intellectual world that mirrors the entire universe and unites with absolute beauty and goodness.

Core Idea
The rational soul's perfection is to become a mirror of the entire cosmos.

Ibn Sina concludes his argument by describing the highest goal of the rational soul. After establishing principles about pleasure and hindrances, he turns to the ultimate perfection: the soul's transformation into an intellectual world. This lesson unpacks that profound vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the original flow, then read the translation to understand the meaning. Notice the dense philosophical terminology.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the definition of perfection
Find where Ibn Sina explicitly states the 'specific perfection' (al-kamal al-khas) of the rational soul. Notice the phrase 'an intellectual world' ('aqlan).
3
Trace the hierarchical progression
List the stages the soul traverses: from the First Principle, through spiritual substances, to celestial bodies. Observe the downward movement from absolute spirituality to embodied forms.
This hierarchy mirrors Neoplatonic emanation.
4
Understand the final state
Ibn Sina describes the soul as becoming 'an intelligible world parallel to the entire existing world'. Note the verbs: united, engraved, entering its order, becoming of its essence.
This is not mere knowledge but transformative union.
5
Reflect on the comparison with other perfections
The final sentence contrasts this perfection with those of other faculties. Ask yourself why Ibn Sina says it would be 'ugly' for philosophers to ignore this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا تقررت هذه الأصول، فيجب أن ننصرف إلى الغرض الذي نؤمه، فنقول: إن النفس الناطقة، كمالها الخاص بها، أن تصير عالماً عقلياً، مرتسماً فيه صورة الكل، والنظام المعقول في الكل، والخير الفائض في الكل، مبتدئاً من مبدأ الكل، سالكاً إلى الجواهر الشريفة، فالروحانية المطلقة، ثم الروحانية المتعلقة نوعاً ما من التعلق بالأبدان، ثم الأجسام العلوية بهيئاتها وقواها، ثم تستمر كذلك، حتى تستوفي في نفسها هيئة الوجود كله؛ فتنقلب عالماً معقولاً، موازياً للعالم الموجود كله، مشاهداً لما هو الحسن المطلق، والخير المطلق، والجمال الحق، ومتحداً به، ومنتقشاً بمثاله وهيأته، ومنخرطاً في سلكه، وصائراً من جوهره. وإذا قيس هذا بالكمالات المعشوقة التي للقوى الأخرى، وجد في المرتبة التي بحيث يقبح تهافت الفلاسفة
So when these principles are established, it is necessary that we turn to the purpose we intend. We say: the rational soul, its specific perfection is that it becomes an intellectual world, in which is imprinted the form of the whole, the intelligible order in the whole, and the good overflowing in the whole, starting from the principle of the whole, proceeding to the noble spiritual substances, then absolute spirituality, then spirituality that is somewhat attached to bodies, then the celestial bodies with their forms and powers, then it continues in this way until it comprehends in itself the form of all existence; so it transforms into an intelligible world parallel to the entire existing world, beholding what is absolute beauty, absolute good, and true beauty, and becoming united with it, and being engraved with its likeness and form, and entering into its order, and becoming of its essence. And if this is compared to the beloved perfections of the other faculties, it will be found in the rank such that the incoherence of the philosophers becomes ugly.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of Ibn Sina's argument. After establishing the principles about pleasures and hindrances, he turns to the main purpose: defining the special perfection of the rational soul (al-nafs al-natiqa). It is to become an intellectual world ('aqlan) that contains the form of the whole (sūrat al-kull), the intelligible order of the universe, and the good that flows through everything. The soul begins from the First Principle (Mabda' al-Kull) and proceeds through the noble spiritual substances, then absolute spirituality, then spirituality related to bodies, then the celestial bodies with their forms and powers, until it comprehends the entire form of existence within itself. Thus it becomes an intelligible world parallel to the physical world, witnessing absolute beauty, absolute good, and true beauty, united with them, and becoming part of that order. Compared to the perfections of other faculties, this perfection is so exalted that it would be absurd for philosophers to fail to recognize it. The text ends abruptly but the argument is clear: the rational soul's ultimate fulfillment is intellectual union with the whole.

Misconception: This perfection is merely abstract intellectual knowledge of the universe.
Correction: Ibn Sina describes a transformative union: the soul becomes united, engraved, and enters the order of absolute beauty and goodness—it becomes part of that essence, not just knows about it.

The Second Principle: Hindrance Blocks Perception of Suitable
Ghazali enumerates the second principle with two illustrations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a temporary hindrance can prevent the soul from perceiving and enjoying what is naturally suitable.

Core Idea
A hindrance can block the perception of a good, not the good itself.

Ghazali continues his exploration of why we sometimes fail to enjoy what is good for us. He introduces a second principle: even when something perfect and suitable is present, a hindrance or distraction can cause aversion or numbness. This explains why sick people dislike sweet tastes or why a fearful person cannot feel pleasure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the full translation. Notice the structure: a principle followed by two examples.
The passage is short but dense; pay attention to the shift from 'dislike' to 'lack of delight'.
2
Identify the core principle
Find the sentence that states the principle: even when perfection and the suitable matter are present, a hindrance or occupation of the soul can cause aversion or lack of pleasure.
The key phrase is 'مانع أو شاغل للنفس'.
3
Analyze the two illustrations
The first example is of sick people who dislike sweet tastes and desire bad tastes. The second is of a fearful person who does not feel pleasure at all. Note the difference: active dislike vs. absence of delight.
Both show that the fault lies in the perceiver, not the object.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: What does this principle mean for judging whether something is good? It means absence of pleasure is not proof of lack of goodness, because a hindrance may be interfering.
This principle will later be applied to the rational soul's perception of intellectual pleasures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فإن الكمال والأمر الملائم ، قد يتيسر للقوة الداركة ، وهناك مانع أو شاغل للنفس ، فتكرهه ، وتؤثر ضده عليه ، مثل كراهية بعض المرضى ، الطعم الحلو ، وشهوتهم للطعوم الردية الكريهة بالذات ، وربما لم تكن كراهية ، ولكن كان عدم الاستلذاذ به ، كالخائف أو اللذة ، فلا يشعر بهما ، ولا يستلذهما . وهذا أصل
And also, indeed perfection and the suitable matter may be available to the perceiving power, but there is a hindrance or an occupation of the soul, so it dislikes it and prefers its opposite, like the dislike of some sick people for the sweet taste, and their desire for bad tastes that are intrinsically unpleasant. And perhaps it is not dislike, but it is lack of delight in it, like the fearful person or pleasure, so he does not feel them and does not take delight in them. And this is a principle.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a classification and illustration of a second principle. Ghazali identifies a situation where a perfect and suitable thing (الكمال والأمر الملائم) is present for a perceiving power, but due to a hindrance or distraction (مانع أو شاغل) the soul dislikes it and prefers its opposite. He gives the example of sick people who dislike sweet tastes and crave bitter ones. Alternatively, it may be not outright dislike but a lack of delight, as in the case of a fearful person who does not feel pleasure. This principle shows that the absence of pleasure does not imply the thing lacks goodness; rather, the faculty is impeded. This will be applied to the rational soul's perception of intellectual pleasures.

القوة الداركة
مانع

Correction: Ghazali shows that a temporary hindrance (such as sickness or fear) can block the perception of a suitable good, so absence of pleasure does not indicate lack of goodness.

The Third Principle: Affliction by Contraries and Delayed Perception
Ghazali explains the third principle with three examples of delayed perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the concept of a 'contrary' to explain delayed perception of pain and pleasure, and why this supports his argument about the afterlife.

Core Idea
Delay in sensation does not mean absence—contraries can block perception temporarily.

In this passage, Ghazali continues his refutation of the philosophers by presenting a third principle: that a perceiving power may be afflicted by its contrary, preventing it from sensing what is naturally harmful or beneficial. He gives three concrete examples—the jaundiced person, the animal without appetite, and the numb body—to show that pain and pleasure can be delayed but still real. This principle will be used to argue that we do not experience intellectual pleasures now due to bodily hindrances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation once to get the overall argument. Notice that Ghazali states a general principle and then gives three examples.
2
Identify the three examples
List the three examples: the jaundiced person, the animal without appetite, and the numb body. For each, note what the 'contrary' is and what is perceived after its removal.
The contrary is the blocking condition (e.g., jaundice, lack of appetite, numbness).
3
Connect to the broader argument
Consider why Ghazali uses these examples. How does this principle help explain why we don't experience spiritual pleasures now? The bodily constitution acts as a contrary that blocks perception.
This is part of his refutation of philosophers who claim the afterlife is only spiritual.
4
Reflect on the misconception
The common misunderstanding is that if we don't feel pain, it's not harmful. Ghazali shows that feeling can be delayed. Test your understanding by explaining this to someone else.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فإنه قد تكون القوة الداركة ، ممنوة بضد ما هو كمالها ، ولا تحس به ولا تنفر عنه ، حتى إذا زال العائق ، تأذت به ، ورجعت إلى غريزتها ، مثل الممرور ، فربما لم يحس بمرارة فيه ، إلى أن يصلح مزاجه ، وتشفى أعضاؤه ، فحينئذ ينفر عن الحال العارضة له . وكذلك قد يكون الحيوان ، غير مشته للغذاء البتة ، كارهاً له ، وهو أوفق شيء له ، ويبقى حتى عليه مدة طويلة ، فإذا زال العائق ، عاد إلى واجبه فى طبعه ، فاشتد جوعه وشهوته للغذاء ، لا يصبر عنه ، ويهلك عند فقدانه ، وقد يحصل سبب الألم العظيم ، مثل إحراق النار ، وتبريد الزمهرير ، إلا أن الحس منوف ، فلا يتأذى البدن به ، حتى تزول الآفة ، فيحس حينئذ بالألم العظيم .
And also, indeed the perceiving power may be afflicted by the contrary of what is its perfection, and it does not sense it nor does it shrink from it, until when the hindrance is removed, it is harmed by it and returns to its instinct, like the jaundiced person: perhaps he does not sense bitterness in it, until his temperament is corrected and his organs are healed, then at that time he shrinks from the accidental state that befell him. And likewise, an animal may have absolutely no appetite for food, disliking it, while it is the most suitable thing for him, and it remains for a long time, then when the hindrance is removed, it returns to its natural obligation, so its hunger and appetite for food become intense, it cannot bear being without it, and it perishes at its loss. And the cause of great pain may occur, like the burning of fire and the freezing of bitter cold, except that the sense is removed [deadened], so the body is not harmed by it, until the affliction is removed, then at that time it feels the great pain.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents the third principle through examples. Ghazali explains that a perceiving power (القوة الداركة) might be afflicted by a contrary state that prevents it from sensing what is naturally harmful or painful. He gives three examples: (1) A jaundiced person may not taste bitterness in food until his health is restored, then he recoils from it. (2) An animal may have no appetite for food that is perfectly suitable, but after a long period without eating, its hunger becomes intense and it cannot survive without food. (3) A body that is numb from extreme cold or nerve damage may not feel the pain of fire or freezing until the numbness wears off, then it feels intense pain. These examples illustrate that the absence of pain or desire does not mean the object is not harmful or beneficial; the perception is blocked by a contrary condition. This principle will be used to explain why we do not experience intellectual pleasures in this life—our bodily attachments act as hindrances.

Key Terms
القوة الداركة
ضد
غريزة
ممرور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we don't feel pain from something, it must not be painful or harmful.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the perceiving power can be afflicted by a contrary that deadens sensation; the pain is real but delayed until the obstacle is removed.

Checkpoint
Which example does Ghazali NOT use for the third principle?
A) The jaundiced person not sensing bitterness
B) An animal losing appetite for suitable food
C) A person feeling pleasure from music
D) A body not feeling fire due to numbness

Key Takeaway
The perception of pain or pleasure can be delayed by a contrary state, but it remains real and will manifest once the obstacle is removed—this analogizes our experience of the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'contraries' in your own life—like distractions, desires, or complacency—are deadening your perception of the ultimate reality of the afterlife? When will you remove them?
?
The animal in the example eventually perishes without food. Are you starving your soul of its essential nourishment because your appetite for truth has been blocked? What will it take for you to feel that hunger?
?
You may feel no pain at sin now, but what if, like the numb body, your spiritual senses are deadened? When the numbness of this world wears off, will you be prepared to face the pain you ignored?

Superiority of Intellectual Pleasures Over Sensory Ones
Ghazali compares intellectual and sensory pleasures in three aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's three-fold argument for why intellectual pleasures surpass sensory ones in virtue, permanence, and intensity.

Core Idea
Intellectual pleasures are nobler, everlasting, and unite the knower with the known.

Ghazali has been arguing that the pleasures of the intellect are far greater than any sensory delight. In this passage, he presents three concrete comparisons: virtue, permanence, and intensity. He culminates with the striking claim that in intellectual apprehension, the intellect and its object become one—a key idea in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the three comparative points Ghazali raises.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three criteria
Ghazali compares pleasures in virtue (فضيلة), permanence (دوام), and intensity of attainment (شدة الوصول). Find each in the text.
These are the three dimensions of superiority.
3
Understand the intensity argument
Ghazali says sensory attainment is by contact of surfaces, while intellectual attainment makes the intellect, intelligible, and intelligent one. Reflect on what this means.
This is a Neoplatonic concept.
4
Consider the implications
Ask: Why does Ghazali emphasize 'one thing or close to one'? How does this strengthen the argument for superiority?
It avoids pantheism while claiming deep union.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعها، أن يقال إنه أتم وأفضل منها ، بل لا نسبة لها إليه بوجه من الوجوه ، فضيلة ، وتماماً ، كثرة ، وسائر ما يتم به تلذذ المدركات مما ذكرناه . وأما الدوام ، فكيف يقاس الدوام الأبدى ، بالدوام المتغير الفاسد ؟ ! وأما شدة الوصول ، فكيف يكون حال ما وصوله بملاقاة السطوح ، بالقياس إلى ما هو سار في جوهره قابله ، حتى يكون كأنه هو هو ، بلا انفصال ؟ ! إذ العقل ، والمعقول ، والعاقل ، شيء واحد ، أو قريب من الواحد
And along with that, it is said that it is more complete and better than them; rather, there is no proportion of it to them in any respect: in virtue, completeness, abundance, and all that perfects the delight of perceptions that we have mentioned. As for permanence, how can eternal permanence be compared to changing, corrupting permanence? As for intensity of attainment, how is the state of that whose attainment is by contact with surfaces compared to that which is diffused in its essence, its counterpart, until it is as though it is the very same, without separation? For the intellect, the intelligible, and the intelligent are one thing, or close to one.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a comparative argument. Ghazali asserts that intellectual pleasures are more complete and better than sensory ones without any proportion. He then breaks this down into three aspects: (1) Virtue and perfection (فضيلة وتماماً): the object of intellectual pleasure is nobler. (2) Permanence (دوام): intellectual pleasure is eternal, while sensory pleasures are changing and perishable. (3) Intensity of attainment (شدة الوصول): sensory pleasures involve contact of surfaces (e.g., touching), but intellectual pleasure involves the intellect grasping its object in such a way that the intellect, the intelligible, and the intelligent become one or nearly one. This is a Neoplatonic idea common in Islamic philosophy. Ghazali uses it to elevate intellectual pleasures above all sensory ones.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the unity of intellect and intelligible implies pantheism.
Correction: Ghazali qualifies it as 'one thing or close to one' (قريب من الواحد), avoiding literal identity.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that intellectual pleasures are superior in virtue, permanence, and intensity, culminating in the unity of knower and known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In what area of your life are you choosing sensory pleasures over intellectual ones? What would it take for you to prioritize the pursuit of truth and knowledge even if it requires effort?

Ghazali says that in intellectual pleasure, the knower and known become 'one thing, or close to one'. Think of a truth you have come to know that has transformed you. Did you feel a sense of union with that truth? How does that compare to the fleeting satisfaction of a physical desire? Are you investing in such transformative knowledge?
?
The passage contrasts eternal permanence with changing, corrupting permanence. Is your daily life oriented more toward fleeting pleasures or toward what lasts forever? If you were to die tomorrow, would you regret not having pursued more intellectual pleasures? What step can you take today to begin that pursuit?

Why We Don't Taste Intellectual Pleasure Now
Ghazali explains why intellectual pleasures are not felt now and how they will be experienced after death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intellectual pleasures are not felt in this life due to bodily immersion, and how the soul can experience them after death or through purification.

Core Idea
Our immersion in vices blinds us to the soul's highest pleasures, which await us after purification.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a potential objection: if the rational soul is so superior, why do we not feel its pleasures? He explains that our current state—immersed in bodily desires—acts like a sickness that prevents us from tasting true intellectual delight. Only by casting off the yoke of appetite and anger can we catch glimpses, and full experience comes after death.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the Arabic text ends mid-sentence, but the argument is still clear.
2
Identify the three reasons we don't feel intellectual pleasure
Find the causes Ghazali gives: immersion in vices, lack of purification from appetite and anger, and the body as a veil. Note how he compares us to a sick person who cannot taste sweetness.
These reasons build on earlier principles alluded to in the text.
3
Examine the common experience proof
Ghazali appeals to everyday choices: even base souls prefer honor, victory, or avoiding shame over immediate desires. This shows intellectual ends are naturally more honorable.
This is a key argument for the inherent nobility of intellectual goods.
4
Understand the afterlife hope
After death, the soul, if awakened in life, will seek its perfection. But if preoccupied with the body, it forgets its true beloved, like forgetting hunger when sick.
The passage ends abruptly, but the point is clear: we must train the soul now.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أن المدرك فى نفسه أكمل ، فأمر لا يخفى ، وأما أنه أشد إدراكاً ، فأمر أيضاً تعرفه بأدنى تذكر لما سلف بيانه . فإن النفس النطقية أكثر عدد مدركات ، وأشد تقصياً للمدرك ، وتجريداً له عن الزوائد ، غير الداخلة في معناه ، إلا بالعرض ، ولها الخوض في باطن المدرك وظاهره . بل كيف يقاس هذا الإدراك ، بذلك الإدراك ؟ ! أو كيف تقاس هذه اللذة ، باللذة الحسية ، والهيمية ، والغضبية ، ولكننا في عالمنا و بدننا وانغماسنا فى الرذائل ، لا نحس بتلك اللذة ، إذا حصل عندنا شيء من أسبابها ، كما أومأنا إليه في بعض ما قدمناه ، من الأصول ، ولذلك لا نطلبها ولا نحن إليها ، اللهم إلا أن نكون قد خلعنا ، ربقة الشهوة والغضب وأخواتها من أعناقنا ، وطالعنا شيئاً من تلك اللذة ، فحينئذ ربما تخيلنا منها ، خيالا طفيفاً ضعيفاً ، وخصوصاً عند انحلال المشكلات ، واستيضاح المطلوبات النفيسة . ونسبة التذاذنا هذا ، إلى التذاذنا ذلك ، نسبة الالتذاذ الحسى ، بتنشق روائح المذوقات اللذيدة ، إلى الالتذاذ بتطعمها ، بل أبعد من ذلك بعداً غير محدود . وأنت تعلم إذا تأملت عويصاً يهمك ، وعرضت عليك شهوة ، وخيرت بين الطرفين ، استخففت بالشهوة ، إن كنت كريم النفس ، والأنفس العامية أيضاً كذا ، فإنها تترك الشهوات المعترضة ، وتؤثر الغرامات ، والآلام الفادحة ، بسبب افتضاح ، أو خجل ، أو تعبير ، أو شوق لغلبة ، وهذه كلها أحوال عقلية . فيعلم من ذلك أن الغايات العقلية، أكرم على الأنفس من محقرات الأشياء فكيف في الأمور البهية العالية ؟ ! إلا أن الأنفس الخسيسة ، تحس بما يلحق المحقرات من الخير والشر ، ولا تحس بما يلحق الأمور البهية ، لما قيل من المعاذير . وأما إذا انفصلنا عن البدن ، وكانت النفس منا قد تنبهت ، وهى فى البدن ، لكمالها الذي هو معشوقها ، ولم تحصله وهى بالطبع نازعة إليه ، وإذ عقلت بالفعل أنه موجود ، إلا أن اشتغاطا بالبدن كما قلنا ، قد أنساها ذاتها ومعشوقها ، كما ينسى المرض الحاجة ، إلى بدل ما يتحلل ،
As for that the perceiver itself is more perfect, that is not hidden. And as for that it is more intense in perception, that too you know with the least recollection of what has preceded in explanation. For the rational soul is greater in number of perceptions, and more thorough in investigating the perceived, and abstracting it from accretions not entering into its meaning except accidentally, and it has the capacity to delve into the inward and outward of the perceived. Rather, how can this perception be measured against that perception? Or how can this pleasure be measured against sensory, animal, and irascible pleasure? But we, in our world and our body and our immersion in vices, do not sense that pleasure, when some of its causes occur to us, as we alluded to in some of what we presented of the principles. Therefore we do not seek it nor incline to it, unless we have cast off the yoke of appetite and anger and their sisters from our necks, and we have glimpsed something of that pleasure; then perhaps we imagine from it a slight, weak imagination, especially at the resolution of difficulties and the clarification of precious objects of inquiry. And the proportion of this our delight to that delight is the proportion of sensory delighting by inhaling the smells of delicious foods to the delighting of tasting them; rather, more distant than that by an unbounded distance. And you know that if you contemplate a difficult puzzle that concerns you, and a desire is presented to you, and you are given a choice between the two, you would consider the desire trivial, if you are of noble soul; and even common souls are like this, for they leave aside intervening desires and prefer intense pains and afflictions due to disgrace, or shame, or a reprimand, or a longing for victory – and all these are intellectual states. So it is known from this that intellectual ends are more honorable to souls than lowly things, so how about the sublime, high matters? But the base souls sense what attaches to lowly things of good and evil, and do not sense what attaches to sublime matters, due to the excuses mentioned. And when we separate from the body, and our soul has been awakened while in the body towards its perfection which is its beloved, and it did not attain it, while it is by nature aspiring to it, and since it intelligizes in actuality that it exists, except that immersion in the body, as we said, has made it forget itself and its beloved, as sickness makes one forget the need for what is metabolized.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and argument about the soul's current state. Ghazali first reaffirms that the rational soul is more perfect in itself and has a more intense perception, because it can grasp many objects, abstract essences, and penetrate both inner and outer aspects. He then addresses the apparent problem: if intellectual pleasures are so superior, why do we not feel them? The answer lies in the three principles established earlier: we are immersed in bodily vices, so we are like the sick person who cannot taste sweetness, or the animal that has no appetite. However, when we purify ourselves from appetite and anger, we get glimpses, especially in moments of intellectual discovery (حل المشكلات). Still, our experience is like smelling food compared to tasting it—vastly inferior. Ghazali then appeals to common experience: even worldly people often prefer intellectual goods (honor, shame, victory) over immediate sensual desires, proving the innate nobility of intellectual ends. But base souls are only aware of lowly things. Finally, Ghazali discusses the state after death: the soul, having been awakened in this life, will at separation from the body be drawn to its perfection. However, if it was preoccupied with the body, it forgets its true beloved, just as a sick person forgets the need for proper nourishment. The passage ends abruptly, but the point is that the soul must be trained to seek its true perfection.

Misconception: Intellectual pleasures are impossible to experience in this life or the next.
Correction: Ghazali shows they are attainable after purification from vices and fully after death; glimpses occur even in this life during intellectual breakthroughs.

The Five Modes of Existence and Their Role in Defining Tasdiq
Ghazali enumerates five modes of existence to clarify what constitutes tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five modes of existence according to Ghazali and how they affect whether one is considered a denier or affirmier of prophetic reports.

Core Idea
Affirming existence in any of its five modes saves one from being labeled a denier.

Before you can judge whether someone truly denies a prophetic report, you must first understand what 'existence' can mean. Ghazali lists five distinct modes of existence, each of which makes an affirmation valid. The passage below introduces these modes and warns against the common mistake of accusing others of denial simply because they affirm a different kind of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage attentively
Start by reading the Arabic passage once through. Don't worry about details; just get a sense of the flow. Then read the English translation to confirm your understanding.
Notice that Ghazali begins by linking tasdiq to the acknowledgment of existence.
2
Identify the five modes of existence
As you re-read, list the five modes: essential (dhātī), sensory (ḥissī), imaginative (khayālī), intellectual ('aqlī), and analogical (shabahī). Pay attention to how each is defined.
The definitions are brief; each mode corresponds to a different way something can be 'present'.
3
Understand each mode's distinct reality
For each mode, ask: In what sense does the thing 'exist'? For example, sensory existence is an image in the eye without external reality, while intellectual existence is a meaning grasped by the mind.
Take special note of analogical existence: it is when the thing itself does not exist, but something similar does.
4
Reflect on the implications for tasdiq
Consider how this framework prevents hasty accusations of denial. If someone affirms a report in any of these five ways, they are not a mukadhdhib (denier). This was Ghazali's broader aim.
This reflection connects the lesson to real-world theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : التصديق إنما يتطرق إلى الخبر ، بل إلى المخبر ، وحقيقته الاعتراف ، بوجود ما أخبر الرسول - صلى الله عليه وسلم - عن وجوده ، إلا أن للوجود خمس مرات ، ولأجل الغفلة عنها ، نسبت كل فرقة مخالفها إلى التكذيب . فإن الوجود ، ذاتي ، وحسي ، وخيالي ، وعقلي ، وشبهي ، فمن اعترف بوجود ما أخبر الرسول - عليه الصلاة والسلام - عن وجوده ، بوجه من هذه الوجوه الخمسة ، فليس بمكذب على الإطلاق . أما الوجود الذاتي فهو الوجود الحقيقي الثابت خارج الحس والعقل . وأما الوجود الحسي ، فهو ما يتمثل في القوة الباصرة من العين مما لا وجود له خارج العين ، وذلك كما يشاهد الغانم . ... إلخ . وأما الوجود الخيالي ، فهو صورة هذه المحسوسات إذا غابت عن حسك . . . إلخ . وأما الوجود العقلي فهو أن يكون للشيء روح وحقيقة ومعنى ، فيتلقى العقل مجرد معناه ، دون أن يثبت صورته في خيال أو خارج ، كاليد مثلا ، فإن لها صورة محسوسة ومتخيلة ، ولها معنى هو حقيقتها وهو القدرة على البطش ، والقدرة على البطش ، هي اليد العقلية . . . إلخ . وأما الوجود الشبهي ، فهو ألا يكون نفس الشيء موجوداً ، لا بصورته ، ولا بحقيقته ، لا في الخارج ، ولا في الحس ، ولا في الخيال ، ولا في العقل ، ولكن يكون الموجود شيئاً آخر يشبهه ، في خاصة من خواصه ، وصفة من صفاته . . . إلخ . ولا أحب أن أطيل باستيفاء هذه الأقسام وشرحها فإنه يطول ، بل ربما جر إلى نقل الكتاب برمته ، فارجع إليه ، فهو صغير وجيز
They said: Tasdiq pertains only to the report, rather to the reporter, and its reality is acknowledging the existence of what the Messenger - peace be upon him - informed about its existence, except that existence has five modes, and due to heedlessness of them, every group attributed its opponent to denial. So existence is: essential, sensory, imaginative, intellectual, and analogical. Whoever acknowledges the existence of what the Messenger informed about its existence in any of these five ways is not at all a denier. As for essential existence, it is the real existence established outside sense and intellect. As for sensory existence, it is what is represented in the seeing power of the eye, having no existence outside the eye, like what the mirage is seen as... etc. As for imaginative existence, it is the image of these sensed things when they are absent from your sense... etc. As for intellectual existence, it is that a thing has a spirit, reality, and meaning, so the intellect receives its mere meaning without its image being established in imagination or outside, like the hand for example: it has a sensory and imaginative image, and it has a meaning which is its reality, and that is the power to strike, and the power to strike is the intellectual hand... etc. As for analogical existence, it is that the thing itself is not existent, neither by its form nor by its reality, neither in the external, nor in sense, nor in imagination, nor in intellect, but something else similar to it exists in some of its properties and attributes... etc. And I do not wish to prolong by exhaustively covering these divisions and their explanation, for it would be lengthy; rather it might lead to quoting the entire book, so refer to it, for it is small and concise.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of the five modes of existence that determine whether one is considered a 'musaddiq' (affirmer) or 'mukadhdhib' (denier). It begins by stating that tasdiq fundamentally is acknowledging the existence of what the Prophet reported, but existence has five modes. Whoever affirms existence in any of these modes is not a denier. The five modes are: (1) Essential existence (wujud dhati): real, external existence independent of perception. (2) Sensory existence (wujud hissi): what appears to the senses (e.g., a mirage) but has no external reality. (3) Imaginative existence (wujud khayali): mental images of objects when they are absent. (4) Intellectual existence (wujud 'aqli): the abstract meaning of a thing, e.g., the 'hand' as power, not as a limb. (5) Analogical existence (wujud shabahi): when the thing itself does not exist in any mode, but something else resembling it exists. The author then notes that a full exposition would be lengthy and refers the reader to the original book (Faysal al-Tafriqa).

Key Terms
وجود ذاتي
وجود حسي
وجود خيالي
وجود عقلي
وجود شبهي
تصديق
تكذيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think that only literal, physical existence counts as real existence for affirming prophetic reports.
Correction: Ghazali shows that affirming existence in sensory, imaginative, intellectual, or analogical modes also counts as valid affirmation, preventing accusations of denial.

Checkpoint

A) Essential existence
B) Sensory existence
C) Imaginative existence
D) Intellectual existence

Key Takeaway
The five modes of existence expand what it means to affirm prophetic reports, preventing hasty accusations of denial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a prophetic report whose literal meaning you find difficult to accept. Which of the five modes could you sincerely use to affirm it without compromising your intellect? If you are reluctant, what does that reluctance reveal about your trust in the Prophet's message?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone else's faith as insufficient because they did not affirm a matter in the exact literal sense you demanded? In light of this lesson, will you now pause to consider which mode of existence they might be affirming before accusing them of denial?
?
This lesson shows that tasdiq is broader than you may have assumed. Does your own affirmation of the unseen cover all five modes, or have you unconsciously restricted it to essential existence? What would it cost you to expand your understanding and embrace the full scope of affirmation?

Ghazali's Refutation: Possibility as Mental Judgment
Philosophers argue for eternal matter; Ghazali responds by redefining possibility as mental judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the philosophical proof for the eternity of matter by redefining possibility as a mental judgment rather than a real attribute.

Core Idea
Possibility is not a thing in the world—it's a judgment of the mind.

The philosophers argue that the world's possibility is eternal and requires an eternal subject, matter. Ghazali responds by redefining possibility, impossibility, and necessity as purely mental categories. This cuts the ground from under the philosophers' argument, because mental judgments do not need to exist in external subjects.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the philosophers' argument that possibility is an eternal relational attribute needing a subject. Understand their deduction that matter is eternal.
The proof hinges on the idea that possibility is a real attribute existing in something.
2
Identify Ghazali's redefinition
Notice how Ghazali turns the tables by defining possibility, impossibility, and necessity as mental judgments. These are not real attributes inhering in anything.
Ghazali's move is to shift the ontology from external reality to mental conception.
3
See how the refutation works
Ask yourself: if possibility is just a mental judgment, does it need an eternal subject outside the mind? The answer is no, so the philosophers' proof collapses.
This is a classic example of philosophical therapy—diagnosing a category mistake.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل رابع للفلاسفة على قدم العالم قالوا : إمكان العالم قديم ، والإمكان وصف إضافى لا قوام له بنفسه ، فلابد له من محل يضاف إليه ، ولا محل إلا المادة فالمادة قديمة وقالوا : إمكان الشيء يغاير كونه مقدوراً عليه ؛ لأن أحدهما يعلل بالآخر فيقال : هو مقدور عليه ؛ لأنه ممكن ، والشيء لا يعلل بنفسه : ويجيب الغزالى : بأن الإمكان يرجع إلى قضاء العقل . فكل ما قدر العقل وجوده ، فلم يمتنع عليه تقديره ، سميناه ممكناً . . . وإن امتنع سميناه مستحيلا ... وإن لم يقدر على تقدير عدمه ، سميناه واجبا. فهذه قضايا عقلية لا تحتاج إلى موجود حتى تجعل وصفاً له
A fourth proof of the philosophers for the eternity of the world. They said: The possibility of the world is eternal, and possibility is a relational attribute, having no subsistence by itself, so it must have a subject to which it is attributed, and there is no subject except matter, so matter is eternal. And they said: The possibility of a thing is different from its being an object of power; for one of them is caused by the other, so it is said: it is an object of power because it is possible, and a thing is not caused by itself. And al-Ghazali answers: That possibility goes back to a judgment of the mind. For everything whose existence the mind can conceive, and its estimation is not impossible, we call it possible... and if it is impossible, we call it impossible... and if it cannot conceive its non-existence, we call it necessary. So these are mental propositions that do not need an existent thing to be made an attribute of it.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth philosophical proof and Ghazali's initial response. The philosophers argue that the possibility of the world is eternal. Since possibility is a relational attribute (صفة إضافية) that cannot subsist by itself, it requires an eternal subject. The only plausible subject is matter, so matter is eternal. They add that possibility differs from being an object of power (مقدور عليه), since the latter is explained by the former. Ghazali responds by redefining possibility as a mental judgment (قضاء العقل). Whatever the mind can conceive without contradiction is 'possible'; what it cannot conceive without contradiction is 'impossible'; what it cannot conceive as non-existent is 'necessary'. These are mental categories, not real attributes needing an external subject.

Key Terms
وصف إضافي (relational attribute)
قضاء العقل (judgment of the mind)
ممكن (possible)
مستحيل (impossible)
واجب (necessary)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is a real attribute that must inhere in a subject.
Correction: Ghazali argues it is a mental judgment, not a real attribute inhering in anything.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's response, what are possibility, impossibility, and necessity?
A) Real attributes inhering in matter.
B) Relational attributes needing a subject.
C) Mental judgments of the mind.
D) Properties of the world.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali redefines possibility as a mental judgment, thereby dismantling the argument that eternal possibility requires eternal matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own limitations, do you tend to treat them as fixed realities 'out there' or as mental judgments that can be re-evaluated? What would change if you saw possibility as a judgment of your mind, not a thing in the world?
?
Can you recall a time when you dismissed something as 'impossible' simply because you couldn't conceive of it, only to later see it become possible? How does this lesson challenge you to revisit areas of your life where you have closed off possibility?
?
If Allah's attributes like power and will are not dependent on any external subject, how does that deepen your understanding of His absolute independence? Does your own dependence on external subjects (e.g., health, resources) ever make you feel distant from relying solely on Him?

Objection 1: Mental Judgment is Knowledge
First objection: mental judgment is knowledge, possibility is known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to Ghazali's theory that possibility is a mental judgment: that mental judgment is knowledge, possibility is something known, and knowledge and the known are distinct.

Core Idea
Possibility cannot be just a mental judgment because it is the object, not the act, of knowledge.

The philosophers object to Ghazali's reduction of possibility to a mental act. They argue that the mind's judgment is a form of knowledge (علم), whereas possibility is the thing known (معلوم). Knowledge and the known are different, so possibility cannot be identical to the mental judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the three components: mental judgment is knowledge, possibility is known, knowledge is not the known.
2
Distinguish subject and object
Understand the distinction between the act of knowing (subject) and the thing known (object). The objection claims possibility belongs to the object side.
This is a classic epistemological distinction.
3
Connect to Ghazali's view
Recall that Ghazali argued possibility is a mental judgment. This objection says that makes it the act, not the object — but possibility is clearly an object of thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض على الغزالى بأن قضاء العقل من قبيل العلم ، والإمكان شيء معلوم ، والعلم غير المعلوم وبأن الامتناع يقتضى موجوداً يضاف إليه ، فإنه إذا كان المحل أبيض كان ممتنعاً عليه أن يسود ؛ فالمحل الأبيض الموجود هو الموصوف بامتناع أن يصير مع بياضه أسود و بأن مرد قولنا : السواد ممكن أن يوجد ، إلى أن الجسم ممكن أن يكون أسود ؛ لأن العرض من غير محل يقوم به ، مستحيل الوجود ؛ فإمكان وجود العرض ، هو نفس إمكان أن تتصف به المادة .
The objection to al-Ghazali: That the judgment of the mind is of the kind of knowledge, and possibility is something known, and knowledge is other than the known. And that impossibility requires an existent thing to be attributed to it; for if the substrate is white, it is impossible for it to become black; so the existent white substrate is described by the impossibility of becoming black while being white. And that the purport of our saying 'blackness is possible to exist' amounts to that the body is possible to be black; because an accident without a substrate subsisting in it is impossible of existence; so the possibility of the existence of the accident is itself the possibility that the matter be qualified by it.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage presents three objections to Ghazali's view that possibility is a mental judgment. First, the mental judgment is a type of knowledge (علم), but possibility is the known object (معلوم), and knowledge is distinct from the known. So possibility cannot be reduced to a mental act. Second, impossibility also seems to require a real subject: a white surface is described as having the impossibility of becoming black while white; that impossibility is attributed to the surface as a real property. Third, when we say an accident like blackness is possible, this really means the body is capable of being black. Accidents cannot exist without a substrate, so the possibility of the accident is identical to the possibility of the matter to receive it. Therefore, possibility must refer to a real capacity in matter. (Note: Ghazali's response to these objections is not included in the provided pages; the passage ends here.)

Key Terms
العلم (knowledge)
المعلوم (the known)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is just an idea in the mind, so it is the same as the mental judgment.
Correction: Even as an idea, it is the object of the judgment, not the judgment itself. The objection distinguishes the act of knowing from what is known.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what is the relationship between knowledge (العلم) and the known (المعلوم)?
A) They are identical.
B) They are different.
C) Knowledge includes the known.
D) The known is a part of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The first objection to Ghazali is that possibility cannot be a mental judgment because it is the object, not the act, of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a judgment about something being possible or impossible, are you aware that your judgment is an act of knowing, not the actual nature of the thing? How often do you conflate your mental assumptions with objective reality?
?
Is there an area of your life where you treat your own mental judgments as if they are the only truth, ignoring the distinction between your knowledge and the actual known? Will you make a conscious effort this week to separate your personal opinions from the facts?
?
If possibility is something known (an object) and not just a mental judgment, then what does that mean for how you should approach understanding reality? Are you willing to seek knowledge that is grounded in the known rather than relying solely on your mental constructs?

Objection 2: Impossibility Requires a Real Subject
Second objection: impossibility needs an existent subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to Ghazali: that impossibility must be attributed to an existing substrate, not merely a mental judgment.

Core Idea
Impossibility is not just mental — it inheres in real things like a white surface's inability to be black.

The second objection targets Ghazali's claim that impossibility is also a mental judgment. The philosophers argue that impossibility requires a real subject to be attributed to. For example, a white surface has the real property of being unable to become black while remaining white. This impossibility is a description of the surface itself, not just a mental act.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the example of the white substrate that cannot become black.
2
Identify the logical structure
The objection asserts: impossibility (الامتناع) demands an existent thing (موجود) to which it is attributed. The example shows this: the existing white substrate is described by the impossibility of becoming black.
3
Contrast with Ghazali's view
If impossibility were merely a mental judgment, it would not need a real substrate. The example proves it does, so impossibility cannot be purely mental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a sin you often fall into. What 'whiteness' in your soul makes it impossible for you to commit that sin while you remain in a state of faith? Is that impossibility real in your heart, or just a mental thought? How can you make it so real that the sin becomes as impossible as a white surface turning black?
?
You have attributes that you consider 'fixed' — like being a believer or possessing honesty. ?

Is that impossibility a real property of your soul (decree of Allah), or a self-imposed mental block? Submit to Allah's decree, but also ask yourself: are you using impossibility as an excuse to avoid effort?

Objection 3: Accident Possibility is Matter's Possibility
Third objection: accident possibility is matter's possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to Ghazali: that the possibility of an accident is identical to the possibility of the matter that receives it.

Core Idea
The possibility of an accident like blackness is just the possibility of the matter to be qualified by it.

The final objection challenges Ghazali's mental reduction by focusing on accidents (أعراض). When we say an accident like blackness is possible, we really mean that a body is capable of being black. Since accidents cannot exist without a substrate, their possibility reduces to the possibility of the matter that receives them. Therefore, possibility is a real capacity in matter, not a mental judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the statement: 'the possibility of the existence of the accident is itself the possibility that the matter be qualified by it.'
2
Understand the dependency
Recall the philosophical principle: an accident cannot exist without a substrate. So the possibility of an accident depends on the possibility of the substrate.
3
Connect to Ghazali's view
If possibility were merely mental, then the substrate's capacity would be mental too. But clearly the substrate has a real capacity to receive accidents. Thus possibility is real.

Source Text

Rational Judgments

The Three Rational Judgments: Possibility, Impossibility, Necessity
Ghazali defines the three rational judgments and argues they need no existing subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that possibility, impossibility, and necessity are not real attributes of things but pure judgments of the intellect, and why they do not require an existing subject.

Core Idea
The three modal categories are judgments of reason, not real properties.

Ghazali opens his response by establishing a fundamental epistemological point: possibility, impossibility, and necessity are not features of things in themselves but rather how the intellect judges them. Before we can discuss the world's possibility or God's necessity, we need to see that these are rational categories, not physical attributes. This passage lays the groundwork for the entire Ashʿarī critique of the philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the three categories and how Ghazali defines each in terms of the intellect's ability to conceive existence or non-existence.
The definitions are all based on 'the intellect's conception' (qadar al-ʿaql).
2
Identify the key claim
After defining the three, Ghazali states: 'These are rational judgments that do not need an existent thing to be made an attribute of it.' This is the central thesis. Ask yourself why it matters that they are not real attributes.
3
Follow the reductio argument
Ghazali argues that if possibility needed an existent subject, then impossibility would also need one—which is absurd. Then he gives the example of whiteness and blackness before existence. Work through the logic: who would the possibility belong to, the body or the whiteness?
This argument shows that possibility is not a property of anything concrete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجيب الغزالى : بأن الإمكان يرجع إلى قضاء العقل . فكل ما قدر العقل وجوده ، فلم يمتنع عليه تقديره ، سميناه ممكناً . . . وإن امتنع سميناه مستحيلا ... وإن لم يقدر على تقدير عدمه ، سميناه واجبا. فهذه قضايا عقلية لا تحتاج إلى موجود حتى تجعل وصفاً له ويضيف الغزالي : أنه لو استدعى الإمكان شيئاً موجوداً يضاف إليه ، لاستدعى الامتناع شيئاً موجوداً يضاف إليه و إن البياض والسواد ممكنان قبل وجودهما ، فإن كان الإمكان وصفاً الذي يطرآن عليه ، كان إمكاناً للجسم لا لهما ، للجسم والمفروض أنهما الممكنان
And al-Ghazali answers: that possibility (al-imkān) reduces to a judgment of the intellect. So everything the intellect can conceive its existence, and it is not prevented from conceiving it, we call it possible (mumkin). And if it is prevented, we call it impossible (mustaḥīl). And if it cannot conceive its non-existence, we call it necessary (wājib). These are rational judgments (qaḍāyā ʿaqliyya) that do not need an existent thing to be made an attribute of it. And al-Ghazali adds: that if possibility required an existent thing to be attributed to it, then impossibility would also require an existent thing to be attributed to it. And [he says] that whiteness and blackness are possible before their existence; if possibility were an attribute of that which they inhere in, it would be a possibility for the body, not for them, even though they are assumed to be the possible things.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the three fundamental modal categories in Ashʿarī epistemology. Ghazali begins by stating that possibility (imkān) is a judgment of the intellect (qaḍāʾ al-ʿaql). He then gives a threefold classification: (1) whatever the intellect can conceive as existing and does not find any contradiction in that conception is called 'possible' (mumkin); (2) whatever the intellect cannot conceive as existing (i.e., finds a contradiction) is called 'impossible' (mustaḥīl); (3) whatever the intellect cannot conceive of as non-existing (i.e., its non-existence is inconceivable) is called 'necessary' (wājib). These three are rational judgments (qaḍāyā ʿaqliyya). Crucially, they do not require an actual existent entity to which they belong; they are not real properties of things. Ghazali then offers a reductio argument: if possibility required an external existent to which it is attributed, then impossibility would also require such an existent—but impossibility clearly does not require a subject (e.g., a partner of God is impossible but has no subject). He illustrates with the example of whiteness and blackness: before they exist, they are possible, but if possibility were an attribute of the body that will receive them, it would be the body's possibility, not theirs, even though we speak of 'possible whiteness' meaning the whiteness itself is possible, not the body. This establishes that the three categories are not real attributes inhering in substances but judgments of the intellect.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الوجوب
الامتناع
قضاء العقل
قضايا عقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility, impossibility, and necessity are real properties inherent in things, like whiteness or blackness.
Correction: Ghazali argues they are purely rational judgments (qaḍāyā ʿaqliyya) and do not need an existent subject. They are not attributes that inhere in substances.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which of the following is true about the three categories (possible, impossible, necessary)?
A) They are real attributes that inhere in substances.
B) They are judgments of the intellect and do not require an existent subject.
C) They are only applicable to material objects.
D) They are derived from sense perception.

Key Takeaway
Possibility, impossibility, and necessity are rational judgments that do not correspond to real attributes in things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say something is 'possible'—like gaining sincere devotion or quitting a sin—do you treat that possibility as a fixed reality outside your control, or as a judgment of your mind that you can change by strengthening your intellect and will? Are you hiding behind a 'property' you think you lack?
?
Is there something in your life you have labelled 'impossible' that is merely difficult? Could that judgment be a rational judgment of your mind, not a real property of the thing? If so, what would it take for you to reconsider that judgment today?
?
You affirm that only God is necessary. Do you live as if your own existence is necessary? Pause and reflect: your existence, your health, your faith—all are merely possible, sustained by God each moment. Does that awareness translate into gratitude and reliance, or have you become complacent? What would change if you truly felt that your very being is a possibility that could cease at any instant?

Ghazali's Response on Possibility and Impossibility
Philosophers' objections and Ghazali's response on the subject of possibility and impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that possibility, necessity, and impossibility are rational judgments (mental universals) that do not require existing subjects.

Core Idea
Impossibility does not always need a subject; some impossibilities have no subject at all.

Philosophers argued that concepts like possibility and impossibility must be properties of real external things—e.g., a white surface cannot become black while white. Ghazali counters by showing that these are rational categories abstracted by the mind, and that absolute impossibilities like 'a partner of God' have no subject. This lesson examines the objections and Ghazali's decisive response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections carefully
Read the three objections the philosophers raise against Ghazali. Notice that each tries to tie possibility or impossibility to an external subject.
The objections appear in the first part of the passage.
2
Identify Ghazali's key move
Ghazali reframes possibility, necessity, and impossibility as 'rational judgments' (qaḍāyā ʿaqliyya) and compares them to universals like colority. Understand why he says these exist only in the mind, not in external objects.
He appeals to the philosophers' own admission that universals are mental.
3
Examine the counterexample
Ghazali gives a concrete example: 'a partner of God is impossible.' Ask yourself: what existing subject does this impossibility inhere in? None. This disproves the claim that impossibility always requires a subject.
This is a powerful refutation because it uses a case both sides agree is impossible.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this means for theology: if possibility and impossibility are mental judgments, then we need not search for physical substrates for divine attributes or actions. God's uniqueness is safeguarded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض على الغزالى بأن قضاء العقل من قبيل العلم ، والإمكان شيء معلوم ، والعلم غير المعلوم وبأن الامتناع يقتضى موجوداً يضاف إليه ، فإنه إذا كان المحل أبيض كان ممتنعاً عليه أن يسود ؛ فالمحل الأبيض الموجود هو الموصوف بامتناع أن يصير مع بياضه أسود و بأن مرد قولنا : السواد ممكن أن يوجد ، إلى أن الجسم ممكن أن يكون أسود ؛ لأن العرض من غير محل يقوم به ، مستحيل الوجود ؛ فإمكان وجود العرض ، هو نفس إمكان أن تتصف به المادة . . . وقد أجاب الغزالى بأن الإمكان والوجوب والامتناع قضايا عقلية ، وجود المعلوماتها فى الأعيان ، مثلها مثل اللونية والحيوانية وسائر القضايا الكلية ؛ فهي علوم ، ولا وجود المعلوماتها في الأعيان ؛ إذ الفلاسفة صرحوا بأن الكليات موجودة في الأذهان لا في الأعيان و إنما الموجود فى الأعيان جزئيات مشخصة ينتزع العقل منها هذه الكليات وأما القول بأن الامتناع مضاف إلى موجود ، فليس كذلك في جميع الممتنعات ؛ فإن شريك البارى ممتنع ؛ فبماذا يقوم امتناعه
The objection against al-Ghazali: that the judgment of the intellect is a kind of knowledge, and possibility is something known, and knowledge is other than the known. And [the objection] that impossibility requires an existent thing to which it is attributed; for if a locus is white, it is impossible for it to become black; so the existing white locus is what is described by the impossibility of becoming black while it is white. And [the objection] that the meaning of our saying 'blackness is possible to exist' reduces to the body being possible to be black; because an accident without a substrate in which it subsists is impossible in existence; so the possibility of existence of the accident is the very possibility that the matter be qualified by it. ... And al-Ghazali answered: that possibility, necessity, and impossibility are rational judgments; their existence [as known] is in external objects, like the [universals of] colority, animality, and all universal judgments; they are knowledges, and they have no existence [as known] in external objects; for the philosophers have stated explicitly that universals exist in minds, not in external objects; only individuated particulars exist in external objects, from which the intellect extracts these universals. And as for the statement that impossibility is attributed to an existent, it is not so in all impossibilities; for a partner of the Creator is impossible; so in what does its impossibility subsist?
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage presents three objections from the philosophers followed by Ghazali's response. The first objection: the intellect's judgment is knowledge (ʿilm), and possibility (imkān) is the known object (maʿlūm). Knowledge and the known are distinct, so possibility is something real apart from the intellect. The second: impossibility (imtināʿ) requires an existing subject; e.g., a white surface cannot become black while white—the impossibility is a property of that existing white surface. The third: when we say 'blackness is possible,' we mean the body can be black; because an accident cannot exist without a substrate, so possibility of an accident is the possibility of its matter to receive it. Ghazali's response: these three categories are rational judgments (qaḍāyā ʿaqliyya). He argues that just like colority (lawniyya) and animality (ḥayawāniyya)—which are universals—these judgments exist as known objects only in the mind, not in external reality. The philosophers themselves affirm that universals exist only in the mind, not externally; only particularized individuals exist externally, and the intellect abstracts universals from them. Then he addresses the specific objection about impossibility: not all impossibilities require an existing subject. For example, a partner of God (sharīk al-Bārī) is impossible, yet there is no existing subject for this impossibility to inhere in. Thus the objection fails.

العلم
المعلوم
الامتناع
شريك البارى
كليات
جزئيات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Impossibility always requires an existing subject that bears the property.
Correction: Ghazali shows that absolute impossibilities like 'a partner of God' have no subject; impossibility is a mental judgment.

Checkpoint

A) The impossibility of a black object being white
B) The impossibility of a body being in two places
C) The impossibility of a partner of God
D) The impossibility of an accident without a substrate

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that possibility, necessity, and impossibility are mental categories, not real attributes of external objects; some impossibilities like 'partner of God' have no subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali uses the example of a partner of the Creator to show that some impossibilities have no subject. How does this challenge the way you think about God's oneness? Do you ever treat tawhid as a theoretical concept rather than a reality that has no possible exception? What would it mean for you to live as if the impossibility of a partner of God were not just a statement but a truth that governs your every thought and action?
?
If possibility and impossibility are merely rational judgments existing in your mind, as Ghazali argues, then what does that say about the certainty of your beliefs? Are there things you consider impossible that might actually be possible? What does this lesson reveal about the reliability of your own intellect in discerning truth?
?
Consider a time when you assumed something was impossible because it lacked a clear subject or condition. Did that assumption affect your actions? Now, think about the absolute impossibility of a partner of God—does this truth actively shape your daily relationship with God? If not, what would it take for you to let this impossibility transform your worship and trust in Him alone?

Modal Judgments as Rational Constructs
Ghazali refutes the view that imkan, wujoob, imtina' have external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that possibility, necessity, and impossibility are rational judgments with no external existence, and how this refutes the philosophers' corresponding claims.

Core Idea
Modal judgments are in the mind, not in the world.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a fundamental issue in metaphysics: where do categories like possibility and necessity actually exist? The philosophers had treated them as real properties of things, but Ghazali counters that they are mere rational constructs. He uses the philosophers' own theory of universals to undermine their position. By the end, you'll see how Ghazali strategically dismantles a cornerstone of their ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from discussion of the soul to the main argument.
The passage begins with different opinions on the soul; don't get distracted, the key point comes later.
2
Identify Ghazali's main claim
Pinpoint the sentence where Ghazali states that possibility, necessity, and impossibility are rational judgments (qadaya 'aqliyya) with no external existence. This is the core thesis.
Look for: 'وقد أجاب الغزالي بأن الإمكان والوجوب والامتناع قضايا عقلية'.
3
Analyze the argument using universals
Notice how Ghazali compares modal judgments to universals like 'whiteness' and 'animality' that exist only in the mind. He cites the philosophers' own position that universals are mental.
This is a clever twist: he uses their own doctrine against them.
4
Examine the objection and response
Read the part about impossibility being attributed to something existent. Ghazali gives the example of a partner to God (sharik al-bari) to show the flaw in that claim.
The response is rhetorical—'by what does its impossibility subsist?'—highlighting the absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النفس فقد قيل: إنها قديمة، وإمكانها معناه: إمكان تعلقها بالأبدان. وقيل: إنها منطبعة في مادة، تابعة للمزاج، فتكون في مادة، وإمكانها مضاف إلى مادتها. ومن قال: إنها حادثة ومجردة عن المادة، فإمكانها معناه: أن المادة ممكن لها أن تدبرها نفس ناطقة. فلم يلزم شيء مما قيل سابقاً. وقد أجاب الغزالي بأن الإمكان والوجوب والامتناع قضايا عقلية، لا وجود لمعلوماتها في الأعيان، مثلها مثل اللونية والحيوانية وسائر القضايا الكلية؛ فهي علوم، ولا وجود لمعلوماتها في الأعيان؛ إذ الفلاسفة صرحوا بأن الكليات موجودة في الأذهان لا في الأعيان، وإنما الموجود في الأعيان جزئيات مشخصة ينتزع العقل منها هذه الكليات. وأما القول بأن الامتناع مضاف إلى موجود، فليس كذلك في جميع الممتنعات؛ فإن شريك الباري ممتنع؛ فبماذا يقوم امتناعه؟ يبدو من موقف الغزالي خلال بحث مسألة قدم العالم أنه يحاول مقابلة الإشكالات بالإشكالات، ويجيب الغزالي عن هذا بأن المعارضة تبين فساد الكلام لا محالة. . . . ونحن لم نلتزم في هذا الكتاب إلا تكدير مذهب الفلاسفة.
As for the soul, it has been said: it is eternal, and its possibility means: the possibility of its attachment to bodies. And it has been said: it is impressed in matter, dependent on temperament, so it is in matter, and its possibility is attributable to its matter. And whoever said it is temporally originated and abstracted from matter, its possibility means: that matter can be managed by a rational soul. Then nothing follows from what was said previously. And al-Ghazali answered that possibility, necessity, and impossibility are rational judgments; their objects have no existence in external entities, like whiteness, animality, and other universal judgments; they are items of knowledge, and their objects have no existence in external entities; for the philosophers have stated that universals exist in minds, not in external entities, and only particularized individuals exist in external entities, from which the intellect extracts these universals. And as for the statement that impossibility is attributable to something existent, it is not so in all impossibles; for a partner to the Creator is impossible; by what does its impossibility subsist? It appears from al-Ghazali's position during the discussion of the question of the world's eternity that he attempts to counter objections with objections, and al-Ghazali answers this by saying that contradiction inevitably shows the corruption of speech. . . . And we have not undertaken in this book except to muddy the doctrine of the philosophers.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a mixed discussion on the nature of the soul and the status of modal judgments (imkan, wujoob, imtina'). It begins by presenting different opinions on the soul: some say the soul is eternal and its possibility means possible attachment to bodies; others say it is impressed in matter; others say it is originated and abstract, and its possibility means matter can be managed by it. Ghazali then refutes that these views entail anything problematic. He explains that possibility, necessity, and impossibility are rational judgments (qadaya 'aqliyya), not real properties of external objects. Like universals (e.g., whiteness, animality), they exist only in the mind. He cites the philosophers' own position that universals exist in the mind, not externally. Then he addresses the claim that impossibility must be attributed to something existent; he counters by giving the example of a partner to God (sharik al-bari) being impossible, questioning what that impossibility subsists in. The passage then notes Ghazali's method of opposing objections with objections, and states his overall aim in the book is to disrupt the philosophers' doctrines.

Key Takeaway
Modal judgments (possibility, necessity, impossibility) are rational constructs, not real features of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

But after this lesson, ask yourself: are you treating that impossibility as a fixed external reality, or as a judgment that exists only in your mind? How would your life change if you truly believed that God can make anything possible? Will you, this week, identify one 'impossible' thing in your life and actively challenge it as a mental construct, not a real barrier?
?
Ghazali says possibility is a rational judgment, not a real attribute. Think of a sin you consider 'possible' to do again. Is that possibility a real property of you, or a mental construct? If possibility is only in the mind, you have the power to change that judgment. Will you repent with the conviction that you can be different and that your future is not determined by past patterns?
?
The philosophers admitted universals are in the mind, yet they treated modal judgments as real. You might do the same—believing that 'this is necessary' or 'that is impossible' as if it were an unchangeable law. But this lesson says these are rational judgments. Can you identify one belief you hold about yourself or your circumstances that you have treated as necessary or impossible, and commit to re-examining it as a mental construct?
