Philosophers' Proof for Soul's Indivisibility and Ghazali's Objections
Philosophers' first proof for soul's indivisibility and Ghazali's two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the soul is indivisible because it houses indivisible knowledge, and Ghazali's two objections that challenge the inference from indivisibility of knowledge to indivisibility of the soul.

Core Idea
Divisible bodies cannot host indivisible knowledge? Ghazali says: not so fast.

The philosophers claim that since intellectual knowledge contains indivisible concepts, the soul that hosts this knowledge must itself be indivisible. But Ghazali offers two incisive objections: first, why can't the soul be an indivisible atom? Second, even if we accept that inhering in a divisible body implies divisibility, cases like the sheep's perception of a wolf's enmity show that indivisible judgments exist in divisible bodies. This lesson unpacks both the proof and the objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Note the premise: knowledge contains indivisible units. Conclusion: the soul's locus is indivisible.
Notice that the proof assumes that what contains indivisible parts must itself be indivisible.
2
Identify Ghazali's first objection
Ghazali asks: why reject that the locus is an indivisible atom? This challenges the assumption that the soul must be non-physical.
The atom (jawhar fard) is still a body but indivisible, so the conclusion does not rule out a physical soul.
3
Examine the second objection with the sheep and wolf example
The sheep's estimative power perceives the wolf's enmity as a single indivisible judgment, yet the sheep's body is divisible. This refutes the claim that all inherence implies divisibility.
This counterexample targets the underlying principle linking indivisibility of content to indivisibility of locus.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Ghazali's objections undermine the proof's logic without necessarily denying the conclusion. The soul may still be indivisible, but not for the reasons given.
This is a classic example of refuting an argument rather than a thesis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الأول : أن العلوم العقلية تحل النفوس الإنسانية وفيها آحاد لا تنقسم فلابد أن يكون محلها لا ينقسم . وكل جسم فمنقسم قال الغزالي : والاعتراض على مقامين : المقام الأول : أن يقال : بم تنكرون على من يقول : محل العلم جوهر فرد متحيز لا ينقسم . المقام الثاني : أن نقول : ما زعمتموه من أن كل حال في أن ينقسم، باطل عليكم بما تدركه القوة الوهمية التي في الشاة ، من عداوة الذئب ؛ فإنها في حكم شيء واحد لا ينقسم
The first proof: that the intellectual sciences inhere in human souls and contain indivisible units, so necessarily their locus is indivisible. And every body is divisible. Al-Ghazali said: The objection is in two stages: The first stage: that one says: Why do you deny the one who says: the locus of knowledge is an atomic substance, occupying space, indivisible? The second stage: that we say: what you claim that everything that inheres [in a body] is divisible is refuted upon you by what the estimative power in the sheep perceives of the enmity of the wolf; for it is in the judgment of a single thing, indivisible.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first rational proof for the soul's indivisibility and Ghazali's two objections. The proof runs: intellectual knowledge (al-`ulūm al-`aqliyya) inheres in human souls and contains indivisible units (āḥād lā tanqasim), so the locus of that knowledge must itself be indivisible. But every body (jism) is divisible; therefore the soul is not a body but an indivisible substance. Ghazali then raises two objections (maqāmān). First, why do you reject the possibility that the locus of knowledge is an atomic substance (jawhar fard mutaḥayyiz) that is indivisible? That is, the conclusion that the soul is an indivisible substance could be accepted, but it does not prove it is not a body in the sense of an atom. Second, even if we grant that what inheres in a divisible body must be divisible, the example of the sheep's estimative power (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya) perceiving the enmity of the wolf as a single indivisible perception shows that perception need not be divisible despite being in a divisible body. Thus the philosophers' claim that all inherence implies divisibility is false.

Key Terms
العلوم العقلية
آحاد لا تنقسم
جوهر فرد
متحيز
القوة الوهمية
عداوة الذئب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali's objections disprove the soul's indivisibility itself.
Correction: Ghazali only refutes the philosophers' proof; he does not claim the soul is divisible. He may accept the conclusion but not their reasoning.

Checkpoint
What example does Ghazali use to refute the claim that everything inhering in a divisible body must be divisible?
A) The reflection in a mirror
B) The perception of a wolf's enmity by a sheep's estimative power
C) The indivisibility of the intellect
D) The multiplicity of colors in an apple

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's two objections show that the philosophers' argument from indivisible knowledge to an indivisible soul is not logically airtight, as alternative explanations and counterexamples remain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a piece of knowledge you hold that you consider 'indivisible'—a truth you cannot break down further. If someone attacked the logical argument you use to justify it, would you be able to defend it? Ghazali's critique shows that even seemingly strong arguments can have hidden assumptions. What assumption in your own belief system might need re-examination?
?
The sheep perceives the wolf's enmity as a single, indivisible perception. When you feel a deep conviction—whether about God, yourself, or a moral truth—do you experience it as a unified whole, or as a chain of logical steps? How might your reliance on reasoning sometimes obscure the directness of that perception?
?
Ghazali does not simply accept the philosophers' conclusion nor reject it outright—he dismantles their proof. Is there a belief you hold that you have accepted because it 'makes sense' logically, but you have never personally examined the evidence against it? Will you take time today to test one such belief against counterarguments?

The Second Proof: Nothing New
Ghazali reduces the second proof to the first and gives the same answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali treats the philosophers' second proof as a restatement of the first and how his response applies identically.

Core Idea
A duplicate argument deserves the same answer.

The philosophers offer a second proof for the soul's immateriality, but Ghazali quickly dismisses it as a mere variation of the first. In this brief passage, he shows that the same reasoning and the same refutation apply. This lesson examines why Ghazali sees no need for a new response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the ellipsis and how Ghazali states the proof reduces to the first.
The ellipsis indicates Ghazali is summarising, not quoting fully.
2
Identify the core claim
The proof is about 'impression of knowledge' and its division by the division of its place. This is the same concept from the first proof.
Impression (intiṭābā`) refers to knowledge being 'imprinted' in a substrate.
3
Understand Ghazali's response
Ghazali says the answer is exactly the same as before. So the second proof adds nothing new to the debate.
This shows Ghazali's economy: he does not waste time on redundant arguments.
4
Review the first proof's answer
If needed, recall that Ghazali refuted the first proof by denying the analogy between physical impression and intellectual cognition. That same answer suffices here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الثاني : وهو يؤول إلى انطباع العلم، وانقسامه بانقسام محله على ما مر في الدليل الأول . . . وجوابه هو نفس الجواب
The second proof: and it reduces to the impression of knowledge and its division by the division of its place as has passed in the first proof . . . And its answer is the same answer.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This short passage presents the philosophers' second proof and Ghazali's response. The proof is described only in summary: it reduces to the concept of knowledge being impressed (intiṭābā` al-`ilm) and therefore divisible as its place is divisible—the same idea as in the first proof. Consequently, Ghazali states that the answer (jawāb) is the very same answer he gave to the first proof. This indicates that the second proof offers no new argument, and the earlier refutation suffices.

Key Terms
الدليل الثاني
انطباع العلم
الجواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second proof for the soul is a distinct and stronger argument than the first.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states it reduces to the first proof, and the answer is identical, so it is not a new argument.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali respond to the philosophers' second proof for the soul?
A) He presents a new objection
B) He says the answer is the same as for the first proof
C) He accepts it as valid
D) He refers to a later discussion

Key Takeaway
The second proof for the soul is not a new argument; Ghazali's refutation of the first proof suffices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been persuaded by an argument that sounded new, only to realize later it was essentially the same as one you had already rejected? How does Ghazali's method challenge you to examine the core of every claim before accepting it?
?
In your own faith and worldview, are there beliefs you hold that you think are supported by multiple independent proofs? What would it mean for your certainty if you discovered they all relied on the same unexamined assumption? Will you take time this week to identify one such assumption and test it honestly?
?
Ghazali dismisses the second proof with a single sentence: 'its answer is the same answer.' Are there areas of your life where you keep revisiting the same doubts, arguments, or sins, hoping for a different outcome, when the answer has already been given? What would change if you fully accepted that the answer is already there?

Refutation of the Sixth Proof for Intellect's Immateriality
Ghazali presents the sixth proof and answers it by reference to the fifth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' argument that the intellect cannot be physical because it perceives its own instrument.

Core Idea
Ghazali dismisses the proof by linking it to an earlier answer.

The philosophers argue that if intellect were physical, it couldn't perceive its own instrument, yet it does. Ghazali simply refers to his previous rebuttal, showing his method of handling similar arguments without repetition.

Guided Path
1
Read the sixth proof
Read the philosophers' argument carefully. They use an analogy with other senses that do not perceive their own organs.
Note the structure: conditional statement leading to a conclusion.
2
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is that intellect does not perceive by a physical instrument because it perceives its alleged instrument (heart/brain).
3
Examine Ghazali's response
Ghazali says the answer is like the answer to the fifth proof. This means he has already refuted a similar line of reasoning.
What was the fifth proof? It is not given here, but you can infer that Ghazali's method is to refer back.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل السادس : قالوا : لو كان العقل يدرك بآلة جسمانية . كالإبصار ، لما أدرك آلته ، كسائر الحواس ، ولكنه يدرك القلب والدماغ ، وما يدعى أنه آلته ، فدل أنه لا يدرك بآلة جسمانية.
قال الغزالى : والجواب عنه كالجواب عن الدليل الخامس

الدليل السابع : القوى المدركة بالآلات الجسمانية يعرض لها من المواظبة على العمل بإدامة الإدراك كلال ، والعقل على العكس من هذا
قال الغزالي : نقول : لا يبعد أن تختلف الحواس الجسمانية في هذه الأمور .

الدليل الثامن : أجزاء البدن كلها تضعف عند الأربعين فما بعدها .... والقوة العقلية فى أكثر الأمور إنما تقوى بعد ذلك
قال الغزالي : والاعتراض أن نقول : نقصان القوى وزيادتها لها أسباب كثيرة ، فقد يقوى بعض القوى في ابتداء العمر ، وبعضها في الوسط ، وبعضها في آخره

الدليل التاسع : كيف يكون الإنسان هو الجسم مع عوارضه ، وهذه الأجسام لا تزال تنحل ، والغذاء يسد مسد ما ينحل
قال الغزالي : هذا ينتقض بالبهيمة والشجرة ، إذا قيست حال كبرهما بحال الصغر

الدليل العاشر : أنا ندرك من الشيء الواحد أمراً جزئيا، وأمراً كلياً . والجزئى يدرك بالحس ، والكلى المجرد يدرك بالعقل.
قال الغزالى : إن المعنى الكلى الذى وضعتموه حالا في العقل ، غير مسلم ، بل لا يحل في العقل إلا ما لا يحل في الحس
The sixth proof: They said: If the intellect perceived by a physical instrument like sight, it would not perceive its instrument, like the other senses, but it perceives the heart and brain and what is claimed to be its instrument, so it indicates that it does not perceive by a physical instrument. Al-Ghazali said: The answer to it is like the answer to the fifth proof. The seventh proof: The powers that perceive by physical instruments are subject to fatigue from persevering in action by continuous perception, while the intellect is opposite to this. Al-Ghazali said: We say: It is not unlikely that the physical senses differ in these matters. The eighth proof: All parts of the body weaken at forty years and after, while the intellectual power in most matters only strengthens after that. Al-Ghazali said: The objection is that we say: The decrease and increase of powers have many causes; some powers may strengthen at the beginning of life, some in the middle, and some at its end. The ninth proof: How can a human be the body with its accidents, while these bodies continually dissolve and food replaces what dissolves? Al-Ghazali said: This is refuted by the animal and tree, when comparing their state at bigness to their state at smallness. The tenth proof: We perceive from one thing both a particular matter and a universal matter. The particular is perceived by sense, and the abstract universal is perceived by intellect. Al-Ghazali said: The universal meaning that you have posited as inhering in the intellect is not conceded; rather, only that which does not inhere in sense inheres in the intellect.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a series of five refutations by Ghazali of philosophical arguments for the immateriality of the intellect. Each proof is presented concisely and then answered. The sixth proof argues that if intellect used a physical instrument, it couldn't perceive that instrument, like other senses; but it does perceive heart/brain, so it is not physical. Ghazali dismisses it by reference to his earlier answer to the fifth proof. The seventh proof notes that physical faculties tire from exertion, while intellect does not. Ghazali counters that physical senses may differ in this respect. The eighth proof observes that body weakens with age while intellect strengthens; Ghazali replies that various powers have different growth patterns. The ninth proof questions how the self could be the body when the body constantly changes; Ghazali compares to animals and trees which also grow. The tenth proof distinguishes perception of particular vs. universal; Ghazali denies that universals exist in the intellect as claimed, asserting that only what is not in sense can be in intellect.

Key Terms
آلة جسمانية
العقل
الإبصار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's response to the sixth proof?
A) He refutes it in detail.
B) He says the answer is like the answer to the fifth proof.
C) He concedes the argument.
D) He ignores it.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the sixth proof by referring to his earlier answer, demonstrating his method of reusing arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a new challenge in your faith, do you impatiently seek a brand-new answer or humbly recall a lesson you already learned? Ghazali's concise reference to an earlier proof shows mastery lies in recognizing when an old answer suffices. Where in your life have you neglected a previous lesson, searching for novelty instead of applying what you already know? Commit to revisiting your foundations before pursuing complexity.
?
The philosophers argued that intellect perceives its own instrument, and Ghazali dismissed this by linking to an earlier refutation. How often does your pride demand an original response when a simple reminder of a known truth would serve? In what area of your spiritual journey are you overcomplicating matters, refusing to accept the straightforward teachings you've already received? Resolve to embrace simplicity where appropriate.
?
Ghazali's refutation is so brief it could seem dismissive. Does this challenge your need for elaborate proofs? Are you willing to trust that a sound principle, once established, can answer many questions without repetitive detail? What truth in your life are you currently overthinking, avoiding the simple obedience or acceptance you know is required? Take a moment to commit to acting on what you already know to be true.

Refutation of the Seventh Proof for Intellect's Immateriality
Ghazali refutes the seventh proof by noting possible variation among physical senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the argument that intellect cannot be physical because it does not fatigue.

Core Idea
Ghazali counters by noting that physical senses can differ in their fatigue properties.

The philosophers argue that physical faculties tire from continuous use, while intellect does not, implying it is non-physical. Ghazali simply points out that it is not far-fetched for different physical senses to have different characteristics.

Guided Path
1
Read the seventh proof
Read the philosophers' claim that physical powers tire but intellect does not.
Note the universal claim: 'all physical powers' vs. intellect.
2
Identify the supposed implication
The implication is that intellect is not a physical power because it lacks fatigue.
3
Analyze Ghazali's response
Ghazali says it is not unlikely that different physical senses differ in this matter. He challenges the universal premise.
This is a logical refutation: the proof assumes all physical senses fatigue, but Ghazali denies that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل السادس : قالوا : لو كان العقل يدرك بآلة جسمانية . كالإبصار ، لما أدرك آلته ، كسائر الحواس ، ولكنه يدرك القلب والدماغ ، وما يدعى أنه آلته ، فدل أنه لا يدرك بآلة جسمانية.
قال الغزالى : والجواب عنه كالجواب عن الدليل الخامس

الدليل السابع : القوى المدركة بالآلات الجسمانية يعرض لها من المواظبة على العمل بإدامة الإدراك كلال ، والعقل على العكس من هذا
قال الغزالي : نقول : لا يبعد أن تختلف الحواس الجسمانية في هذه الأمور .

الدليل الثامن : أجزاء البدن كلها تضعف عند الأربعين فما بعدها .... والقوة العقلية فى أكثر الأمور إنما تقوى بعد ذلك
قال الغزالي : والاعتراض أن نقول : نقصان القوى وزيادتها لها أسباب كثيرة ، فقد يقوى بعض القوى في ابتداء العمر ، وبعضها في الوسط ، وبعضها في آخره

Refutation of the Eighth Proof for Intellect's Immateriality
Ghazali refutes the eighth proof by pointing to varying developmental patterns of powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the argument that intellect strengthens with age while body weakens.

Core Idea
Ghazali argues that different powers have different growth trajectories.

The philosophers note that the body weakens after forty, but intellectual power often strengthens after that, suggesting they are separate. Ghazali counters by pointing out that the increase and decrease of powers can vary; some powers peak early, others later.

Guided Path
1
Read the eighth proof
Read the philosophers' observation: body weakens after forty, intellect strengthens.
Notice the implied contrast.
2
Understand the underlying assumption
They assume that if intellect were bodily, it would also weaken with age.
3
Examine Ghazali's objection
Ghazali says increases and decreases have many causes; some powers strengthen early, some middle, some late.
This shows that the philosophers' assumption is not necessary.

Refutation of the Ninth Proof for Intellect's Immateriality
Ghazali refutes the ninth proof by analogizing to animals and trees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the argument that the self cannot be the body because the body constantly changes.

Core Idea
Ghazali points to animals and trees as counterexamples that persist despite material change.

The philosophers argue that since the body continuously dissolves and is replaced by food, the self cannot be identical with the body, which is always changing. Ghazali refutes by noting that animals and trees also undergo continuous material change yet are considered the same individual.

Guided Path
1
Read the ninth proof
Read the philosophers' argument about the body's constant dissolution and replacement.
They question how a changing body can be the same self.
2
Identify the philosophers' conclusion
They conclude that the self must be something other than the body.
3
See Ghazali's counterexample
Ghazali points to animals and trees: they change materially from small to large but are still considered the same individual.
This shows that material change does not preclude identity.

Refutation of the Tenth Proof for Intellect's Immateriality
Ghazali refutes the tenth proof by denying the philosophers' conception of universals in the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the argument that universals must be perceived by a non-physical intellect.

Core Idea
Ghazali denies the philosophers' claim about universals existing in the intellect as they propose.

The philosophers argue that since we perceive both particulars and universals from one thing, and particulars are perceived by sense, universals must be perceived by a separate non-physical intellect. Ghazali rejects the notion that universals inhere in the intellect in the way claimed.

Guided Path
1
Read the tenth proof
Read the philosophers' distinction between perception of particulars and universals.
They assign particulars to sense and universals to intellect.
2
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is that the intellect must be non-physical because it perceives abstract universals.
3
Analyze Ghazali's response
Ghazali says the universal meaning they posit in the intellect is not conceded. He claims only what does not inhere in sense can inhere in intellect.
This directly challenges the premise that universals are in the intellect as separate entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل السادس : قالوا : لو كان العقل يدرك بآلة جسمانية . كالإبصار ، لما أدرك آلته ، كسائر الحواس ، ولكنه يدرك القلب والدماغ ، وما يدعى أنه آلته ، فدل أنه لا يدرك بآلة جسمانية.
قال الغزالى : والجواب عنه كالجواب عن الدليل الخامس

الدليل السابع : القوى المدركة بالآلات الجسمانية يعرض لها من المواظبة على العمل بإدامة الإدراك كلال ، والعقل على العكس من هذا
قال الغزالي : نقول : لا يبعد أن تختلف الحواس الجسمانية في هذه الأمور .

الدليل الثامن : أجزاء البدن كلها تضعف عند الأربعين فما بعدها .... والقوة العقلية فى أكثر الأمور إنما تقوى بعد ذلك
قال الغزالي : والاعتراض أن نقول : نقصان القوى وزيادتها لها أسباب كثيرة ، فقد يقوى بعض القوى في ابتداء العمر ، وبعضها في الوسط ، وبعضها في آخره

الدليل التاسع : كيف يكون الإنسان هو الجسم مع عوارضه ، وهذه الأجسام لا تزال تنحل ، والغذاء يسد مسد ما ينحل
قال الغزالي : هذا ينتقض بالبهيمة والشجرة ، إذا قيست حال كبرهما بحال الصغر

الدليل العاشر : أنا ندرك من الشيء الواحد أمراً جزئيا، وأمراً كلياً . والجزئى يدرك بالحس ، والكلى المجرد يدرك بالعقل.
قال الغزالى : إن المعنى الكلى الذى وضعتموه حالا في العقل ، غير مسلم ، بل لا يحل في العقل إلا ما لا يحل في الحس
The sixth proof: They said: If the intellect perceived by a physical instrument like sight, it would not perceive its instrument, like the other senses, but it perceives the heart and brain and what is claimed to be its instrument, so it indicates that it does not perceive by a physical instrument. Al-Ghazali said: The answer to it is like the answer to the fifth proof. The seventh proof: The powers that perceive by physical instruments are subject to fatigue from persevering in action by continuous perception, while the intellect is opposite to this. Al-Ghazali said: We say: It is not unlikely that the physical senses differ in these matters. The eighth proof: All parts of the body weaken at forty years and after, while the intellectual power in most matters only strengthens after that. Al-Ghazali said: The objection is that we say: The decrease and increase of powers have many causes; some powers may strengthen at the beginning of life, some in the middle, and some at its end. The ninth proof: How can a human be the body with its accidents, while these bodies continually dissolve and food replaces what dissolves? Al-Ghazali said: This is refuted by the animal and tree, when comparing their state at bigness to their state at smallness. The tenth proof: We perceive from one thing both a particular matter and a universal matter. The particular is perceived by sense, and the abstract universal is perceived by intellect. Al-Ghazali said: The universal meaning that you have posited as inhering in the intellect is not conceded; rather, only that which does not inhere in sense inheres in the intellect.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a series of five refutations by Ghazali of philosophical arguments for the immateriality of the intellect. Each proof is presented concisely and then answered. The sixth proof argues that if intellect used a physical instrument, it couldn't perceive that instrument, like other senses; but it does perceive heart/brain, so it is not physical. Ghazali dismisses it by reference to his earlier answer to the fifth proof. The seventh proof notes that physical faculties tire from exertion, while intellect does not. Ghazali counters that physical senses may differ in this respect. The eighth proof observes that body weakens with age while intellect strengthens; Ghazali replies that various powers have different growth patterns. The ninth proof questions how the self could be the body when the body constantly changes; Ghazali compares to animals and trees which also grow. The tenth proof distinguishes perception of particular vs. universal; Ghazali denies that universals exist in the intellect as claimed, asserting that only what is not in sense can be in intellect.

Key Terms
جزئي
كلي
الحس
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the tenth proof, what does Ghazali say about the universal meaning the philosophers posit in the intellect?
A) It is conceded but needs further proof.
B) It is not conceded, and only what does not inhere in sense inheres in the intellect.
C) It is the same as the particular perceived by sense.
D) It is abstract but still depends on a physical instrument.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the tenth proof by rejecting the philosophers' account of universals in the intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an argument that seems logically sound, do you ever accept its premises without scrutinizing them? How does Ghazali's refusal to grant the philosophers' premise challenge you to examine the foundations of your own beliefs?
?
Is there a 'universal truth' you hold that you have never critically examined? What would it mean for you to apply Ghazali's skeptical approach to that belief?
?
If your intellect can be mistaken about something as basic as how it perceives universals, what does that say about your confidence in other intellectual convictions? Are you willing to re-evaluate them?
